HEADLINE: MUTUAL FUNDS;
FUNDS WATCH

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:

Shooting Stars In Minnesota
Bitter winters produce some sweet returns. Minnesota has given rise to three mutual funds that invest heavily in the state and that have outpaced the stock market recently, said Matthew S. Muehlbauer, who edits the Value Line Fund Highlight, which showcases above-average funds in the Value Line Mutual Fund Survey. 
 The three are Mairs and Power Growth, which has outperformed the market every calendar year since 1989; First American Regional Equity, which has done so for the last three years, and Perkins Opportunity, which has done so for two years. Each of the funds has an annualized 36-month return exceeding 20 percent, compared with 15.3 percent for the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index.
The funds all have their headquarters in the Twin Cities area, and the first two have at least 80 percent of their assets invested in Minnesota companies. The third, Perkins, has about half its portfolio invested in local businesses.
Perhaps all this is just a coincidence. But Minnesota is home to several big companies, like 3M, that produce a steady stream of seasoned employees who eventually set up their own companies. The state also has a large, established venture-capital network to support fledgling companies and business groups that support entrepreneurs, Mr. Muehlbauer said.
Investors should note that the small stocks owned by First American and Perkins can be more volatile than bigger stocks, as can the midsize stocks owned by Mairs. And Minnesota's economy has been strong lately, contributing to the funds' performance. Mr. Muehlbauer cautioned that the funds were best suited for people with long time horizons for their investments, and who weren't concerned by volatility.
 
Ordering On Line
Mutual fund activity on the Internet is hopping. Fund companies are scrambling to set up home pages on the World Wide Web with information about their funds and investing in general. But the movement to let investors buy and sell securities on line is still in its infancy.
Jack White & Company says it is the first brokerage firm to process mutual fund orders through its Internet connection on the PAWWS Financial Network (http://pawws.com/jwc). Investors can trade 4,400 hundreds of funds in the company's mutual fund network, including more than 800 in a no-transaction-fee program.
Look for Charles Schwab & Company to add a similar service in May.
 
In Brief
Dreyfus Retirement Services has created a 401(k) program for companies with 10 to 150 employees. For information about the program, which allows employees to invest their retirement dollars in Dreyfus funds, call (800) 557-4015.
Fidelity Investments is offering a new service that allows customers of its Spartan brokerage unit to trade stocks outside the United States.

HEADLINE: VIEWPOINT;
A Handshake Unwise for Europe

BYLINE: By BARRY EICHENGREEN; Barry Eichengreen is the John L. Simpson Professor of Economics and Political Science at the University of California, Berkeley.

 BODY:
NOTWITHSTANDING negative forecasts, European monetary unification is likely to happen on schedule on Jan. 1, 1999. But while there are clear potential economic benefits, unification is being carried out in a way that is likely to produce unfortunate effects for Europe and its trading partners, including the United States.
Already, the move toward unification has slowed growth and increased unemployment in Europe. Now, France and Germany have agreed on a structure that will impede Europe's ability to respond effectively to economic downturns. 
 European monetary unification will happen because France and Germany want it. A recent poll found that the French favor a single currency. While the Germans may have reservations, Chancellor Helmut Kohl has vowed to shepherd his program, of which EMU is a central element, through parliament.
The core French-German bargain is monetary union for political union. Germany wants political union in order to acquire a foreign policy role in a united Europe. To obtain it, Bonn must offer Paris monetary union, which France wants in order to regain some control of Europe's financial conditions from Germany.
The rationale for European integration has always been to lock Germany into Europe, economically and politically. Institutionalized restraint appeals to the French, who fear German might, and to the Germans, who for historical reasons are unwilling to use political and military power unilaterally. Mr. Kohl recently appealed to this notion, insisting that monetary union was necessary to free Europe from war in the 21st century.
But in neither France nor Germany is EMU without problems.
Germany fears that its EMU partners will run excessive deficits and incur excessive debts. To reduce debt-service costs, Germans worry, the partners will pressure the European Central Bank to keep interest rates artificially low, resulting in an inflationary monetary policy.
Bonn is therefore insisting on a "stability council" to monitor the budgetary policies of participating countries. Members that don't keep deficits within 1 percent of gross domestic product and otherwise obey the dictates of the council would be subject to political and economic sanctions. While the Maastricht Treaty contains an excessive-deficit procedure and a budget deficit guideline of 3 percent of G.D.P., Germany wants a tougher test.
France, for its part, fears competitive currency depreciation and the dumping of goods by European Union states like Italy and Britain, which will not be founding members of the monetary union. Those countries' departure from the Exchange Rate Mechanism of the European Monetary System in 1992 and subsequent export-led recoveries -- partly at the expense of France -- resonates with a long-standing French aversion to currency fluctuations. (The new Italian Government will try to bring the lira back into the Exchange Rate Mechanism. But because of the country's very serious public debt problem, Italy will not be a founding member of EMU.)
Paris has demanded that member states outside the monetary union participate in a new European Monetary System to be built around a single currency.
At the meeting of French and German finance ministers and central bankers in Laval, France, last month, the French agreed to back the idea of a stability council in return for German support for a system of exchange-rate bands for countries that do not participate in the monetary union. This pact was effectively confirmed at a meeting in Verona, Italy, of all European Union finance ministers and central bankers two Sundays ago. Now there is virtually no doubt that monetary union will happen. But it also suggests it will be on undesirable terms.
Germany's stability pact is undesirable because it will hamstring stabilization policy. Monetary union will by definition remove exchange rates and interest rates as instruments for absorbing shocks that affect one European country more strongly than another.
In principle, fiscal policy will remain; thus, if France succumbs to recession -- but Germany does not -- France's automatic fiscal stabilizers can come into play. French taxes will fall, sustaining demand until the recession is over. But a strict 1 percent cap on budget deficits, plus the fact that governments will be up against this limit when monetary union begins, will effectively eliminate all fiscal flexibility.
There may be good reasons to tie Europe's monetary hands, but to remove its fiscal defenses as well would leave its economies naked in the face of business cycles.
France's idea of exchange-rate bands for the outsiders is equally appalling. If there is one lesson to be gleaned from the history of the European Monetary System in the early 1990's, it is that exchange-rate bands are exceedingly difficult to defend in today's highly liquid markets.
Once the strong-currency countries join a monetary union, only the weak will remain outside, rendering them sitting ducks for speculators. The newly created European Central Bank will be intent on establishing the credibility of its commitment to price stability. It will hardly be prepared to lend extensive exchange-rate support to outside currencies.
Monetary union is probably desirable insofar as it will advance the cause of European integration. But if it comes packaged with a stability council and exchange-rate bands, the economic price may be too high. To keep these issues off-limits at the European Union Intergovernmental Conference that began last month in Italy would be shortsighted in the worst way.

HEADLINE: MUTUAL FUNDS;
For the Brave, an Open-End Russia Fund

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY MIDDLETON 

 BODY:
ABOUT the time that the people of Russia elect a president in June, Americans are expected to get their first chance to buy an open-end mutual fund invested there. Neither is without substantial risk.
The American company sponsoring the fund, the Lexington Management Corporation of Saddle Brook,N.J., has devoted nearly a third of the draft prospectus to outlining the investors' risks -- from political uncertainty and rampant commercial crime to punitive taxes and a dubious legal and accounting system.
"There are unusual risks, but the exciting part of the story in Russia is that valuations on these companies are very, very low relative to other countries," said Richard Hisey, a managing director and chief financial officer at Lexington.
Low valuations aside, the Lexington fund will encounter some big market obstacles, very thin trading and delays in settlement, to name just a couple. Given how sketchy corporate financial information is there, investors might also think long and hard about how reliable those low valuations really are.
Jim Rogers, known for his international investing in years past and author of "Investment Biker," says most financial data about Russian companies are invented. Any profits, he adds, could easily be stolen by insiders, taxed away by the Government or consumed by raging inflation, which has abated from the 2,600 percent rate of 1992 but is still running about 70 percent.
"Anybody who thinks Russia is attractive doesn't understand history," Mr. Rogers said in a recent interview. "When empires like this dissolve, there is chaos for years."
Mr. Rogers is not the only one with apparent reservations. J. Mark Mobius, who travels the world as a Templeton fund manager and who manages a closed-end fund that invests in Russia, recently acknowledged that he did not have any of his own money in that fund. Americans can buy shares in Templeton Russia, begun last summer, on the New York Stock Exchange, where they now trade at a price about 20 percent higher than the underlying value of the fund's assets.
Perhaps the best summary of the Russian situation came from David Tripple, chief investment officer of the Pioneer Group in Boston. "I would define the Russian equity markets as pre-emergent," he said, drawing a distinction from the emerging markets, commonly considered the most volatile of investments.
Five years after the collapse of the Soviet Union, the Russian economy is struggling. The nation is desperately poor, with gross domestic product in 1994 estimated at $300 billion, or les than one-twentieth of the United States figure. One member of the Parliament, Pavel G. Bunich, estimated that two-thirds of industrial plant and equipment had been broken up and sold or had decayed beyond repair in this decade. He said the richest 20 percent of Russians received half the nation's total income while the poorest 20 percent got 3.5 percent. Hundreds of bankers and plant managers have been murdered, and the currency has fallen from official parity with the United States dollar under Communism to 4,918 to the dollar in recent trading.
The Russian equities market, with a total capitalization estimated at $20 billion (about the size of Sears, Roebuck), itself is fraught with peril. Shares of about 900 companies are publicly listed, but only about 60 trade at least once a month, and fewer than 30 on a weekly basis, according to Arnhold & S. Bleichroeder Inc., an investment bank in New York. Although there are stock exchanges, shares trade over the counter in practice, and just establishing their value is difficult. Share registries have been known to disappear, and with them the sole proof of investors' ownership.
Not everyone, of course, is completely sour on the market. George Soros, the hedge fund manager, and George Russell, chairman of the pension consulting and money management firm of Frank Russell Company, have expressed cautious support for Russian prospects.
Part of the appeal is that Russian assets are cheap by international standards. Mr. Hisey said one of the Lexington fund's potential investments, Lukoil, has proven reserves of nearly 16 billion barrels of oil, more than twice those of Exxon, but a market capitalization of $4 billion, compared with $105 billion for Exxon.
A recent research report by Bleichroeder says the price of Russian telephone stocks works out to less than $100 a line, compared with at least $2,000 in Latin America and $4,500 in Asia. Nevertheless, the report urges investors to avoid Russia. "Little harm will befall those who choose to remain on the sidelines and place investments in more secure places," it concluded.
Among the problems for investors is that financial disclosure is haphazard, and sometimes entirely absent. Nicholas Bratt, director of the global equity group of Scudder, Stevens & Clark Inc., noted that a prospectus for a private placement of stock in Gazprom, Russia's largest gas company, carried this disclosure: "Neither the company nor Morgan Grenfell, the British merchant bank, nor the U.S. placement agent, has 1.) obtained or secured any information, financial or otherwise, which is deemed reliable; 2.) asked any questions of officers, directors, managers or employees of Gazprom, or 3.) conducted any other due diligence with respect to Gazprom." Not exactly a ringing endorsement.
Nor is it an isolated example. Lukoil, cited by Lexington as a potential investment, noted in its most recent annual report, for 1994, that its auditor, KPMG, had reviewed only that information submitted by the company, and that it did not expect to meet international standards of accounting until 1996. "Neither in its objective, scope nor procedures used can the work that we have carried be considered a full audit of authenticity of the above consolidated accounting standards," the auditors' statement concludes.
Mr. Hisey said Lexington was well aware of these problems, and with its Moscow-based subadviser, Troika Dialog Asset Management, has taken numerous safeguarding steps. He said the fund would invest largely in the biggest companies with the most liquid stocks like Lukoil; Mosenergo, the Moscow power company; Rostelecom, the national long-distance phone company, and Red October, a candy manufacturer. It will also limit itself to companies that have truly independent outside registrars for stock records.
"We have what we believe to be a very conservative set of procedures to protect our interests and the interests of our shareholders there," Mr. Hisey said.
Another potential problem, perhaps even harder to skirt, is having shares diluted if a company decides to issue new shares to insiders, politicians or the Russian people. "The biggest risk is not a company stealing or erasing your ownership interest, but diluting it," Mr. Hisey said.
Similar concerns, and a fear that Americans will not fully understand them, have caused the Pioneer Group to rule out the possibility of a Russia fund for the mass market in the near future. Instead, Pioneer expects to offer a mutual fund to Russian investors in June through a joint venture and to add two funds later this year for American institutions.
Single-country funds for retail investors are terribly volatile and risky even when they focus on more established markets, Mr. Tripple said. Based on Pioneer's experience managing funds for Polish investors, he said, "it's tough to match U.S. versions of liquidity in foreign operations." Along with the difficulty sometimes of finding a willing buyer or seller for Russian securities, the actual settlement can take far longer than the three to five days we are accustomed to here, he said.
So far, Russian profits have proved mostly elusive for American investors. While still very young, the Templeton Russia fund experienced a 13.9 percent decline in its net asset value in the second half of last year and a 6.62 percent decline in the first quarter of this year. As a closed-end fund, Templeton Russia has something working in its favor that the Lexington fund won't. Should there be a sharp market decline, it doesn't have to worry about raising cash to meet shareholder redemptions at a time when Russian equities might be even harder to trade than usual or when selling might depress prices further. Its shareholders can simply sell their stake on the Big Board. For this reason, closed-end funds are the most common vehicle for single-country investing.
"I can't imagine anyone running an open-end mutual fund to invest in Russia," said Thomas J. Herzfeld, an investor in Miami who specializes in closed-end funds. The prospect of needing to sell assets in a down market is daunting, he said. "How do you sell securities in an illiquid market?"
Mr. Hisey said the Lexington fund was intended for long-term investors, and he did not expect a problem with redemptions or a resulting cash squeeze. The fund intends to discourage redemptions by imposing a 2 percent fee on shares held less than one year. While the fund will not carry an upfront sales charge, expenses are expected to run a hefty 3.35 percent of assets a year. The minimum investment will be $5,000. The suggested stomach: strong.

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Will Japan's Success Soon Shine on Its U.S. Investors?

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

 BODY:
IT'S bad enough when a seemingly smart investment idea goes wrong and costs you money. But it's an even greater frustration when being right doesn't pay off.
Take, for example, Japan. 
 Investors anticipating the end of Japan's five-year recession have driven up the Nikkei 225, the blue-chip stock index, more than 30 percent the last year. The performance matches that of the Dow Jones industrial average and surpasses that of the leading stock indexes of Britain and Germany.
But what does the American who bought mutual funds that invest in Japan have to show for making their sagacious bet on the Nikkei, which has soared 56 percent from its early-July low? A relatively paltry gain of about 9 percent in the last 12 months, according to a tabulation of Japanese funds by Lipper Analytical Services.
"From the perspective of the United States investor, it has not felt like a recovery in Japan," said George A. Murnaghan, executive vice president of Rowe Price-Fleming International in Baltimore, sponsor of the $220 million T. Rowe Price Japan Fund.
The root cause of this underperformance is the decline in the value of the Japanese yen against the dollar.
In the first three months of 1996, for example, T. Rowe Price Japan posted a 7 percent profit in Japan -- only to wind up with a net gain of just 3 percent after the currency translation.
But some money managers think that the yen's relationship to the dollar is likely to stabilize -- and that American investors may finally get their long-awaited reward.
One is Ron Chapman, head of international equities for the Dreyfus Corporation, the New York sponsor of Dreyfus Global Growth and other funds with sizable foreign stakes. While Mr. Chapman could hedge his Japanese investments to neutralize part of the currency risk, he is not doing so.
Only when the dollar has a "significant" chance of rising by more than 10 percent over a 12- to 18-month period would he hedge, Mr. Chapman said. And by his calculation, if the yen falls at all, it will be by no more than about 3 percent.
Even if the currencies cooperate, will the Japanese stock market continue upward after so much gain? "I can't be as bullish at 22,000 as at 14,000 last summer," said Mr. Chapman, referring to the Nikkei 225 index, which closed at 22,230.1 this week. But Japan, he said, is the only major economy that is in the early stages of business expansion and in which corporate earnings, far below historic highs, could improve significantly.
Japanese investors, Mr. Chapman said, are gradually recovering from the shock of the Nikkei's 63 percent dive from its December 1989 peak to its July 1995 low. That means all the money stuffed into mattresses since then -- "an enormous amount of liquidity," he said -- will slowly become available to power further stock market advances.
"This market has maybe 15 percent upside" potential during 1996, Mr. Chapman estimated, backing his view with a greater weighting of Japanese stocks -- 32 percent against 22 percent -- than the average of his competitors.
Like Mr. Chapman, Mr. Murnaghan of Rowe Price-Fleming does not see any need to hedge against a weakening yen. While the Japanese currency may fall a bit, he said, there will not be much movement.
But opinions differ among money managers. Paul Kirkby, investment manager of the GAM Japan Capital Fund, the top-ranked fund last year among those invested entirely in Japan, said he is "not as bullish" on Japan these days as most of his peers.
There is "clearly a currency risk" in Japanese stocks, said Mr. Kirkby, who has hedged 75 percent to 80 percent of his $37.7 million fund against that danger. He also thinks other investors may be fooled by the Nikkei's past peaks. "If the market's previous peak in December 1989 had been 25,000 rather than 39,000, it is unlikely Japan would be considered a 'bargain' today," he said recently in an internal memorandum.
And he agrees with many analysts who do not expect Japan to grow more than 2 percent this year, less than half of what is typical for an economy emerging from recession. One large source of drag is that many companies, driven by years of yen appreciation that has made exporting less profitable, have been shifting production to lower-cost countries in the Far East, a development referred to as the "hollowing out" of Japanese industry.
Still, Mr. Kirkby has identified dozens of stocks, including insurance, leasing and other nonbank financial companies, that he believes represent good value. He said he also may buy various high-tech issues, some benefiting from deregulation, but would prefer to do so on price dips.

HEADLINE: IN BRIEF;
Downtown Renewal Includes Pieces of History

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
Englewood's historic Monument section is about to undergo a face lift.
Last week, civic leaders and city officials inaugurated a $150,000 fund-raising drive to renovate of the Monument intersection, site of the historic Liberty Pole and a World War I memorial. If successful, the drive, called the Liberty Square Project, will help pay for new historical markers, a memorial walk and wall, landscaping, better lighting and other renovations. Organizers say some work could start this year. 
 The first Liberty Pole was erected in 1766 by colonists celebrating the repeal of the British Stamp Act. Since then, there have been poles of various materials. The current one, installed in 1964, is a 60-foot metal pole topped by a gold liberty cap.
The war memorial, which dates to 1924, features a statue of a soldier in uniform. But the area has fallen into disuse in recent years, obscured by trees and ignored by drivers circling Palisade Avenue and Tenafly Road.
The fund drive is part of a broad plan to revitalize downtown Englewood. Organizers have raised $16,000 from private sources and $3,500 in public money. City officials have also pledged $20,000.
"We just have to remind people of the status of the town, and try to identify the city as a beautiful and historic place," said Wesley Marshall, a trustee of the Liberty Square Association, a nonprofit group spearheading the campaign. DAVID W. CHEN

HEADLINE: DIARY

BYLINE: By HUBERT B. HERRING 

 BODY:

GETTING AND SPENDING
 
Oh. Right. The Budget.
 
It brings a cartoon to mind: the handyman suggests to his fraying employers that since it's September and he hasn't taken the storm windows off yet, maybe they should just forget about it for this year. Which brings us, of course, to the Federal budget. You'd forgotten about it, right? You probably figured that so much time had passed that those squabblers in Washington would just let it slide and try again next year. But wonder of wonders, with the fiscal year more than half over, Bill Clinton and Bob Dole pulled a deal out of their hats last week. So for five months, at least, museums will stay open, the Government will take no more sick days. But it was a bit like the animal patrol rounding up the kittens and leaving the elephants. For while Democrats salvaged some job and education programs and Republicans got some spending cuts, the compromise tiptoed around the big-money issues, like Social Security, Medicare, Medicaid and welfare. Next time.
 
 
STATISTICS
 
What the Downsized Folk Do
 
It's a cacophony of revisionism. Everyone's pronouncing all the downsizing anxiety a bad dream. First a manufacturers group said wage gains had been obscured by exaggerated inflation numbers. Then last week the Council of Economic Advisers put out a study aimed at exploding the notion that yesterday's white-collar paper-pusher is now holed up at the local McDonald's counting pickles. No, the study insists, the new jobs are good jobs, at mutual funds, hospitals, software companies. The head of the council admitted that "the anxieties are real," but insisted they are overblown. It is a coincidence, isn't it, that this rose-colored economics is blossoming in an election year?
 
UTILITIES UNBOUND
 
Get Your Kilowatts Here!
 
Just to clarify one point: When regulators told 166 large utilities last week to open their transmission systems to competition, that didn't mean you'll suddenly have 166 power lines to your house. You won't hear: "Honey, turn on the Duke Power light. Their rates are lowest today." Marketers won't call, as your dinner's first bite is in midair, to urge you to plug your television into the Southern California Edison outlet. No, this is wholesale stuff. Your local utility still has you all to itself. Regulators put the annual savings to industry and consumers at $4 billion or $5 billion. In the high-cost Northeast, utilities can buy cheap power elsewhere and, presumably, pass the savings along to consumers. Presumably.
 
 
TOBACCO STOCKS
 
Mutual Fund Country
 
Many investors balk at tobacco stocks, partly for the risk from all those lawsuits, partly as a matter of principle. Yet those same investors may, knowingly or not, own mutual funds heavy on tobacco. A consumer group, Co-op America, reports that 6 of the 10 biggest mutual funds list tobacco stocks in their top 10 holdings, a total investment in the billions. Now the American Medical Association wants investors to rethink where their savings go, last week urging "people interested in the health and welfare of our children" to sell tobacco shares and funds that buy them.
 
PHONE MERGERS
 
Mr. Dumpty? It's for You!
 
It was the darkest of technological times, those days before the AT&T breakup. No pocket phones. A few scattered modems. Phone bills that private citizens could decipher. But that phone industry, quaint as it was, was owned by AT&T, so it had to be dismantled. The rules have changed, though, and new giants are being born. Bell Atlantic and Nynex, which linked up last week, will dominate the Northeast and be the nation's No. 2, behind a humbler AT&T. What of phone customers? Well, they're being told things will be simpler; one company to serve many needs, less troublesome competition. Which sounds, of course, like the old AT&T. Speaking of layoffs, though, the companies say they'll cut just 3,000 jobs from a combined work force up 133,000, a far cry from the tens of thousands in other megamergers.
 
THE PRICE OF PAIN
 
Crime's Bottom Line?
 
Can an explosive issue like crime really be reduced to a clear, crisp item on a balance sheet? A report for the Justice Department tries to do just that, putting a price tag on everything from legal fees to mental anguish to come up with a startling estimate: crime costs Americans $450 billion a year. That doesn't even count the $40 billion spent locking people up. Does it follow, then, that it makes cold economic sense to jail everyone you can? If America were a corporation, fixated on next quarter's results, then the manic prison-building would be justified. "The public and politicians demand immediate gratification," says one criminologist, "so there is considerable push for being tough." Thus, he added, "we have largely abandoned prevention and rehabilitation efforts." Another study suggests that saving a high-risk juvenile from a life of crime could net the country at least $1.5 million. Or, as the pros say, invest and hold.
 
WORKING GIRLS
 
Was It Cool? Affirmative
 
Affirmative action. Those are heavy words, political words, adult words, not words you'd find in the mouths of 11-year-olds. Ask most Daughters who were Taken to Work about their Day, and they'll probably say it was neat or cool or a drag or awesome. And their brothers might say, "Yeah, my yucky sister got to skip school, and the brat got more ice cream last night, too." Discrimination? Some companies thought so, making it Take Our Children to Work Day. But that, says the Ms. Foundation, the event's organizer, misses the whole point, to repair some of society's damage to girls' self-esteem. A Ms. spokeswoman, referring to one company's comment that it would never "exclude by gender," pointed to all the males running that company and said, "Executives with glass ceilings shouldn't throw stones."
 
 
AUTO SAFETY
 
A Record Ford Recall
 
An internal company memo from February 1995 shows that Ford was aware of a "very small potential for ignition switch fires on certain vehicles" and warned of possible "adverse publicity." No, the danger did not prompt Ford to recall the vehicles then, but the memo was right on target about that publicity. The company got complaints of at least 1,100 fires and some injuries, Canada ordered a recall in November, and lawyers got into the act with a flurry of class-action suits. No self-respecting topic these days would be without an Internet page, and sure enough, the Association of Flaming Ford Owners was born. Last week it all got too much for Ford, which recalled 8.7 million cars and trucks, the largest recall ever by a single car maker.

GRAPHIC: Photos: "JACKIE'S ATTIC: Oh, What Does It All Mean?"
It's a quandary for anyone whose parents have died: what stuff to keep, what to sell, what to toss. Many a ragged item hovers awkwardly between memory-laden prize and, well, junk. Now suppose, for a moment, that Caroline Kennedy Schlossberg suddenly regretted not holding onto the ratty old satin-covered footstool that, in a long-ago White House, helped her up to the window seat. Sorry. Too late. No, it didn't go out with the trash, it sold at Sotheby's last week for $33,350. (That's right, enough for a house down payment or a modest luxury car.) For a few brief shining days, auction mania ("There's only one of these," the auctioneer bubbled. "This is about history.") transformed one family's attic into a quite healthy-sized business, a $4.47 million take the first night alone. What do you suppose, say, Dan Quayle's old easy chair would fetch?

HEADLINE: Harbor Is a Natural Asset, Not Just an Economic One

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In his letter on the crisis posed by the buildup of silt in New York Harbor (April 14), J. William Van Dyke, chairman of the North Jersey Transportation Planning Authority, soon lapses into a biased diatribe, obfuscating the complexity of the problems he seeks to resolve. 
 Complex social issues like this can be constructively addressed only when there is a balanced understanding based on all perspectives. To illustrate, let me quote the last paragraph of Mr. Van Dyke's letter, then follow it with a reworded version of my own.
VERSION 1 The Port of New York and New Jersey stands poised to become far and away the most important port on the Atlantic Coast. But unless a realistic, cost-effective solution to the dredging crisis is found soon, the ebb and flow of nature will dictate economic decisions that will forever remake the quality of life and prosperity of the region -- for the worse.
VERSION 2 The Port of New York and New Jersey has become far and away one of the most problematic contaminated ports on the Atlantic Coast. The inability to identify a realistic, environmentally acceptable solution to the dredging crisis indicates that environmental and economic limits have been exceeded. Unless such a solution can be identified, these limits should be recognized and incorporated into decisions about the harbor. That will remake the quality of life in the region -- for the better.
 
GEORGE L. WETZEL
Hopewell

HEADLINE: The Soul of a School Board Race

BYLINE: By MICHAEL COOPER 

 BODY:
IT was time to talk about contingency plans. The race for School Board 3 was just beginning, and a group of candidates were huddling around a dining room table in an Upper West Side apartment and, between crackerfuls of blue cheese and cups of seltzer, discussing what to do if things turned ugly.
"The attack you're going to get is on P.S. 9 -- that your kids are all in gifted programs," Deborah Pollock, a member of the outgoing school board, warned the neophytes. "You're going to be called the Gifted and Talented slate." 
 The four candidates -- two white and two Hispanic -- looked at each other nervously. It was true: three had children in programs for the gifted at Public School 9 on West 84th Street, the academic pride of the district. On the playing fields of school politics, "gifted" is a loaded word; charges of elitism could be damaging. So the nascent slate needed a vigorous defense. It also needed a name, something catchy, something that would play in the northern end of the district, up in Harlem, as well as the southern end, the Upper West Side.
Ivette Guerra, a candidate, had an idea.
"How about Padres por los Ninos?" she suggested.
"Padres por los Ninos," mused Gerald Gliber, another candidate. "Parents for Children. The other side can't claim that. We are all parents."
The name stuck. It would appeal to Hispanic parents, it did not sound elitist and it aimed a sly barb at the three members of the rival slate with no children. Giggles and thumps sounded from the next room, where children were playing with a Tinkertoy set and paper airplanes.
Meet the warriors of school district politics, a combative but tiny group. Widely ignored by the electorate, still plagued by accusations of corruption and facing extinction by a city- and statewide officialdom now largely convinced of its ineffectiveness, the 26-year-old school board system is hobbling toward its next election.
Only this year it's worse: There are fewer candidates than ever in the 32 districts and some districts do not even have enough candidates to fill their nine-member boards. And if even 10 percent of the voters go to the polls on May 7, it will be a heavy turnout.
So once again the elections will be placed in the hands of only the most highly motivated parents, special interest groups promoting particular agendas, political clubs, unions, gay groups, the Christian Coalition -- anyone who can get out the vote.
In this era of fiscal austerity, to run for school board is to seek the unpleasant task of fighting over crumbs, of deciding what to keep and what to cut. Guidance counselors or after-school programs? An extra teacher or desperately needed supplies? These decisions take a toll: In District 3, seven of the nine board members are stepping down, generally out of frustration in spite of having made crucial decisions like putting a gifted program in a Harlem middle school; postponing the relocation of P.S. 9's gifted program to a school accessible to people in wheelchairs; writing guidelines for teaching its youngest pupils and planning a school for the musically talented.
The triage atmosphere makes school boards the locus of even more vitriolic disputes, the arena where neighborhoods vie with neighborhoods and parents compete with other parents for resources. These tensions, played out in a school board campaign, provide a glimpse of the city's political life at the ground level.
These competing interests are clear in District 3, a rectangular chunk of Manhattan from Columbus Circle to 122d Street. The district is a study in urban contrasts. It represents both the largely white gentry of the Upper West Side and Morningside Heights, the mostly black poor in lower Harlem, where one resident in three lives below the poverty line, and the Spanish-speaking residents of Manhattan Valley.
The contrasts extend to its public schools. At P.S. 9, four of five students read and solve math problems at grade level. A mile and a half to the north, at P.S. 113 on 113th Street, the opposite is true: four of five students are performing below grade level.
Those two schools represent the extremes of a pattern that is repeated throughout the district. The top five schools are all below 98th Street; the bottom five are all above 110th Street. This disparity is emerging as a major issue in the campaign. It is no accident that the eight-member slate running against Padres por los Ninos calls itself Equity and Excellence.
Why this gulf exists, and what can be done about it, are contentious issues. Some point to economic and social factors, noting that poverty and the breakdown of the family put pressures on children in Harlem that their wealthier peers downtown could not even imagine. Others note that downtown parents are more active in their schools and raise money on their own. Parents at P.S. 9, for instance, raised more than $50,000 this year.
Whatever the reason for the disparity, it does not appear to stem from unequal public financing. The struggling schools uptown actually receive more public dollars than the successful schools downtown, district records show. The current board released a school-by-school budget to correct the perception that it favors downtown schools. Their budget shows, for example, that P.S. 113 receives $4,163 per student a year while P.S. 9 gets $3,058. This is largely because schools with poor students are eligible for extra government aid.
"It's not just a money issue," said Ernestine E. Roach, one of two Harlem residents on the current board.
Just what the issue is has become a major topic in the campaign.
 
UPTOWN, DOWNTOWN: THE HARLEM CONTINGENT
Wearing his black satin Harlem Little League jacket and clutching a copy of "Savage Inequalities," Jonathan Kozol's book about the gulf between poor black schools and rich white schools, William Perkins took a seat at a candidates' forum.
His choice of attire and reading material were a reminder of his main concern: What can be done to give children in Harlem the same educational opportunities as those of the Upper West Side?
"This part of the district is under-represented," said Mr. Perkins, a Harlem district leader and a founder of the Sojourner Truth Democratic Club. "The more politically connected, economically influential have been able to reap the better benefits."
He is a big supporter of Yasmin N. Hurston, the lone candidate living in Harlem, which has nine of the district's 21 elementary schools but only two of the current school board's nine members.
Ms. Hurston, 24, has worked for various Harlem politicians. "If you make more noise, you get more results," Ms. Hurston said. "I want to encourage people in Harlem to make more noise."
Ms. Roach said the problem of uptown schools had been stubborn. "It's hard to get good teachers to come uptown," Ms. Roach said. "We don't get many with experience. Many are not state certified."
Teachers with seniority, she noted, get preference when choosing where they want to work and few choose Harlem.
The other Harlem board member, Liz Brock, said she would have liked to see more pressure put on Harlem schools to succeed. She pointed to the example of P.S. 191 on West 61st Street, whose scores were so low that it was put under state review. The board hired a new principal with a mandate to shake things up. Scores doubled.
"That principal worked hard and had gutsy ideas," Ms. Brock said.
But Page Best-Hardy, a black candidate who lives in an Upper West Side housing project and sends her daughter to Wadleigh Secondary School on 114th Street, said of the disparities: "I believe it has a lot to do with race. A whole lot to do with race."
Wadleigh is a Harlem school that has been shaken up. Housed in a turn-of-the-century building, it was rated one of the city's most decrepit schools in 1988. Then the city spent $47 million renovating the school, and it was divided into alternative schools for writing, technology and the arts. But parents say the work is unfinished, with supplies scarce and test scores too low.
The struggle to help Wadleigh shed its old image and problems led Ms. Hardy to run. "I'd like to see teachers held accountable when they fail the students," she said. "When my daughter went to Wadleigh her grades went from honor to horror. We had teachers who couldn't teach."
Stumping for votes at the Frederick Douglass Houses in Manhattan Valley, Ms. Best-Hardy and Carmen Quinones, an outspoken Democratic district leader and Wadleigh parent, stopped parents in a playground. "Mira, this is Page Best-Hardy," Ms. Quinones told her neighbors. "She's running for school board."
Norma Rutledge, another Wadleigh parent, accompanied them. "We need textbooks and materials," said Ms. Rutledge. "But when the parents come out it's like the district is not really listening."
Public school board meetings often resemble gripe fests, but they give parents a sounding board for their complaints in the form of a microphone, an audience and a captive panel of board members. Both Harlem board representatives said that Harlem parents sometimes felt like they were not taken seriously at meetings.
"It was very hard to get attention focused on schools north of 110th Street," Ms. Brock said. "When my parents came out the board's attention was not as focused. But they have concerns just like Upper West Side parents. Many are young and don't work and are turned off by how the board treats them."
 
GIFTED AND TALENTED ON THE UPPER WEST SIDE
While the issue for Harlem residents is improving low-performing schools, parents downtown worry about keeping their schools successful.
Take the squabble about moving P.S. 9. A group of parents, enraged by the district's plan to move their gifted and talented program to a wheelchair-accessible school five blocks away, sent protest letters and faxes, held a candle-light vigil, and lobbied the board until they won a yearlong reprieve.
The issue stirred up the old debate about gifted and talented programs. Supporters say they are needed to stimulate smart children, give poor students a better education and offer middle-class students an alternative to private schools. Detractors worry that they siphon smart students away from other schools, fail to identify qualified minority candidates and are elitist.
Echoes of the P.S. 9 skirmish resonate quietly but unmistakably in the current campaign. It radicalized many parents there, including the three who went on to form Padres por los Ninos. Mr. Gliber, as president of P.S. 9's P.T.A., played a crucial role in the struggle to keep the program.
While the two slates are publicly cordial and even friendly, the issue brings out pointed attacks in private. The Padres accuse the Equities of secretly wishing to destroy gifted programs. The Equities accuse the Padres of running for one reason: out of concern for their own children's gifted classes.
The issue surfaced at a forum when the candidates were asked their views on gifted programs.
"I have very mixed feelings about them," answered Judith Moore, an incumbent running on the Equity and Excellence slate. "I think that ideally, heterogeneous classes are preferable. Of course, there are some children who are so precocious that they need special attention."
After the debate a man in a tweed coat saw Ms. Moore speaking with a reporter and approached her. "How can you say you have mixed feelings about gifted programs," he asked, "when both of your children attend gifted programs?"
The man happened to be Dr. Paul Ehrlich, the husband of Avis Alexander, a candidate on the Padres por los Ninos slate. This was a bit of family spin doctoring, school-board-election style.
But one of the campaign's most interesting alliances has emerged between the P.S. 9 parents and some Harlem and Manhattan Valley parents. The Padres por los Ninos have been outspoken on the subject of uptown schools. "The people in the south," Mr. Gliber said at a forum, his voice rising, "for the most part don't believe that the people in the north can succeed. And it's unfair."
One of the endorsements his slate picked up was that of Ms. Quinones, who is acting as campaign manager for Ms. Best-Hardy. And they have also made inroads with many Hispanic candidates through the candidacy of Gabriel Guzman, the only member of their slate who is not from P.S. 9. Mr. Guzman lives in Manhattan Valley.
 
BILINGUAL EDUCATION IN MANHATTAN VALLEY
If gifted programs rouse Upper West Siders, bilingual education is the hot topic in Manhattan Valley, a corridor bounded by 100th Street, 110th Street, Central Park West and Amsterdam Avenue.
More than 2,100 children, or 15 percent of District 3's students, took bilingual classes in 1993. All but a few spoke Spanish. And at the loudest stop along the campaign trail, the fund raiser for Mr. Guzman at the Taller Latinoamericano, a community gallery on Broadway at 104th Street, the talk between dances, drinks and flaky empanadas centered on bilingual classes.
Kiko Franco and El Negro Merengue pumped out the music. The one-two thump of the electric bass anchored couples as they twirled around the dance floor with rigid shoulders and busy legs.
"Los ninos necesitan mejor educacion," Mr. Guzman, an immigrant from the Dominican Republic, told about 80 supporters. "Children need a better education."
There are two main concerns about bilingual classes: that they do not teach students English quickly enough and that the quality of instruction in Spanish is poor. Fewer than 9 percent of the district's bilingual elementary school pupils could read at grade level in 1993, even in their own language.
Radames Rivera, a supporter from Washington Heights, was worried that the bad bilingual classes could derail New York's tradition of welcoming immigrants, educating them and giving them opportunities for success. "We're talking about a large community, a poor community," he said. "And education is important."
 
THE POLITICAL INTERESTS AND THEIR CLOUT
Despite the crucial issues, the race for Board 3 has the air of a phantom campaign at times. At forum after forum, the 14 candidates outnumbered their audience.
But it took 2,056 votes to win in the last election. So candidates must seek support from the focused interest groups adept at getting out the vote with big mailing lists, phone banks and active members.
The United Federation of Teachers is not taking sides in District 3. But Local 372 of District Council 37, the union representing school paraprofessionals, is printing up fliers and offering free phones to the the Equity and Excellence slate.
With this kind of support in mind, the candidates filed into the auditorium of Bank Street College one night to ask for the endorsement of the Broadway Democrats, a Morningside Heights-based political club.
As the candidates prepared to speak, the moderator, Alex Peltz, offered a friendly piece of advice: "This club has a history of voting against people who speak too long."
But there was never much suspense. After all, two Equity and Excellence candidates, Larry Sauer and Lucy Wicks, are members. Both won endorsements, along with three other members of their slate. Before the election the club's newsletter will break this news to hundreds of likely voters.
As the only openly gay candidate running in the district, Mr. Sauer has other, potentially more valuable, endorsements. He is backed by the Gay and Lesbian Independent Democrats, the Stonewall Democrats and the Empire State Pride Agenda.
Those endorsements made all the difference in the 1993 election, when the debate over the Rainbow Curriculum -- a series of initiatives about teaching about sexuality, tolerance and related themes -- mobilized both gay groups and the Christian Coalition and attracted the highest turnout in the history of school elections, 12.5 percent.
"I thought getting elected would be easy, and I would have a lot of extra votes to pull a slate," said Mr. Sauer, who has no children and has never been to a school board meeting. "In the last election, whoever Empire Pride supported in Manhattan became the greatest vote-getter."
But the Rainbow Curriculum has receded as an issue, and the Christian Coalition appears to be staying away from the race. The most conservative candidate is David C. Isaacs, a civil service examiner running as an independent who said he rejected the coalition's help. "I'm open to a pledge of allegiance followed by a moment of silence," he said in a telephone interview. "As far as as the gay agenda goes, I have spoken to gay groups and I feel very strong for tolerance. But not for teaching life styles."
 
THE FUTURE: TAKE IT TO THE SCHOOLS
This could well be the twilight of the school board era, its last hurrah. Several lawmakers have proposed bills that would alter or abolish the system. But many parents have already anticipated them and chosen to focus their involvement at the school level, as opposed to the district level.
Several schools in District 3 already have parents councils, which help select teachers and go over curriculum. The State Assembly leadership wants school-based councils to replace the local boards. "It's much more than bake sales," Ms. Alexander, who serves on one such council, likes to say.
Advocates of school-based management argue that parents will be more likely to get involved at the school level. Skeptics note that the bitter fights over decentralized school boards led only to continued apathy.
Ms. Wicks has made the councils a major theme of her campaign. "We need to train administrators and teachers to bring families in," she said. "I really believe it is through local school-based management that the schools will improve."

GRAPHIC: Photos: APRIL 16, P.S. 9 -- There is so little interest that it is not unusual for candidates to outnumber the people in the audience at forums. (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times); APRIL 18, MARTIN LUTHER KING JR. HIGH SCHOOL -- The vast majority of parents choose to get involved on the school, not district level. (Thomas Dallal for The New York Times) (pg. 12)
 
 
Chart/Map/Photos: "School District 3: A Portrait"
 In the school board election in District 3 in Manhattan on May 7, a major issue is the disparity in the performance of schools in the northern part of the district and the southern. Map showing the location of the district's schools shows where at least half of the students tested above grade level in math and in reading during 1995 and where less than half tested that way.
 
THE INTERESTS AND WHERE THEY ARE
A campaign strategy session in Harlem: Parents believe that schools in the southern portion of the district get too much attention and that Harlem schools need a major boost in support.
A merengue fundraiser in Manhattan Valley: Bilingual education is a major concern in the eastern part of the district, especially above 90th Street, where parents are worried that Spanish-speaking children are being short-changed.
A candidates' forum at P.S. 9 on West 84th Street: Gifted and Talented programs are a big issue in the Upper West Side, where middle and upper-middle class parents are trying to avoid private school. (pg. 1)
 
 
Charts: "POWER AND LIMITS OF SCHOOL BOARDS"
School boards are nine-member, unpaid bodies that help set educational policy in elementary and middle schools. Here is a look at what they can, and can't do:
 
POWER: Approve budgets, made by superintendents, that often are more than $50 million a year
 
LIMIT: Little discretion: most money already earmarked by city and state.
When Board 3 refused to pass its budget no one noticed.
 
POWER: Dictate class size in each grade, create special programs and alternative schools, choose how to spend limited funds
 
LIMIT: In era of fiscal straits, it often comes down to what to cut: after school, guidance counselors, teacher's aids, etc.
 
POWER: Hire superintendent to run day-to-day operations of the school; Tell superintendent what the educational priorities should be.
 
LIMIT: Since superintendents often know more about education than the board, strong-willed ones sometimes run the board rather than vice-versa.
 
POWER: Hire principals, assistant principals
 
LIMIT: School staff and parents choose a list of candidates and a superintendents whittles it down to a few names before the board decides.

"School Elections: A Primer"
WHEN TO VOTE
Polls open Tuesday, May 7 from 6 A.M. to 9 P.M.
 
WHO CAN VOTE
 Registered voters, and those with children in public schools, regardless of whether they are citizens, who registered as parent voters before April 12.
 
WHERE TO VOTE
 At your local polling station. But if your child attends school outside your district, and you are a registered parent voter, you can vote at a polling site in your child's school district. Call (212) VOTE-NYC (868-3692) with any questions.
 
HOW TO VOTE
 Rank candidates on paper ballots in the numerical order of your preference: first choice, second choice, and so one down the line.
 
HOW THE VOTES ARE COUNTED
This part confuses even the most civic-minded and well-informed voters, not to mention many candidates themselves.
The Board of Elections counts the total number of votes cast and calculates the minimum number needed to win a seat on a nine-member board: 10 percent of the votes plus one. So if 10,000 people vote, it takes 1,001 votes to win a seat on the board.
Then first-place votes are tallied. All the candidates who win 1,001 first-place votes win a seat. Once they pass the 1,001 threshold, the superfluous votes are transferred to the second choice candidates. After the second choices win, the excess votes go to the third choices. When the redistributed votes no longer put a candidate over the edge, the votes are transferred up from the last-place candidates.
This system is called proportional representation. Critics say it is too confusing to voters. Fans say it increases the representation of ethnic and ideological minorities.
It makes forming a slate a virtual necessity. Candidates band together and distribute small fliers listing their own names as the suggested first choices and their colleagues as second choice, third choice, and so on down the line. With so many candidates to keep track of, voters often take cards from a candidate they like and then vote the rest of the slate. (pg. 13)

HEADLINE: Perspective;
The Urban Myth of Baseball as Savior

BYLINE: By ROBERT LIPSYTE 

 BODY:
Sit in the spare, cramped owner's box at Milwaukee's County Stadium, and it's actually possible to feel sorry for the owner; the box has the comfort and charm of a Formica booth in a greasy diner off a bypassed old highway. Even on the crisply glorious afternoon two weeks ago when the Brewers were beating the Yankees, what was the point of belonging to the league of loathsome richies if you didn't even have a condo wet bar to entertain politicians, objects of desire and the kids who snubbed you in high school?
Allan H. ("Please call me Bud") Selig, president of the Brewers and the acting baseball commissioner, accepts the sympathy but not the commentary. He is a romantic; he thinks that Milwaukee truly needs baseball, that baseball truly needs Milwaukee and that Lord knows, Bud needs baseball in Milwaukee. And a new ball park. 
 Because of his obvious passion for the game -- and because it has become his only business -- Bud's fiscal field of dreams is the most poignant of the current cries by the richies for their very own Camden Yards, almost always paid for by taxpayers who are then supposed to further support their teams by buying tickets that generally have to be made more expensive to pay off the debt on the new parks. This is happening in Cincinnati, Detroit, Boston, Seattle and Minneapolis.
Like warring expert witnesses, designated economists fly in to stoutly maintain that a new park will either pump fresh capital into a city or merely divert other entertainment dollars. Most agree, however, that it's at least a short-term feel-good for a city otherwise failing its citizens.
But baseball doesn't really have the power now, if it ever did, to provide a city with a lasting plaza of the spirit. Cities are too fragmented now, their problems too complex for pep rallies, which don't even work in urban high schools. Wise cities like San Francisco make the richies buy their own sandboxes. Crazed cities like New York flip-flop from plan to plan, whipped by an untrustworthy owner and a mayor who seems to think baseball will give him soul.
Selig's Milwaukee is in the middle; some people are willing to be a minor-league satellite of Chicago if the alternative is a tax and a bond issue, and some demand a big club of their own, at any price, as proof of their big cityhood.
Bud just wants baseball.
It was his mother, a schoolteacher, who hooked him on the game. During summer trips to New York, she alternated museum visits with trips to the ball parks. Bud was a history major at the University of Wisconsin, came home to take over his father's auto dealership and became a proper burgher. After the Braves quit Milwaukee, it was Selig who led the local search for a replacement.
"I remember after the last Braves game," said Bud, his mobile face twisting, his long, graceful fingers tracing words, "a handicapped woman came up to me and said, 'You're all we've got, Bud, don't fail us.' "
He didn't, and in 1970, an old minor-league name put Milwaukee back on the major league news network again. But this big, shabby, multi-purpose municipal stadium could make the city minor league again; Bud says that without the profits from luxury boxes and the other amenities of the modern old-fashioned "ball yards" that have been such hits in Baltimore and Cleveland, small-market clubs like Milwaukee cannot be competitive on the field, and probably won't stay in business.
Now 61, Bud raised his eyes beyond the giant beer stein in deep center (after each Brewer homer, a man slides into the beer) to the parking lot and saw an adult version of his childhood -- a baseball-only ball park that evokes Ebbets Field, but has a retractable roof. The beginnings and ends of baseball seasons are a cold sit in Wisconsin.
Bud is not threatening to leave. Where would he go? Unlike George Steinbrenner, he is of the place. And unlike Peter A. Magowan, the Safeway son who is raising private money for the Giants park in San Francisco, Bud doesn't seem to have the money or the collateral to do this by himself, despite the promise of help from the league.
It is "the posturing politicians," according to Bud, "making themselves look good to voters who don't want to spend money" who are making all the noise in Milwaukee and in other cities about taxpayer rip-off. But he says it almost sadly, certainly philosophically, which makes him the anti-George, a bright and pleasant man, direct and without bluff, who can sound almost confident when he says, "I think this will all work out because it is right."
The long fingers are moving again, weaving his dreams. "Baseball is such a wonderful part of our lives, there is nothing bad about it. And I sense we are in the early stages of a renaissance. Revenue sharing. Interleague play. The owners aren't saying bad things about the players and they're not saying bad things about us.
"But the heart of the game is not players or owners, it's the ballparks. Bart Giamatti called them 'our cathedrals.' They bind people together."
His eyes lift again to the Ebbets Field that he sees best. It's too bad that Bud and Bart are wrong.

HEADLINE: Credit Check;
Happy Days Are Here Again! Right, Voters?

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
BILL CLINTON wrote the book on how to run against the economy in 1992, when the country was in a recessionary funk and the now-famous "It's the Economy, Stupid" sign hung in his campaign headquarters.
Four years later, he is discovering that it is far harder to take credit for an essentially well-paved economy -- 8.5 million jobs created, exports surging -- that is cratered by one large pothole: the much-documented if inchoate anxiety that jobs are less secure than ever and that wages are going nowhere, unless you're in the loftiest executive suite. 
After months of internal bickering, the Administration is finally rolling out its economic theme song for Campaign '96: Look at what a great job we've done! The refrain: For the next four years, we'll ease the pain of the hard-working souls caught in the transition from the old economy to the new one.
A happy tune, but it leaves open the question of how to get much mileage from statistics that are the envy of the G-7 allies, who don't vote, but little solace to American workers who do.
 
 
 
Bored by the Details
 Fortunately for Clinton campaign strategists, however befuddling the nation's economic mood looks to them, it has truly left Bob Dole flummoxed. His own advisers concede that the whole subject of job insecurity and wages bores him. That may explain his awkward handling of the minimum wage debate, an example of what can happen to politicians who wander aimlessly into the minefield of the economic inequality debate.
"You can't have an economic strategy until the candidate sits down and develops one," one of Mr. Dole's colleagues and supporters in the Senate fumed on Thursday, as Democrats popped up on the evening news portraying Mr. Dole as the enemy of workers struggling to stay off welfare on a measly $4.25 an hour.
It's still early in the campaign, and Mr. Dole may yet step forward with a convincing economic pitch. But the Republicans have been struggling to find an economic agenda for six months now, and Mr. Dole would be forgiven for wishing for the comparative simplicity of the economy that faced Mr. Clinton in 1992.
Then the issue was reviving American industry and making the world's biggest economy competitive against a host of external threats. Mr. Clinton's answer was one part "Putting People First," one part tough trade talk and one part big Government programs like Al Gore's "information superhighway."
"The world view in 1992 was that Japan is unstoppable, and the challenge to America was external," said John N. Yochelson, the head of the Council on Competitiveness, a Washington organization born in the mid-80's to help create a national industrial strategy.
But today American officials treat Japan as a nation choking on its own bank failures and arcane regulations. That is why Mr. Clinton could go to Tokyo two weeks ago, barely utter a harsh word about trade, and know that Mr. Dole would not make a peep in response.
 
 
 
The Pay Problem
 Now, even while some workers fear that their jobs are headed to Mexico or China, Mr. Yochelson argues that America's main challenge is "squaring domestic growth with high-wage jobs."
Some Democrats say this conundrum is not all bad. It keeps eyes trained on the economy rather than on issues of character, Mr. Dole's presumed strength. And polls show most workers do not blame the President for their insecurities.
Nonetheless, capitalizing on a sound economy that is not generating bigger paychecks is no easy task. The Democrats learned that in 1994, when the economy looked pretty good and the Republican Congress was swept into power.
So the White House is settling on a middle ground that was mapped out last week by the Council of Economic Advisers. After recent press reports of devastation from corporate downsizing, Laura D'Andrea Tyson, Mr. Clinton's chief economic strategist, asked the Council a few basic questions: Do the new jobs being created pay as well as the old ones? Are A.T.&T. executives really flipping burgers? And are white-collar layoffs -- sorry, Washington calls them "displacements" -- really rising, or is that an illusion created by white-collar reporters?
The answers: Yes, 68 percent of the new jobs pay better than the median wages of America's 120 million or so workers. No, employment in burger-flipping is actually falling, and most new service-sector jobs are managerial or professional. But the skeptics are on to something: in the 1991-92 recession, white-collar workers were "considerably more at risk of displacement" than in the 1981-82 recession, particularly those 55 or older. And the average displaced worker takes a 10 percent pay cut -- more or less permanently.
"Are we saying there are no reasons for workers to be anxious?" asks Ms. Tyson. "Absolutely not. There are plenty of reasons. But you can't expect to reverse a long-term trend in just three years."
Polls show nearly three-quarters of voters know someone who has lost a job, and the White House is betting they will like Mr. Clinton's calls for portable health insurance (passed unanimously by the Senate last week), portable pensions and a $10,000 tax credit for college education or retraining. The argument inside the Administration is over how much to intrude upon corporate decision-making. Ms. Tyson and Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin drew the line when Robert Reich, the Labor Secretary, advocated tax benefits for companies that guarantee jobs in profitable times and give workers a share in growing profits.
If the Administration is nervous about bashing corporate America. Mr. Dole won't even think about it. Asked how to address worker anxiety, he talks almost entirely about shrinking the Government and cutting the deficit. And he has yet to put together a plan beyond the rising-tide-raises-all-boats approach that Republicans love but that blue-collar voters find increasingly suspect.
He is already in retreat on the minimum wage, as his own troops defect from the party line.
"The Republicans are in trouble on this in a larger way than they ever expected," Mr. Reich crowed in an interview Friday, still surprised by the issue's sudden revival.
"When the stock market is soaring and chief executives are earning princely sums, it's hard to argue that the nation will fall apart because we mandate another 90 cents an hour to the poorest wage-earners in the nation," Mr. Reich said. "But if Dole wants to dig that hole, I'll be happy to hand him a shovel."

HEADLINE: On Shanghai Visit, Yeltsin Finds It Is Russia's Turn to Be Envious

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

DATELINE: SHANGHAI, April 27

 BODY:
The last time a Russian leader came to this city, in May 1989, the streets were clogged with student protesters demanding democracy and China seemed to be on the brink of cataclysmic political change.
The leader, President Mikhail S. Gorbachev of the Soviet Union, was hailed by students camped outside City Hall as the kind of forward-looking man that China needed to abandon its outdated ideology and rescue dilapidated cities like Shanghai. 
 Mr. Gorbachev's host, who steered him away from the students, was a local Communist Party chief named Jiang Zemin, who looked like an old-fashioned apparatchik about to be washed away by the tides of history.
It did not work out that way.
This week, seven years later, President Boris N. Yeltsin came to Shanghai to find it transformed, a showcase for China's remarkable economic growth, overseen by the Communist Party. And Mr. Yeltsin's host was none other than Jiang Zemin, now the Communist Party chief for all of China, and President as well.
The Russian President left Shanghai this morning, ending a four-day trip to China that called attention to an odd turn of fortunes, one that nobody could have predicted. Mr. Gorbachev is long gone, and now Mr. Yeltsin himself is facing a tough election in June and is plagued by economic trouble, while Mr. Jiang sits serenely atop a thriving economy.
Social scientists often debate the relative merits of emphasizing political and economic reform in a society emerging from Communist rule, pointing to a Russian versus a Chinese model, as if the experience of one country could be transplanted to another. But it is clear that during Mr. Gorbachev's visit, many Chinese coveted the kind of political freedom that was flourishing in the Soviet Union at the time, and that this time it was the Russian's turn to be envious.
Mr. Yeltsin looked wide-eyed as he toured a high-tech plant in East Shanghai, a special economic zone that was not even opened until 1990, a year after Mr. Gorbachev came. He shook his head in wonder as Chinese officials explained how the zone had grown at an average of 30 percent a year, and been transformed from a cowpatch to an industrial park with more than 3,000 joint ventures and well over $7 billion in investment.
During his tour of Shanghai, Mr. Yeltsin said he was eager to learn from the Chinese experience, describing what he saw as "very impressive."
"We are also in the middle of reforms," he told reporters. "Here, they call it a socialist market economy with Chinese characteristics. Russia is not building capitalism either -- it is building a market economy with its own specific flavor."
James R. Lilley, who was the United States Ambassador to China in 1989, came to Shanghai to welcome an ill-timed visit by American warships on the day Mr. Gorbachev came that year. By chance, Mr. Lilley was here again this week on a private visit when Mr. Yeltsin came.
"The streets were full of demonstrators," Mr. Lilley recalled of 1989. "It was out of control."
Mr. Lilley said part of China's aim in inviting Mr. Yeltsin to visit was to try to send a message to the United States, China's biggest export market, that Beijing had alternatives. He dismissed it as an empty threat.
"If China wants to go back to the Sino-Soviet relationship of the 1950's, well, lots of luck," Mr. Lilley said, noting that China's trade with Russia, at $5 billion, was a tiny fraction of the $55 billion it bought and sold with the United States. "It's a different world."

HEADLINE: Thai Shrimp Farmers Facing Ecologists' Fury

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS 

DATELINE: KHO KIAM, Thailand, April 22

 BODY:
Like thousands of other shrimp farmers in Asia and Latin America, all Maitri Visetsak wanted was to get rich quick.
He meant no harm, Mr. Maitri said the other day on a tour of his property here in southern Thailand. And at first it worked. In two years, Mr. Maitri, a small landowner, earned enough to buy two pickup trucks. 
 But then, in a pattern that has left much of Thailand's coast a polluted wasteland, his project failed. His shrimp sickened and died, and his two half-acre ponds now lie stagnant and toxic, useless for further cultivation.
On Monday, men like Mr. Maitri will go on mock trial in absentia in New York at a "Shrimp Tribunal" organized by environmental groups. They are accused of despoiling their country's coastal wetlands by raising shrimp.
Organizers of the tribunal say they are out to moderate the surge over the last decade of shrimp farming that is fast depleting the mangrove forests that are an essential coastal ecosystem.
"It is a very important issue in terms of global biodiversity," said Kate Cissna, one of the organizers, noting the role that mangroves play in sustaining food chains and stabilizing coastal lands.
Here in the southern Thai province of Trang, shrimp are everywhere -- in the mangrove forests, in the rice fields and even in the rubber plantations.
Somewhat bewildered, villagers continue to farm and fish, even as their land and water become polluted by shrimp-farm runoff. For the most part, they are victims, not beneficiaries, of a boom industry that is controlled from far away by syndicates or middle-class investors.
The transformation of Trang Province is part of a worldwide explosion of shrimp farming from a $1.5 billion industry 30 years ago to an $8 billion industry today, said Ms. Cissna, who is field coordinator for the Seattle-based Mangrove Action Project.
Her small lobbying group is sponsoring the forum on Monday, in offices at the United Nations, along with the Natural Resources Defense Council, the Sierra Club of Canada and other groups.
In Western countries, shrimp farming is mostly environmentally sound and monitored by marine biologists, said Bob Rosenberry, who edits and publishes Shrimp News International, an industry publication, in San Diego.
But in developing countries like Thailand, India and Mexico, he said, "The overall track record has been a bad one." Shrimp farmers here are "mostly small-scale, irresponsible guys" with little concern or understanding of the damage they are causing, Mr. Rosenberry said.
The farmers' badly managed ponds, with their heavy doses of chemicals and antibiotics, overstocked conditions and high rate of toxicity and disease, have an average life span of less than five years. And so, much in the manner of slash-and-burn farmers, shrimp farmers simply march down the coastline, leaving hundreds of miles of poisonous brown blotches in their wake.
The ponds saturate the surrounding soil with salt and pollute the land and water with a chemical sludge made up of fertilizer and antibiotics as well as larvicides, shrimp feed and waste. This is not what was envisioned by international lending agencies when they encouraged aquaculture as an aid to developing economies and a means to help feed the world's growing population.
While in some cases peasant farmers have benefited, the industry has mostly been taken over by profiteers, producing shrimp as a quick-cash luxury crop for export, mostly to the United States and Japan, and providing little income to the local poor.
"Yes, there is a need to feed the world's population, but shrimp farming is not the answer," said Pisit Chansnoh, director of Yadfon, a small environmental lobbying group here in Trang. "In general, the shrimp farm industry does not belong to the poor. Only the rich can run this business."
Since 1994, Thailand has been the world's leading exporter of seafood, with farmed shrimp its primary product. Exports of frozen shrimp nearly doubled in value in the last two years to $2 billion, from $1.2 billion, giving Thailand 40 percent of the world market. In 1995, Thailand produced 220,000 metric tons of shrimp in 20,000 farms, far more than any other country.
The Government has encouraged shrimp farming, with its fast profits, despite the lessons of Taiwan, China, Indonesia and other countries, where a quick boom was followed by collapse. Thailand has sustained its industry for a decade because its thousands of miles of coastline allow investors to exhaust their farms and then move on.
This pattern has been devastating to the mangrove forests that once lined as much as three-fourths of the world's tropical shorelines. Today, the United Nations Environment Program calculates that half have been lost, and some estimates blame shrimp farming for one-fourth of this destruction.
Mangroves, dismissed until recently even in academic literature as existing in "wastelands" and "mosquito-infested swamps," are coastal trees that grow half in and half out of the water in places like the Everglades of Florida and the bayous of Louisiana.
In Thailand, the United Nations estimates that only 13 percent of the original mangrove forests remain. On the coast of the Gulf of Thailand, mangrove forests have been virtually wiped out; just 40,000 acres remain of what was nearly a million acres just 30 years ago, Government figures show.
Here on the coast of the Andaman Sea, the country's last broad stands of mangrove still support an entire way of life. The village of Wang Won, for example, has subsisted for a century by harvesting the shellfish that thrive among the mangrove roots.
Its 600 people fish and collect honey and mud crabs, thatch their houses and roll their cigarettes from among the mangroves. Even the village paths are paved with crushed shellfish.
But in the fields that surround the village, the area's first shrimp farms bake in the hot sun, some already exhausted and abandoned, some still productive, their motorized paddle wheels churning to aerate the briny water.
The ponds here at Wang Won are owned by city folk from far away and run by low-paid workers imported from the even poorer villages of northeastern Thailand.
Their only effect on the people who live here is to make life harder, said Supit Bunrit, who sorts and shucks seashells on her front stoop for a living. They have siphoned away its drinking water, killed edible sea grass and begun to reduce the catch of grouper and crabs.
This is the start of a familiar process of environmental loss, said Mr. Pisit. "If the mangrove forests on the Andaman coast follow the destruction in other provinces, I do not see a future for Thai mangrove forests," he said. "And once the mangrove forests are lost, there will be no future for these small fishermen."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Shrimp farming has produced quick profits in Thailand, but polluted thousands of acres of wetlands. Maitri Visetsak, a small landowner, walked between two shrimp farms, the one on the left thriving, the other dead. (Seth Mydans/The New York Times)
 
Map of Thailand highlighting Kho Kiam: Much of the coast near Kho Kiam has become a polluted wasteland.
