HEADLINE: Iraqi Oil Impasse Hits Gulf War Victims

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

DATELINE: GENEVA, April 27

 BODY:
The suspension of talks between Iraq and the United Nations this week was especially bitter news for Carlos Alzamora.
For more than four years, this former Peruvian diplomat and his team of experts have been collecting and sifting the claims of more than two million people who say they were hurt in some way -- economically, physically or psychologically -- by Iraq's invasion of Kuwait in August 1990. Thirty percent of the money that would have been collected from the sale of Iraqi oil that might have come out of the talks would have flowed into the nearly empty coffers of Mr. Alzamora's compensation fund, at the rate of about $100 million a month. 
 A failure to reach agreement -- when and if the talks resume -- Mr. Alzamora said in an interview, "would postpone indefinitely the payment of compensation and would dash the hopes of millions of innocent victims relying on this promised relief that we stand ready to provide."
The processing of claims has gone quickly and efficiently -- "one of the greatest U.N. success stories," Mr. Alzamora said as he sat in his quiet office in a small villa called La Pelousse, set in the lawns of the United Nations' Geneva headquarters.
The house has an appropriately melancholy history. It was the residence of the secretaries general of the ill-fated League of Nations, whose Art Deco headquarters nearby are now occupied by the United Nations.
By the time the League collapsed, shunned by the United States, it had so little money left that it could no longer afford to heat La Pelousse, and the house became unlivable.
By the time Mr. Alzamora's United Nations Compensation Commission inherited the villa, it needed work. Most of all, it needed modern storage space. That was created out of a dank basement whose wine cellar now holds shelves of computer disks. The rest of the basement is piled high with shipping cartons and courier packages.
"We have 29 million pieces of paper here," he said. "They would cover 20 soccer fields."
The Compensation Commission was created by the United Nations Security Council shortly after the end of the 1991 war that drove the Iraqis out of Kuwait. It began receiving claims by early 1992 -- sometimes at the rate of 30,000 a day.
There were the obvious cases: the many thousands of Kuwaitis who lost homes and livelihoods, and foreigners who had been working in both Kuwait and Iraq, as well as the relatives of people who died in the invasion. But there were also many unexpected claimants, Mr. Alzamora said, some with stories that need a lot of checking out.
"We had a rich Kuwaiti claiming millions for his wife's jewels that he said had been in the basement," he said. "He had no papers, no receipts. There was a man who wanted to be paid for his three kilograms of rice. There was a lady in Prague who claims that she crashed her car when she heard the news of the invasion."
Even unexceptional claims were often difficult to verify. If someone claimed to have been chased out of Iraq or Kuwait, are there papers to prove it? Where, for instance, are the receipts for travel and relocation? To resolve such cases, Mr. Alzamora said, Iraq cooperated by turning over its records on foreign workers in the country.
When the period for filing claims ended at the beginning of this year, 2.6 million claims had been submitted, seeking a total of more than $190 billion in compensation. The commission divided the claims into six categories: people forced out of Iraq or Kuwait; those who died or suffered serious injury; those who lost property up to $100,000; those who claimed more than $100,000 in losses; corporate claims, and claims made by governments or international organizations.
Mr. Alzamora said that by dealing with the most urgent individual cases first, the commission and its 100 staff members -- speaking 30 languages among them -- were able to pay a number of claims in full, at a cost of $13.4 million. But the compensation fund, which has depended on borrowing and contributions from United Nations members, is now down to $7 million, and only down payments are being made, with promises of full settlement in the future. With the suspension of the oil talks, that future has become more distant.
The commission is now facing the adjudication of 6,150 corporate claims from companies in 10 countries, including the United States, and 256 claims from governments and international organizations.
"Our main challenge has been to be ready when the money is ready," said Mr. Alzamora, who apologized for bragging when he said that the speed with which these compensation claims had been decided compared favorably with similar processes after the two world wars and many other international disputes.
Only now there will not be any ready money soon.

HEADLINE: Stamford Shapes Up to Hold Off Lean Years;
After a Decade of Malaise, the City Takes Steps to Tame Its Bureaucracy

BYLINE: By GEORGE JUDSON 

DATELINE: STAMFORD, Conn., April 27

 BODY:
In the sleek corporate office building called Government Center here, the chief diversion of city workers these days is looking out the window to watch the swift progress of the construction of the Swiss Bank Corporation's new headquarters.
Day by day, the international banking giant, which is moving its trading floor and 1,500 employees from Manhattan, is filling in one of the last gaps in a downtown that corporations leaving New York transformed in the 70's and 80's. A new wave of construction and growth appears assured. 
 But while marveling at the work in progress from his 10th-floor office, Mayor Dannel Malloy says with urgency that it is now the city government's turn to be transformed -- and he is looking to the hard experiences of cities like Bridgeport for lessons on how to do it.
If government does not become more efficient, in the next economic crisis Stamford, despite its wealth, would face the same dismal choice that other cities have: cut services or raise property taxes to levels that drive out the middle class. But if Mr. Malloy succeeds, and Stamford can control taxes but still spend enough on its schools and the police, this city of 110,000 people has a chance to be unlike almost any other in the Northeast: a stable, racially and economically mixed city paying its own way.
But to achieve that, after already cutting its work force by one-sixth during the recession of the late 80's and early 90's, the city must remake its bureaucracy in good times, in major ways that other cities have tried only in financial crises.
Mr. Malloy hired his director of public safety, John Byrne, from Bridgeport, where he was an assistant to Joseph Ganim, the Mayor elected after that city tried to declare bankruptcy in 1991. "When we came to Bridgeport, a grenade had been thrown into City Hall," Mr. Byrne said. "The unions were ready to play, and we used that to our advantage and to their advantage. We used the budget as the motivation for a lot of management changes."
"But all taxpayers seem to think the same," said Mr. Byrne, who oversees the police and fire departments. "They don't think they're getting their money's worth, and to a degree they're right. The employees of this city have to understand that taxpayers here don't want to pay another dollar."
A number of factors have combined to give Mr. Malloy, a Democrat elected last November, a strong hand in trying to modernize city government. Voters amended the City Charter to merge several departments. And they ended gridlock between the Republican finance board and Democratic city council by electing Democratic majorities to both.
Perhaps most important, the voters were reacting to a property-tax increase of 22 percent during the term of his predecessor, Stanley Esposito, as the collapse of commercial real-estate values in the recession hastened a shift of the tax burden toward homeowners. The bill for the boom times had arrived at the worst possible time.
Some residents made noises about moving or even, in the affluent suburban area north of the Merritt Parkway, about seceding. Others listened to Mr. Malloy's theme that Stamford was strong enough to solve its problems; by spending money smarter, future tax increases could be limited to the rate of inflation.
"The community psyche was on a downward trend, maybe for as long as the last eight years, worrying about the economy, worrying about lost jobs, worrying about quality-of-life issues, and not seeing things get better," Mr. Malloy said.
"Stamford was clearly suffering a malaise, and that's what I was trying to respond to," he added. "I said, 'Wait a minute, we can manage our way out of this.' "
To a large extent, the economic dislocations of the past decade in the New York region were sorting themselves into ways that were now nourishing the economy of Stamford and lower Fairfield County.
Madison Avenue's mass dismissals in the mid-80's had spawned dozens of small advertising agencies here. As large corporate headquarters shed employees, some started their own businesses, providing services that the headquarters now needed to buy. And Stamford had begun to court smaller companies like reinsurance, that were more likely to grow in the future.
With 20 million square feet of premium office space, Stamford, particularly downtown, had plenty of room for a new breed of businesses that wanted to be near each other -- that found value in being in a city.
"The smaller companies and the streamlined headquarters aren't self-sufficient anymore," said Kip Bergstrom, Stamford's director of economic development, "and people are understanding that you need to interact with the people you work with. You can't be off by yourself in the woods."
Even Stamford's government benefited from the corporate restructuring: Government Center. Built and then declared surplus by GTE in the 1980's, it seemed to symbolize the city that Stamford had become.
But little had changed besides appearances. When Mr. Malloy and his cabinet moved in, they found their offices equipped with 1982 Wang word processors.
Employees were covered by a "hodgepodge" of insurance plans, said Thomas Hamilton, the director of administration. The heads of small departments were accustomed to taking budget issues to the Board of Finance themselves, instead of coordinating them.
Often city employees did their work well, said Patricia Broom, the director of operations, but not because there was any method to it. Putting together this year's budget for pothole and other street repairs, she said, she found that the city never surveyed its own streets; it repaired whatever residents complained about.
"If you didn't call me and tell me your road needed to be fixed, it would never get fixed," she said.
During flush times, these inefficiencies did not matter. "While the tax base was growing, the needs of the city could be met simply by growing the city government, by spending more money," said Christopher Bruhl, president of a regional business group, the Southwestern Area Commerce and Industry Association.
"At the same time," he said, "there was no pressure on Stamford to modernize either its form of government or the management of government. They're locked into a lot of things like 1980's computers and management systems out of the 1950's."
Now, however, the large tax increases mean that Stamford has to make government work better without growing, except for two areas that are essential to keeping the city attractive to its middle class and to its corporations: schools and police.
For what sets Stamford apart from other old industrial cities is that it is still overwhelmingly middle class, in large part because the northern half of the city never broke off as a separate suburb. As a result, the problems of poverty are still manageable, the schools still integrated.
So Mr. Malloy has pledged to solve the city's problems by redesigning city government. He has formed a task force to reorganize it. Like officials in West Haven, he has formed a labor-management committee to begin reclassifying jobs and renegotiating benefits to make the city's money go further for both taxpayers and employees. He is working with Mr. Bruhl's group to find executives to serve as consultants.
He has one powerful incentive for city workers to join him: Because of the city's property wealth, it receives less state aid per capita than all but the state's most affluent suburbs, and so almost every dollar it spends is its own. More than 83 percent of its revenue is derived from local taxes. Whatever the city accomplishes will be its own.
"We've made a relatively rapid transition from an industrial city to another economy," Mr. Malloy said. "The quality of our residential areas has been successfully defended. I think Stamford's upside potential is greater than its downside for the first time in 10 years.
"I call Stamford a city that works," he said, "and there aren't too many. I think we can get it right."

HEADLINE: Reporter's Notebook;
The Auction Aftermath: Was It Worth the Price?

BYLINE: By JAMES BARRON 

 BODY:
The television crews have packed up their equipment. The bidders who lost out in the volleying for ever-higher prices have gone home empty-handed. And the buyers who shelled out megabucks at the Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis estate auction at Sotheby's last week are facing the morning-after question: Did I pay too much?
After all, the sale was more about who had touched the merchandise than about who had made it. Few of the items on the block were prime examples of an artist's work or a furniture maker's craftsmanship. And many were acquired after Mrs. Onassis left the White House, a period presumably less significant historically than the thousand or so days between John F. Kennedy's inauguration and his assassination. 
"The value will not last," said Robert White of Annapolis, Md., who bid on 40 items to add to the 350,000 pieces of Kennedy memorabilia he already owns. He said he was "appalled" by the prices, which ranged from $2.6 million for a diamond ring to $453,500 for a rocking chair to $343,500 for a painting by the 17th-century English artist John Wootton.
But Chris Jussel, a consultant and host of the public-television program "The Antiques Road Show," said that the buyers would probably not define the value the way hard-headed investors would.
"Historically, people have always viewed what we call a name sale like that as carrying a premium of anywhere from a quarter to a third to sometimes 50 percent," he said. "Obviously with someone this famous, the premium was even higher."
Arnold Schwarzenegger, who is married to John F. Kennedy's niece Maria Shriver, paid the greatest premium: his $772,500 bid for a set of the late President's golf clubs was 858 times more than Sotheby's estimate. The session at which Mrs. Kennedy's fashion jewelry was sold brought the highest premium, Sotheby's said, with buyers paying $2.45 million, or 45 times the top estimate.
But was what the buyers got any good?
"There seems to be so much, I don't know, stuff: that's all it was, as opposed to antiques," said Thomas Devenish, an antiques dealer with a shop on Madison Avenue. "It was a load of rubbish."
For example, the Nuclear Test Ban Treaty desk, a Louis XV mahogany table that was sold for $1.43 million to a European museum that has not been identified. "As a piece of furniture, I don't think it was very much," Mr. Devenish said. "It's better than what's in someone's basement, I guess."
So was the auction just a highly publicized yard sale? Mr. Jussel said not. "She represented a type of person who wanted to live a certain way, surround herself with, no question, books, and certain types of things and drawings that were not the best things in the world, but they appealed to her," he said. "She was not collecting. She was furnishing her homes. Having the latest upholstery didn't matter to her as much as it does to some people. And it was nice. Speaks well for her."
Clearly, it was fashionable the first day or so. But for many New Yorkers the inflection in "Can you believe it?" did a sweet-to-sour transformation as the week continued. Water-cooler conversations went from nostalgia for the Kennedy years to comments on unreasonable extravagance. A flea market on 43d Street between Eighth and Ninth Avenues posted a sign over its used books, old clothes and secondhand cutlery that said, "All items never owned by Jackie O."
But in the high-pressure atmosphere, the Onassis auction was a turning point for the president and chief executive officer of Sotheby's, Diana D. Brooks, who had not previously conducted an evening sale in the house. She had confined herself to daytime sales, in which the stakes are lower.
Mrs. Brooks, 46, was a splash of color in Sotheby's gray-on-beige sales room. Before the auction, Sotheby's staff was told to wear black to the opening session. Mrs. Brooks was the exception: she wore an electric-blue satin suit that evening, a pink suit the next day and an off-white one trimmed with black the day after that.
Clearly, she relished the entire experience.
"I never want to get off the podium," she said after presiding over part of one session on Thursday. "I'm dreading having to go back to my real job."
On the subject of extravagance, is it any surprise that the auction included something that once belonged to Marie Antoinette?
Maybe the surprise is that the buyer said he got it for a song, even though the price was $105,000.
The item was a traveling "casket," a small leather trunk from about 1770. The buyer was J. B. Maas, an antiques dealer from London who said he thought its resale value would approach $250,000. "If a humidor is worth $575,000," he said, "this was cheap."
Mrs. Onassis was a lifelong bookworm as well as, in her 50's and 60's, a book editor. Some 48 copies of "The White House: An Historic Guide," published at her suggestion in 1962, went for $11,500. Nine volumes about the Kennedy family went for $5,750. Six books of French history, including one from 1890, sold for $5,175.
"I wonder if she actually read it?" said the author Ellen Gilchrist, whose 1984 novel "Victory Over Japan" was included in a group of 14 that sold for $8,050. "Wonder if it actually pleased her in some way?"
Ms. Gilchrist said she had never met Mrs. Onassis and did not know how she came to have a copy of the book. Neither did John Updike, whose "Pigeon Feathers and Other Stories" was sold in the same group.
"But I am delighted to be participating in the massive inflation of the Onassis auction," he said. "It has us all dumbfounded. And I am pleased to think of 'Pigeons' as being part of it."
Richard Whalen, who published a biography of John F. Kennedy's father, Joseph P. Kennedy, in 1964, knows how Mrs. Onassis got hold of his book. He sent it to her.
"When the book was first published I was told by a mutual friend that she had given a bound leather copy to Joseph Kennedy as a sort of Christmas gag," he said. "Apparently they had a good laugh about it. So I decided to send her a copy. I think I said something like, 'You've given your copy away, so I thought you might like another.' "
"If her kids want to sell it, more power to them," said Mr. Whalen. "I hope I can sell a copy of my book for $5,000 someday."


GRAPHIC: Perhaps the lesson to be learned from last week's touched-by-fame frenzy over Kennedy collectibles at Sotheby's is this: Don't throw out anything, including that tape measure (which fetched $48,875) and footstool ($33,350, stain included). Of course, the value of the items, some 1,200 lots that brought in $34.5 million, was in having been in the possession of one Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis. It was not the first time, of course, that famously owned objects drew handsome prices at auction. A pair of Buddy Holly's plastic eyeglasses, stamped "Japan" (as in "made in"), went for $45,100 in 1990 at Sotheby's. And John Lennon's psychedelic Rolls-Royce sold for TK in TK. A black top hat worn by Lincoln went for a mere $10,000 in 1979, but that was before the recent boom in collectibles. As the dust settled at Sotheby's, several New York notables were asked to select a possession that might attract attention on an auction block of the future.

When I got to Congress, they made this fuss about the hat instead of what was under it. I didn't know whether they wanted me to keep it on or take it off. Since I decided they wanted me to take it off, I've kept it on."

On occasion he would even have two pairs on top of his head. They were probably as unique as the salt and pepper shakers on the Kennedys' table."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL: NOTEBOOK;
The Padres Discover a New Winning Formula

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
For part of last week, the list of National League leaders showed Tony Gwynn and Wally Joyner as the top two hitters with averages in the high .400's, but they weren't even playing and the San Diego Padres kept winning. Are the Padres that good? "Earl Weaver said you have to have deep depth," said Kevin Towers, the team's rookie general manager, who then cited two examples of the Padres' deep depth.
Scott Livingstone played first base in Joyner's place last Monday and hit a second-inning home run, starting the Padres on the way to a 5-3 victory over Florida. Livingstone was unavailable the next night because he had returned home to be with his wife when she gave birth to their first child. So Archi Cianfrocco played first and rapped a run-scoring double in the second inning that ignited a 7-2 victory. 
 As helpful as Livingstone and Cianfrocco have been, no one expects them to lead the Padres in the National League West race. But it is apparent by now, four weeks into the season, that the Padres will be in the race. Suddenly, the team that not long ago staged fire sales to dump high-salaried stars has a solid collection of legitimate players.
Towers, 34, is the beneficiary of the groundwork laid by his predecessor, Randy Smith, and the different economic philosophy of the new owner, John Moores, and new club president, Larry Lucchino.
"I feel very fortunate coming in here now," Towers said, citing both reasons. "Ownership has allowed me to do some things that make my job a heck of a lot easier. A lot of the groundwork was set, too. The way I looked at this club reminded me of the '84 club. They went out and got a Garvey, a Gossage, a Nettles. We're hoping that Tewksbury and Joyner and Henderson will be the three pieces that help this team win."
The Padres signed Bob Tewksbury as a free agent, and the 35-year-old right-hander has won his first three decisions. Joyner, acquired in the Kansas City Royals' salary slash, leads the league in hitting (.466 entering last night's game) and on-base percentage (.581). Rickey Henderson, making his N.L. debut via free agency after 17 years in the other league, was hitting only .173 with 9 hits, but 18 walks helped raise his on-base percentage to .403.
Add those ingredients to Gwynn, third baseman Ken Caminiti, second baseman Jody Reed, center fielder Steve Finley, closer Trevor Hoffman and starting pitchers Andy Ashby and Joey Hamilton and the mix is volatile for the division's other teams. And as recent events showed, the Padres have put together a good bench, too, including Craig Shipley, who was hitting .448, and Marc Newfield, who has been invaluable moving between left field and right while hitting .333 and driving in 12 runs.
"The bench is one of the things we wanted to improve during the winter," said Lucchino, who began his baseball career with the Orioles. "We talk about deep depth all the time around here."
Henderson, suffering from a strained rib cage, has played sporadically since April 13. Gwynn, nursing an inflamed right heel, missed eight consecutive games before returning Friday night and raising his average to .474 (he doesn't have enough at-bats to qualify for the league leaders.) Throw in Joyner's bruised ribs, and Bruce Bochy's starting lineup had no more than one of those three players in any of the nine games (the Padres won seven) before Friday, when the three appeared together for the first time since April 13.
When Joyner is out of the lineup, the Padres miss more than his bat. "It's been a long time, probably since Garvey, that we had a strong defensive player at first," Towers said. "Bringing in Wally makes the others better defensively. When they throw in the vicinity, he's going to scoop."
In trading Bip Roberts for Joyner, the Padres created a need for speed at the start of the lineup. That need led them to Henderson. "In the National League," Towers said, "I think you have to have speed to have success. Now we have a catalyst."
Despite Henderson's slow start with the bat, he has made an impact, as he can do. "Every time you look, he's on base," the general manager said. "He finds ways to get on and he bothers pitchers. Greg Swindell threw 35 pitches to first on Rickey alone. John Burkett was pitching well, Rickey gets on base, he throws over to first eight, nine times. When he went back to throwing to the plate, he was erratic."
Towers, who was the Padres' scouting director before gaining a promotion last November, was Joyner's teammate on the Brigham Young University baseball team. As a minor league pitcher, he played against Caminiti. He also played with Bochy at Las Vegas in 1988 and was Bochy's pitching coach in Spokane, Wash., in 1989. "We won the Pacific Coast League championship in '88 and the Northwest League championship in '89," Towers said. "I told Boch maybe we have good karma. We can win here, too."

 
 
A Torrent of Runs
 If any holdouts remain from the view that expansion has drastically diluted major league pitching, it's time to become a believer.
This season's games have produced an average of 10.4 runs a game compared with 8.3 at the corresponding point of the schedule in 1992, the year before the majors expanded from 26 to 28 teams. That's a whopping 25 percent increase. Even more whopping is the 57 percent increase in home runs, from a 1.45 average to 2.27. "There are some pitchers here who are learning how to pitch," Twins Manager Tom Kelly said. "Some teams have a lot learning; some teams don't have as many."
Kelly was forced to sit through the debacle that was his team's 24-11 victory over Detroit last Wednesday, a day that produced a record 195 runs. That game followed by only five days the Texas Rangers' 26-7 romp over Baltimore. "I don't know why it happened to the Orioles, but I can see it happening to us and the Tigers and some other teams," Kelly said. "I guess if it can happen to the Orioles, it can happen to anybody. Some of us are learning."
Jesse Orosco, who gave up half of the 16 runs the Rangers scored in the eighth inning, entered the season with 14 years and 46 days of major league experience. The seven Tigers pitchers the Twins battered totaled 10 years and 44 days in the majors.

 
 
Power On, Power Off
 On the other hand, if the pitching is so weak and the ball so explosive, why hasn't anyone told the Royals? They had hit seven homers entering yesterday's games. Eight players had hit more, 10 the same.
The Dodgers' Mike Piazza and the Giants' Matt Williams haven't been clued in either. Piazza, who hit 92 home runs the previous three seasons, didn't hit his first of the season until the Dodgers' 18th game and had only three homers before the weekend. Williams, who hit 104 the past three seasons, also has only three. Kevin Elster of Texas, whose baseball life seemingly ended five years ago and was never a home run hitter when he had life, has hit four.
But for the really big numbers, how about 9 home runs for Henry Rodriguez of Montreal? Rodriguez, having matched his career high of 8 in 306 at-bats in 1994, then hit his 9th in his 56th at-bat of 1996.
When Cecil Fielder hit his ninth on April 16 in Detroit's 15th game, he had only four other hits on the season. It took him until the 22d game for his other hits to exceed his home run total, which remained at 9. And Jim Bullinger, a Chicago Cubs pitcher, has more home runs (2) than victories (1).

 
 
A Cannon of an Arm
 Lest anyone doubt that Albert Belle could have deliberately hit a photographer with a thrown baseball from the outfield, a feat baseball officials are investigating, it should be recalled that in 1993, Belle had 16 assists from the outfield, second most in the American League.
Oakland was the last team in the majors to record a save, but once they got their first, by Mike Mohler last Sunday, they reeled off three more in the next three games, by John Briscoe, Mohler and Jim Corsi.

LENGTH: 1540 words

HEADLINE: G.O.P. Is Preparing Budget Plan With Political Traps for Clinton

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 27

 BODY:
Bloodied but only partly bowed after a 16-month battle over the 1996 Federal budget, Republicans in Congress are finishing plans to shave at least an additional $600 billion from spending by 2002 and, they hope, to seize the political high ground on the deficit issue from President Clinton in this election year.
These new plans are outlined in a budget resolution that Republican leaders hope to issue as early as next week. The resolution, a six-year blueprint for Federal spending, is the first step toward writing a Republican version of the proposed budget for fiscal 1997 that Mr. Clinton sent to Capitol Hill last month. 
 But Republicans see it as more than that. In interviews, they said the document aims to shift the budget debate away from spending cuts in popular Federal programs, where Mr. Clinton has all but demonized the House and Senate, and back to the themes of lower taxes and smaller government. In particular, Congressional leaders hope to avoid refighting this spring's one-sided battle over spending on Presidential priorities like education and the environment by locking into place many of the spending levels that Mr. Clinton negotiated in the 1996 budget bill that he signed on Friday.
Instead, aides in the House and Senate said, the party is planning to rehash a number of fiscal battles that leaders believe they can win, mostly over tax cuts and spending on Federal benefit programs for health care and welfare.
"The challenge this year is not about numbers anymore because we've shifted the whole debate to the point where people recognize the numbers on both sides are in the ball park," one aide to House leaders said on Friday. "The challenge is over policies and over tax relief."
In those battles, the aides argued, Congress has an edge. The White House has already agreed that the budget should be balanced by 2002 and that popular entitlement programs must be restrained if that goal is to be met.
That, of course, is the Republican view. Many other experts say the advantage in the budget debate still goes to President Clinton, who has tried to take on the role of the protector of politically popular programs. Mr. Clinton "has played a weak hand brilliantly," said Robert Reischauer, the Brookings Institution scholar who stepped down last year as director of the Congressional Budget Office, in an interview this week. And Republicans have squandered many political opportunities.
As a practical matter, the budget maneuvers are more a political exercise than a fiscal one. Few expect Congress and the White House to agree in an election year on a compromise plan to restrain spending on benefit programs.
President Clinton's political strategy this year is to position himself as the voice of moderation and to portray Republicans as unreasonable partisan zealots.
In accord with that strategy, the President used his radio address today to challenge his opponents to negotiate with him to balance the budget instead of making the budget a partisan matter. Speaking directly to Republicans, he said: "If it is your political strategy to concoct a budget that you hope I will not sign, you ought to think again. If you torpedo these negotiations today, after so much progress has been made on a balanced budget, simply to create a campaign issue for later, the American people will see through that with their eyes closed."
But Republicans do want to use the budget debate to highlight the philosophical differences between themselves and the Democrats. This year, Congress will probably not send the President a single huge bill encompassing changes in all tax and benefit programs, as it did last year, giving him the opportunity to veto them all in one amorphous swoop.
Rather, House and Senate strategists said this week that Republicans were likely to send the President at least three bills, confronting him with narrower and potentially more difficult choices.
One bill would overhaul Medicare along the lines of proposals the Republicans offered last year to move the system more quickly toward managed-care programs and other cost-saving schemes. The party hopes to bolster its case with new studies showing that Medicare is in more serious financial trouble than had been forecast only a year ago.
A second bill, potentially more troublesome to Mr. Clinton, would wrap together major changes in Medicaid with a complete rewrite of the Federal welfare system. The President ran on a promise "to end welfare as we know it," but he has vetoed Republican proposals to change the system, saying they are too draconian.
And sometime this fall, Congress is expected to send Mr. Clinton a larger tax cut than he has advocated, giving him the election-season choice of endorsing Republican tax dogma or denying voters any tax relief at all.
Senator Bob Dole, the party's presumptive Presidential nominee, and House Speaker Newt Gingrich are already challenging the President to repeal the Federal gasoline tax of 4.3 cents per gallon, although they have not said how the Government would compensate for the loss of an estimated $4.8 billion in revenue.
Mr. Gingrich announced his support today for the gasoline-tax repeal, saying that as early as next week, Senator Dole would introduce a proposal to end the tax. He predicted wide support for the bill in Congress.
Republicans argue that the budget fight is unlikely to be as difficult, politically or fiscally, as in 1995, when Republicans were starting from scratch.
"We've changed the direction," one aide to the Congressional leadership said. "The plan for the next six years is no longer a plan that relies on that first dramatic step. Now it relies on continuing a trend that's already begun."
Republicans can afford to shelve the debate over how much to cut spending and can even accede to some of Mr. Clinton's priorities because they have a few billions to burn. Despite the Democrats' success in blocking many of their biggest restraints on Federal entitlement spending, Congress is still on track to meet its 1994 pledge to balance the budget by 2002 -- or even ahead of schedule.
Republicans have their own parsimony and a healthy dose of luck to thank for that.
The $160 billion spending bill that ended the 1996 budget wars earlier this week, for example, is widely regarded as a victory for the White House because the President forced Congress to add nearly $5 billion to his most prized programs.
But in the larger scheme, the Republicans appear to have the upper hand. The 1996 budget still shaved $20 billion from the domestic spending levels that were approved in 1995, not counting spending for entitlements, like Medicare and Medicaid, and other mandatory payments.
More important, Republican budget strategists calculate that they will save some $245 billion in domestic spending by 2002 -- and meet their deficit-reduction targets -- merely by capping overall domestic spending at 1996 levels.
In the battle over Federal spending, the White House also won a political victory by vetoing Congress's attempt to overhaul entitlements and save hundreds of billions of dollars in future spending.
Viewed on a balance sheet, however, the Republicans actually gained ground in their seven-year plan to erase the deficit. That is because the White House veto also blocked their seven-year, $270 billion tax cut, which would have gobbled up 40 percent of the entitlement savings this year and would have exceeded those savings in later years.
"If you have neither mandatory savings nor a tax cut, you're better off," Mr. Reischauer said. "So, in effect, inaction on this front improves the situation."
The Republican budget strategy has also benefited from two sizable revisions in economic forecasts by the Congressional Budget Office. Both lowered estimates of future deficits, thus reducing the amount of spending Congress must cut to reach fiscal balance.
The Republicans' last proposal to balance the budget, offered last winter in negotiations with the White House, envisioned cuts in projected spending of $660 billion over seven years. The latest economic forecast by the budget office, released last week, sliced that figure to something less than $600 billion, Republican budget strategists said this week.
None of that can be reduced to a campaign slogan or a 30-second television spot, the turf on which much of the next election will be contested. More broadly, however, the changes mean that Congress will have more leeway this summer to avoid the most unpopular spending cuts, and more freedom -- if it chooses -- to claim some of the middle ground staked out by Mr. Clinton on deficit reduction.
As happened last year, the House has advocated deeper and more partisan spending reductions, and larger tax cuts, than the Senate prefers, aides said this week. Still, one proposal suggests that the deficit could be erased by 2002 by shaving roughly $250 billion from projected domestic spending and $350 billion from mandatory programs like Medicare, which would be $50 billion to $75 billion less than last year.
That does not include the cost of a tax cut, which Senate strategists have pegged at about $100 billion and House experts have said would be somewhat higher.

HEADLINE: Plan to Regulate Cheese Has Quebec Up in Arms

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH 

DATELINE: TORONTO, April 26

 BODY:
In Quebec they are calling it an insult to the French palate and suggesting that it is just another way federalism fails the province. In the rest of Canada people are asking what all the fuss is about.
This time the confrontation between English- and French-speaking Canada is over cheese. 
 The federal health department in Ottawa wants to ban the sale of raw-milk cheese, like certain bries and camemberts that have long been a staple of French Quebecers' diets. Many Quebecers suspect an English plot against their distinctive eating habits.
Most cheese is made with heat-treated milk to get rid of micro-organisms like listeria monocytogenes that can survive in raw milk and cause severe illness. Once the cheese is made, regardless of the type of milk used, current Canadian regulations, like those in the United States, require that it be aged for at least 60 days at a temperature of at least 35 degrees Fahrenheit.
The proposed regulations would require the milk used for any cheese sold in Canada, whether domestic or imported, to be taken to a temperature of 147 degrees for 16 seconds as part of the cheese-making process.
Yet the raw milk imparts a distinct taste highly valued by many cheese-lovers. Along with bacteria, heat removes natural enzymes that influence the taste.
And Quebecers, anxious to preserve their French heritage, not only consume but produce more raw milk cheese than other Canadians.
After last October's narrow loss by separatists, another Quebec independence referendum may be still a few years away. But when the time comes, as Lysiane Gagnon, columnist at La Presse, noted darkly of the cheese issue, "it's this type of quarrel that will make the difference."
Pauline Picard, a member of Parliament from the Bloc Quebecois, raged against Health Minister David Dingwall during a recent House of Commons session in Ottawa.
"Since cheese made from raw milk is already strictly regulated in Canada and nobody is complaining," she asked, "why do the minister and his officials want to annoy us by prohibiting the sale of this cheese here?"
She read out a list of Quebecers opposed to the ban -- "producers, importers, retailers, restaurant owners, consumers, veterinarians and the provincial government."
Jean-Guy Chretien, the agricultural spokesman for the Bloc and no relation to Prime Minister Jean Chretien, warned that the ban could create a "fine-cheese black market," with traffickers smuggling brie like cocaine.
Still, Mr. Dingwall said he was pressing forward with the new regulations, a project of department scientists for the past six years, because of "increased risk of illness or disease" from the raw-milk cheese.
"I know the whole political spin in Quebec and I travel to Quebec a lot," he said. "And I happen to love the city of Montreal, and I happen to love the cheese which they're referring to. But you have to remember, I'm the Minister of Health with a very clear mandate, which is to protect the health of all Canadians."
Department officials stress that nothing is yet final. The public has until June 13 to comment. Then an advisory group will review options and make recommendations.
Many academics see the probability of serious harm from the cheese as remote.
"I was really surprised and astonished at the urgency of the situation as it was set forth by the ministry," said Dr. Jacques Goulet, professor at Laval University's Department of Food Science and Technology. "There is probably more danger coming from chicken and raw vegetables than cheese from raw milk."
Outside Quebec the debate has been followed with a certain detached amusement. Michael McDonald, director of the Center for Applied Ethics at the University of British Columbia, suggested that if the Government is truly worried, "it could post warnings in cheese shops or even require cigarette-style warning labels on brie."

HEADLINE: Policy Findings

 BODY:
To the Sports Editor:
A few years ago I participated in a debate about moving the Yankees from the Bronx to another site in New York City while I was a graduate student at the Wagner School of Public Policy at New York University. My research indicated these findings: 
 *Feasibility studies tend to favor the client's point of view.
*A new stadium costs taxpayers money -- always.
*Team owners, not local governments, make money. In fact, there is a question of large sums of money that the Yankees owed the city at the beginning of the Giuliani administration.
*Baseball largely provides a small number of seasonal minimum-wage jobs to the community after the construction phase.
*Ticket buyers are local residents who use disposable entertainment income, which, if not spent on baseball, would be spent locally on other entertainment and does not radically change the dollars flowing into the local economy.
Keeping the Yankees in New York is an emotional issue and an issue of tradition, and not an issue of increased revenues, except for Steinbrenner.
 
BETTY MAUCERI
New York
