HEADLINE: Inside the Meritocracy Machine

BYLINE: By Bruce Weber; Bruce Weber is a reporter for The New York Times.

 BODY:
THINGS HAVE CHANGED AT VAN Nuys High School since its most famous alumnus, Robert Redford, graduated in 1956. Indeed, inside the sprawling, campuslike complex of sandstone-colored buildings and temporary trailers, there is something futuristic about the place. With its earnestly beleaguered faculty, teeming hallways and stew pot of cultures, with its tension between classes and colors and between homeboys (and girls) and newcomers, it looks and feels the way public high schools everywhere in the country are likely to in the next century as our cities continue to expand into the suburbs.
Van Nuys is a pleasantly undistinguished community in the San Fernando Valley, north of Los Angeles, with large blocs of Hispanic and Asian residents. Local kids constitute about half of the 3,000-member student body at Van Nuys High, but as a "magnet" school it also draws high-achievers, many of them the children of recent immigrants, from all over the Los Angeles area who are interested in careers in medicine, mathematics and the performing arts. 
 As a result of the magnet programs, the school is not only diverse but also highly competitive -- the kind of place that top colleges refer to as a feeder, the source of many potential enrollees. There were 12 national merit finalists in Van Nuys High's senior class this year, and three seniors scored perfect 1,600's on their Scholastic Assessment Tests, the standard college admissions exam. So it is not surprising that 16 Van Nuys students applied to Harvard this year -- hoping to become members of the class of 2000 at the nation's most prestigious university. And it's not surprising that as decision day approached the applicants were going through a nervous-making time. On April 4, the last day of school before spring break, and the day before they would find out if they had got in, 4 of the 16 sat outside, between classes, trying to talk themselves out of wanting it so much.
Maybe it was the natural aversion to being judged, maybe teen-age insecurity, maybe an instinctive self-protection mechanism kicking in. (And then again, maybe it was the fact that they had all already been admitted to other colleges.) But for whatever reason, all four now seemed to think that acceptance to Harvard -- the school that had at some point been the first choice of each -- just didn't matter the way it once had.
"I applied because it was a childhood dream, to see if I could have gone," said Parham Yashar, who will graduate ninth in a class of 640 and who scored a perfect 800 on his math S.A.T. By April 4, though, he had already been accepted at three University of California schools, and he was leaning toward U.C. Berkeley; even if he got into Harvard, he said, he was having second thoughts about going so far away from home. Parham, a slightly built, serious young man who speaks Farsi (his parents are Iranian Jews), plans to be a doctor. Harvard, the name Harvard, still has the cachet, he said; it is still a symbol. But the actual school of his dreams, he said, "is not Harvard per se -- it's whatever school you want to go to."
"Harvard is like a generic term," Mira Lew said. "Like Kleenex."
Mira is a first-generation Korean-American -- her parents immigrated when she was 4. She ranks 32d in her class and had a combined S.A.T. score of 1,490. Editor of the school newspaper and captain of its city championship girls' volleyball team, she is tall and pretty and has a penchant for self-scrutiny. Last fall she was talking about Harvard rather dreamily, saying she frequently envisioned herself walking up the steps to Widener Library or hurrying through Harvard Square on the way to a hockey game. But she had applied to Harvard's early-action program and didn't get in. Her application was deferred until the spring.
"I took it a lot better than I thought I would," she said. "I thought I'd have a rotten Christmas vacation, but it wasn't so bad."
By April 4, she had shifted her perspective to the extent that she was wearing a Yale sweatshirt. She had already been turned down by Stanford but had been accepted at Berkeley, where her sister had gone in disappointment after being rejected by Harvard three years ago. Mira was waiting to hear from Harvard, Columbia and her current favorite, Yale.
"I never thought I'd have my choice," Mira said. "The process is just so brutal -- I saw my sister go through it. What's so maddening is that it's never set in stone. It seems so random."
Anna Fudacz ranks third in the class and earned the highest possible score on four of the five advanced placement exams she took. She speaks Mandarin Chinese, although there is no Chinese in her background; her family is of Polish extraction. She had already got into Brown and was thrilled by the cordiality and enthusiasm she felt from the school, neither of which, she said, she felt from Harvard.
"Brown sends me stuff, like, every day," she said. "And people who went there always say, 'I loved Brown.' Nobody ever seems to say that about Harvard. My parents are always telling me, 'Don't rule it out -- what if you get in?' But if I went there, it'd be for the reputation."
Maya Turre ranks 10th in the class. She is a fine enough musician to have been principal violinist in a community orchestra, and she was undefeated in 29 varsity tennis matches on a league championship team. Her combined S.A.T. score was 1,430, very strong but not dazzling: she was, in fact, one of only two Van Nuys seniors with a score under 1,500 to have been accepted at Stanford.
One possible explanation for that is her remarkable package of credentials; her ethnicity, however, may have been a factor. Her mother, whom she resembles, is black and Native American, her father Mexican and Italian. When asked her ethnicity on college applications, Maya answered African-American. As a group, accomplished black students are the targets of the most competitive recruitment.
"They usually ask for just one, and if I'm asked to choose, which I don't like to do, I choose black," Maya said. "It's the culture I most identify with."
Maya had already been accepted at M.I.T., where she said she would go. She hadn't yet heard from Yale. Harvard, at this point, was running third, she said, because it doesn't offer a major in architecture, which is what she plans to study. Asked what she thought about Harvard, she said, arching a skeptical eyebrow, "It has this reputation for greatness."
Indeed, its reputation continues to precede it. Admission to Harvard has never meant so much to so many. At a time when American parents seem palpably nervous about the nation they will bequeath to their children and when the mere fact of a college education has diminished in value as a ticket to success, the elite colleges -- the Harvards, Yales, Stanfords and Princetons -- look more desirable than ever. Harvard routinely receives applications from more high-school valedictorians (2,900 for the class of 2000) than it could admit even if it were admitting only valedictorians and this year received a record number of applicants (more than 18,000) for the 1,620 slots in next fall's freshman class.
Further, more than half of this class was filled in December through Harvard's early-action program, which meant that 16 out of 17 of those waiting to hear from Harvard this month were going to be rejected. No wonder Parham, Mira, Anna and Maya were finding reasons to reject Harvard first.
HARVARD, WHICH TURNS 360 THIS year, is the nation's oldest college and perhaps, as it is consistently deemed by U.S. News & World Report in its annual rankings, the nation's best, though that is an arguable and maybe moot point. What is undeniable, however, is that it is America's most selective. And as the number of applicants increases and the freshman classes stay the same size, it is growing more so.
This year fewer than 11 percent of applicants were admitted to the class of 2000, compared with 12 percent a year ago and 16 percent four years ago. The nation's other top colleges have also grown more competitive, but none more so than Harvard: Princeton accepted 14 percent of its applicants a year ago, Yale 20 percent, Stanford 20 percent and Columbia 25 percent. What these numbers reflect above all else is that those admitted to Harvard tend to go there, no matter where else they get in, which means Harvard has to admit fewer students than other schools to fill its allotted slots: each year 75 to 80 percent of the candidates offered admission to Harvard actually enroll, by far the highest "yield" in the country.
"It's fair to say that if it weren't for Harvard, everyone else's yield would be much higher," said William R. Fitzsimmons, Harvard's dean of admissions and financial aid since 1986.
In effect, Harvard gets the first pick of the nation's (and, increasingly, the world's) high-school graduates. More than any other university it gets to select its student body, making it the very definition of an elite institution. And what does that mean? Among other things, the necessity of perpetuating itself. Legacies -- applicants whose parents attended Harvard and generally support the institution financially -- are admitted at a rate of about 40 percent, some three times as high as other applicants.
"The institutional side is often undervalued in a discussion of admissions," said Neil L. Rudenstine, Harvard's president, who is currently overseeing a $2.1 billion capital campaign that will add 40 professorships to the faculty of arts and sciences, bolster the endowments of several of Harvard's schools and augment by $200 million the financial-aid fund of its college of arts and sciences. Rudenstine was not just talking about students and their families as potential donors but about how students might contribute to the college's reputation -- in academics, the arts, civil service. Harvard graduates 97 percent of its students, he pointed out, and 70 percent of undergraduates receive some kind of aid, totaling about $70 million annually, more than half of that in outright stipends. The school has to have great confidence in the students it admits, he said, "because we have a tremendous investment in them."
There are some 35 members of the official admissions committee who are involved in the final meetings on candidates; of these, 18 to 20 tend to be faculty members. The admissions committee also includes 15 or so junior officers. And unlike at some schools -- say, Princeton or Stanford, where the admissions deans sign off on all admittees -- at Harvard all final decisions are made with a full committee vote. So, during three weeks of meetings every March, as many as 50 people may take part in a discussion of a candidate for the freshman class, and each has the opportunity to raise a hand, yea or nay on the applicant's fate.
"We are in the business of choosing leaders for the next century," Fitzsimmons said. He acknowledges the hubris of such a claim. It is, he knows, an uncertain quest, to say the least: the Kennedys, yes, but also Theodore Kaczynski. And in a famous case of more recent vintage there was Gina Grant, the young woman whose admittance to Harvard last spring was rescinded when officials learned that she had killed her mother, gone to prison for it and kept that information from the admissions department. (Grant is now completing her freshman year at Tufts University.)
Fitzsimmons, 52, has been in Cambridge almost uninterrupted since his student days in the mid-1960's. He's an amiable hail-fellow in the preppy mode who looks as if he could be Kevin Costner's older brother but who grew up in a blue-collar suburb south of Boston and entered Harvard against the wishes of the nuns at his Catholic high school. He speaks about Harvard with the quiet fervor of a long-ago convert. Grandiose as "leaders for the next century" sounds, he said, what choice does Harvard have? As the demonstrated leader, it is obligated to be the standard-bearer. The standard may be impossible to uphold, but that's the game.
Indeed, in the words of the handbook that Harvard provides to the 5,000 or so alumni who conduct applicant interviews around the world, "selection is not an exact science." And anyway, it seems obvious that whatever standards Harvard declared would be challenged by whoever didn't meet them.
In determining future leaders, according to the handbook, the factors that carry the most weight with the admissions committee are the following: outstanding intellectual ability, unusual attractiveness of personality, outstanding capacity for leadership, creative ability, athletic ability, maturity and strength of motivation for a liberal education, Harvard and Radcliffe parentage and geographic, ethnic and economic distribution. Clearly, as guidelines, these do not exactly provide a decision-making algorithm.
And to complicate matters, the admissions process is need blind (that is, an applicant's financial resources are not a factor). Harvard employs no quotas, nor does it offer academic merit or athletic scholarships of any kind, inducements that many schools use to help satisfy special interest constituencies like the football coach or the chairman of the math department.
In addition, admissions officers say that the applicants grow more accomplished every year. This is partly because Harvard's outreach is becoming ever larger in its embrace, its recruitment more aggressive, and partly because kids (and their parents) start college preparation earlier and earlier. Maya Turre, for instance, took her S.A.T.'s for the first time in the seventh grade and spent one summer in a college-prep architecture program at the University of Southern California and another in a math and logic program at Johns Hopkins.
"It's a lot more of a marathon for these kids than it used to be," said Maya's father, Michael, a math teacher at Van Nuys High. "It's just become more competitive. And parents feel compelled to enroll their kids in these programs in order to get them noticed."
At the same time that the talent pool is growing larger and stronger, admissions officers say, some of the tools for distinguishing among applicants have become less reliable. This year the Educational Testing Service "recentered" its scoring for the S.A.T.'s; that is, it adapted its scoring system around a new, and lower, mean, which had the effect of raising scores in general and thus diluting the signal of intellectual strength traditionally sent by the highest scores. Admissions officers also say that recommendations written by high-school teachers and counselors are less helpful than they once were, largely because counselors are more overburdened than ever: for instance, Van Nuys High School has one college counselor for its roughly 650 seniors each year.
Another factor obscuring the genuine qualifications of applicants is the increasing prevalence of for-hire college counselors, who earn their fees by advising high-school students on how to best position themselves for the admissions process and provide services that may include selecting courses or editing and proofreading an application essay. Mira Lew, at the urging -- and expense -- of her father, has been working since her junior year with Betsy Frederick from a company called College Counseling Service.
"Betsy's my equalizer," Mira said, adding that she hadn't mentioned her private counselor to any of her friends "because the whole thing is way too competitive."
In general, said Harry R. Lewis, the dean of Harvard College, who has served on the admissions committee for 17 years, "I'm not sure the quality of information we're getting now is as good as it was 15 years ago." Given all this, it should come as no surprise -- though it does, somehow -- that admissions officers at Harvard rather easily acknowledge what applicants like Mira Lew fearfully suspect: however scrupulous the selection process may be, it remains, to a degree, random.
"Harvard could probably admit a whole other class," said Robin Worth, who has been an admissions officer for eight years. "Admit the class, put it aside and go ahead and admit a whole other one that would do extraordinarily well and go on to do fabulous things in life."
ON AN EVENING LAST MAY, FIVE TOP admissions officers from five of the nation's best colleges addressed an audience of some 400 personally invited high-school juniors and their parents in a nondescript hotel banquet hall outside Dallas. The title of their presentation was "Exploring College Options," and its purpose was to familiarize these local students with their five distinctly nonlocal schools -- Georgetown, Duke, Stanford, M.I.T. and Harvard. Harvard, which conducts early-awareness college programs in some junior high schools, might be said to have begun assembling next fall's freshman class five or six years ago, but the main work begins two years before a class enters. Admissions officers often travel to schools that have traditionally sent students to Harvard (one visited Van Nuys High around the time of the Dallas gathering), but the Dallas meeting was aimed at students who weren't exactly weaned on the notion of going to an elite private college a long way from home, and it was an evening of serious public relations.
It might seem counterintuitive that top schools need to recruit students, or market themselves, at all. But these schools are more than slightly aware that, particularly in regions where their institutions are not household names, they have snooty reputations. And that, Fitzsimmons acknowledges, is what the admissions-department public-relations work is designed to combat. (The powerful legend, based in considerable historic fact, is that Harvard takes the creme de la creme from Exeter, Andover, Choate and the like, and doesn't bother looking elsewhere.)
Anyone who doubts the power of good will in marketing colleges need only talk to the four Van Nuys seniors. It seems a little paradoxical that Harvard, a school that prides itself on its elite status, succeeds best in recruiting when it persuades applicants that it is a place full of ordinary folks like themselves.
Maya Turre visited the campus last summer and had a long conversation with Roger Banks, Harvard's director of minority recruitment. "Before that, I was having doubts," she said, "but he pretty much convinced me to apply. And I met a lot of students, and they weren't what you'd expect. I remember the guy behind the admissions desk had purple hair. I guess I was pleasantly surprised."
Parham Yashar said his interviewer, a woman who had recently graduated from Harvard Law School, told him that yes, there is some snobbery at Harvard but that it certainly isn't overwhelming; he found her candor persuasive. "Right away when I met her, I felt I could talk to her about anything," he said.
And Mira Lew said her encouraging phone conversation last fall with Jennifer Bates, Harvard's women's volleyball coach, made her feel, for the first time, that getting in was a realistic goal for her; her academic interview, with a Los Angeles businessman, heightened her enthusiasm.
"We really connected," she said. "His story was my story, a middle-class kid who needs financial aid to get through. He said he was really grateful to the school, and that impressed me. I was really high on it when I left."
On the other hand, it was a Harvard interview that soured Anna Fudacz on the place. "The guy was 75, probably, he had graduated from Harvard Business School in the 40's or something and he was very uptight," she said. "He kept asking me, 'So what's so special about you?' And I talked about how I was studying Mandarin Chinese. I thought that was pretty good, but he said: 'Oh, half the population of Harvard speaks Chinese. What else?' Maybe my attitude will change if I get in, but it seems more intimidating than the other schools."
Another paradox inherent in Harvard's marketing of itself is that it essentially makes admissions harder: if college admissions can be seen as a two-pronged process, recruitment and selection, the first part is actually designed to make the second part more difficult. Still, since the mid-1970's, when a decline in the birth rate foretold a declining high-school population, the pursuit of potential students has become increasingly aggressive everywhere. One result of this has been the so-called flight to excellence -- the widening gap between the 40 or 50 top institutions, where applications have been steadily rising for the past 15 years, and the vast majority of others, which continue to wage an annual battle to fill their class rosters.
This trend has dovetailed with emerging pressures on elite schools to diversify their student bodies. That, in fact, was what the five schools represented in Dallas were doing there: all five had discovered a wealth of students willing, in increasing percentages, to be lured away from fine state universities like the University of Texas and Texas A & M. The names of the students at the Dallas gathering had been provided by the College Board Search Service, which uses Preliminary S.A.T. scores and the students' self-reported grades to make up target lists for schools around the country. The College Board has been compiling these statistics annually since the early 1970's and has been multiplying and refining its lists to sell to the nation's colleges ever since.
To help recruit the class of 2000, Harvard asked the College Board for its national lists of African-American, Mexican-American, Asian-American, Native American, Puerto Rican and Hispanic-American high-school juniors whose P.S.A.T. scores had been at least 110 (out of 160) and whose grade-point averages were at least 3.5 (out of 4.0). Harvard identifies more than 21,000 minority students by this means every year, and they are all automatically sent application booklets and other direct solicitations, including invitations to events like the one in Dallas, which was distinctively rainbow-colored. By contrast, the five people running the Dallas show -- Fitzsimmons; Michael Behnke, the director of admissions at M.I.T.; Christoph Guttentag, admissions director at Duke; Charles A. Deacon, dean of admissions at Georgetown, and John Bunnel, Stanford's associate dean of admissions -- were all not only middle-aged white men but also dressed identically, in blue blazers, white shirts and gray slacks. "I want to assure you," Bunnel, as master of ceremonies, began, "that we do not represent the diversity on our staffs or at our schools."
The presentations themselves -- slide shows of the campuses with commentary -- were informative, amusing and designed to allay any suspicions the students and parents might have had about elite institutions in general and these five in particular. Fitzsimmons made sure to praise Harvard's underappreciated engineering program and its national championship math team. And he made the surprising point, to much murmuring, that without offering a single athletic scholarship Harvard fields more intercollegiate sports teams -- 41 -- than any college in the country.
In the end, the meeting -- like similar meetings in Austin, San Antonio and Houston -- accomplished what it was meant to accomplish: it put human faces on institutions that may be perceived as distant and forbidding. Even after a sobering discussion of finances -- the yearly cost of attending these schools is now close to $30,000 -- most people left seemingly satisfied and encouraged, and comparing the schools with one another rather than with, say, the University of Texas, which is precisely what Fitzsimmons and his colleagues desired.
LAST MONTH A FIFTH circuit Court of Appeals decision in a case involving four white applicants to the University of Texas Law School ruled against considering race or ethnicity in college admissions. The ruling does not apply to Massachusetts or to the selection of the class of 2000, but it sent a tremor through Byerly Hall in Cambridge, which houses the Harvard admissions office. Should the case go to the Supreme Court, and should the ruling be upheld and become national law, the effect on the admissions process at Harvard would be profound. Fitzsimmons would not comment directly on the case, but he pointed out Harvard's acute awareness that "an increasing proportion of our future leaders will be coming from the nonwhite population."
The Fifth Circuit ruling appears to conflict with the 1978 Supreme Court Bakke decision. In the pivotal opinion, Justice Lewis Powell wrote that setting aside specific places for racial minorities in college admissions is unconstitutional but that an applicant's race might reasonably be a factor in admissions; it was Harvard's policy that Justice Powell cited specifically as a model. Eighteen years later, asked point-blank if there were two applicants for the final open spot in a class, might race be a decisive factor, Fitzsimmons said: "It could. Yes." A new Supreme Court opinion, then, would require a new policy and, in fact, a substantial revision of institutional thinking.
Attracting minority students is such a current focus at Harvard that Rudenstine, Harvard's president, devoted his entire triennial report in February to a history of the school's efforts to diversify. And in his concluding section, "Admissions: The 1990's and Beyond," Rudenstine equated a future commitment to diversity with Harvard's historical commitment to excellence. "Such diversity is not an end in itself," Rudenstine wrote, "or a pleasant but dispensable accessory. It is the substance from which much human learning, understanding and wisdom derive."
Of course, Harvard has not always been so accommodating. It was not until 1870, 234 years after its founding, that the first black man graduated from Harvard, and dormitories were not integrated until 1917. There were no female students until 1879, when Radcliffe, Harvard's sister school, was founded. In the 1920's, when the percentage of Jews entering Harvard rose to more than 25 percent, A. Lawrence Lowell, then Harvard's president, declared that the university had "a Jewish problem" and proposed a ceiling-setting quota. And though the idea was thwarted by the faculty, Harvard's reputation as a place for elite white Protestants lingered for decades. Fitzsimmons recalls that when he was a student and hockey goalie at Archbishop Williams High School in Braintree, Mass., the first two nuns he approached for recommendations to Harvard wouldn't write them.
"They said it was Communist and atheist and a bunch of elite snobs, and that it was only for the rich," Fitzsimmons said. "I understood where they were coming from. There's a considerable amount of information that until the early 30's Harvard was anti-Semitic and anti-Catholic. What they were reacting to was what they had witnessed."
Since then, minority representation in general at Harvard has risen steadily, reaching 36 percent for the class of 1999. And virtually every individual group is now better represented than it was 15 years ago. As a historical correction this does not please everyone. Harvey Mansfield, a professor of government at Harvard since 1965, has declared that the increased black population on campus and what he perceives as an ill-advised leniency toward blacks and other minorities on the part of the faculty have been responsible for a widespread grade inflation. "I think Harvard has taken too many black or minority students relative to those whom they would have replaced," Mansfield said.
Interestingly, some of those who stand to gain from Harvard's diversity effort are also uncertain of its benefits: Maya Turre, for instance, whose blackness is a plus on her Harvard application but whose academic and extracurricular achievements can perhaps speak for themselves. Maya's parents simultaneously worry that her admission might be tainted by the specter of affirmative action and that Maya might be given an advantage that would better benefit someone else.
"I'm torn," said Maya's mother, Cheryl, an elementary-school teacher's assistant. "I'm in favor of affirmative action, but Maya doesn't need it."
Asian-American applicants like Mira Lew confront a different problem. From 1988 through 1990, the United States Department of Education investigated the Harvard admissions department to determine the veracity of allegations that over a 10-year period, beginning with the class of 1983, Harvard discriminated against Asian-American applicants, holding them to higher academic standards than white applicants and admitting them at a significantly lower rate. The investigation exonerated Harvard, but not without raising a different, thorny admissions issue. The reason for the discrepancy between the percentages of Asian-Americans and whites was found to be the acknowledged advantage, the "tip factor," given to children of alumni and to athletes, categories that don't favor Asian applicants. Could this be construed as simply a veil for racial bias?
The investigation also turned up evidence, albeit not in any quantity or pattern, that some admissions officers made comments on the applications of Asian-American candidates that were indicative of stereotyping, suggesting that Asians were being considered to be more in competition with one another than with the general pool. "He's quiet and, of course, wants to be a doctor," was one comment cited in the report. Another read, "Scores and application seem so typical of other Asian applications I've read; extraordinarily gifted in math with the opposite extreme in English."
This does not appear to reflect an institutional bias. Last year Asian-Americans made up 18.1 percent of Harvard's freshman class, making them by far the best represented of any minority group. (Blacks, who were next, constituted 9 percent of the class.) But in some ways this top-minority status has simply increased fears among Asian applicants that a ceiling has been reached, that they will not be treated individually and that their storied diligence in pursuit of excellence will be held against them.
In "College Success: Advice From Harvard Graduates," written by three Korean-Americans and aimed at Korean-American high-school students, the authors suggest that candidates try to fit themselves into categories -- athlete, scholar, musician, for example -- because admissions officers put candidates in such categories and compare applicants within them. The book also says: "Whether you like it or not, you will also be considered in the category 'Asian-American' consciously or subconsciously at some point in the admissions process. This poses a problem, however, as there is an overabundant supply of Asian-American applicants, and only a few can be considered 'the best' in this category. It is therefore imperative that you position yourself firmly in other 'categories' and emphasize your uniqueness."
Among those families using the book were the Lews. Kwang Lew, Mira's father, came to this country with his wife, Myoung, and two young daughters, to go to graduate school in electrical engineering at the University of Utah. He had planned to return to Korea but ended up staying in the United States so his children would not have to confront the seriously circumscribed educational opportunities in their native country. He and his wife, a college-educated former librarian, now own a hardware store in the Valley.
Three years ago Mira's older sister, Deanne, was valedictorian of her class at Van Nuys High but was rejected by Harvard.
"We were very disappointed," Kwang Lew said. "I can compare the schools to restaurants. The U.C. system is like a fast-food restaurant -- knowledge is like fast food. Harvard, Yale, these are fine restaurants, where you go in, they treat you nice, you know?"
Lew said that if the system here were the same as in Korea, where students are chosen by colleges solely on academic performance and the scores on a college admissions test, his daughter Deanne would be at Harvard today. "We prepared our second daughter differently," he said. Among other things, the family hired Betsy Fredericks to advise Mira and followed the advice in the "College Success" handbook. "We pushed Mira not just academically but into activities and sports," Lew said. "The Ivy League definitely needs Asian athletes."
Margot Hsu Carroll, who oversees Asian-American admissions at Harvard, acknowledges that the Asian tradition undervalues the well-roundedness that is more valued by America in general and Harvard in particular. "The message is getting out in the Asian-American community, slowly, but it's getting out," Carroll said, "that Harvard is looking for more than just high test scores and class rank."
THE APPLICATIONS BEGAN pouring into Byerly Hall last September, the mail gradually becoming more voluminous right up to the early-action deadline on Nov. 1. Early-action applications at Harvard have been on the rise for several years, and this year's total of 3,913 was nearly 1,000 more than last year's, which was itself a record. With 985 students admitted under early action, Maya, Mira, Parham and Anna were competing this spring with about 17,000 others for approximately 1,000 places.
Though the percentages would seem to indicate that students who apply early have a much better chance of being admitted, Fitzsimmons says this isn't true: the early applicants tend to come from the high end of the pool. And he says that his instructions to his staff at the early-action meetings are always the same: if you're sure a candidate would be admitted in March, vote to admit now; if you're at all uncertain, vote to defer. So though the odds are longer in the spring, the quality of the competition is not as stiff.
Fall or spring, the decision-making process is largely the same. It works this way: each applicant's folder, with all the pertinent scores, grades, recommendations, essays and interview reports, is read first by the admissions officer with responsibility for the geographic region where the applicant went to high school. These area reps, as they are known, are meant to be advocates for the students in their assigned areas. It's their job to work with counselors, teachers and administrators at the area schools, and to meet with as many students as possible, in an effort to get as many qualified applicants as possible into the pool. When the applications arrive, they don't exactly switch hats, but they begin to narrow down the field to those applicants they can fairly represent in committee meetings as viable Harvard candidates. So for the officers as well as for the candidates themselves, the process is a bit of a competition.
The area reps indicated that they are impressed by different qualities: one looks for good writing in the essay, another places emphasis on the character references, a third looks for clues that the candidate is ambitious and eager to get to Harvard. About one criterion they agreed: S.A.T. score. If the score means anything at all, it is only an indicator. A very low score, in particular (very low at Harvard being, say, a combined score of 1,000), is a red flag that the student is not academically prepared for Harvard course work, but is not a disqualifier. A high score, particularly with "recentering," doesn't mean much either. This year 9,400 applicants scored higher than 1,400; nearly 1,600 applicants scored a perfect 800 on the verbal section of the test; more than 1,900 had perfect scores on the math section. The school did not admit 165 applicants with double 800's.
Grades are important, and so is a consistently ambitious course selection, though most of the officers say that the strength of the high school is crucial. And most officers say that they are also impressed by a range of pursuits that show up in extracurricular activities. They look for focused excellence -- a Westinghouse national science competition winner, for example. But they also look for energy and a variety of interests, the kind of kid who, say, works 20 hours a week in a hospital, plays sousaphone in the marching band, edits the school's French magazine and organized the local chapter of Act Up.
Area reps have the authority to identify an applicant whose folder does not impress by entering the candidate directly into the regional computer file known as a docket without a second reading. This is called "coding out," and though it's not a disqualification, it is a signal that the application will need bolstering if the candidate is to be considered seriously. The area reps wield this power to varying degrees. Brooke Earley, a young woman in her second year on the committee who represents Georgia, the Carolinas, Kentucky and Wisconsin and parts of Massachusetts, said she coded out maybe 40 of 220 early-action applicants this year. Margot Hsu Carroll, who represents San Francisco and some of California's northern counties, along with Delaware, Maryland, Rhode Island and parts of Massachusetts, said she reads between 800 and 900 folders a year. "Maybe half the ones I read will not get a second reading," she said.
Most applicants get a second reading from another admissions officer and then a third from a senior member of the committee. Many even get a fourth from a reader with a specialized outlook -- a math professor reading to determine whether a candidate is a truly outstanding mathematician or merely very good, for example, or a Hispanic committee member reading with particular sensitivity to English-as-a-second-language candidates.
At each step the applicants are graded, 1 to 6 (1 being extraordinary, 6 preposterously underqualified, both being rare), in four areas -- academics, extracurriculars, athletics and personal characteristics -- besides being given an overall grade. The numbers are meant to be informative to subsequent readers; a candidate's "official" grades, before the folder comes up for public discussion, are assigned by the senior reader.
"Most folders will be read as 2's, 3's, 4's," Fitzsimmons said, adding that though more 2's than 4's will be admitted, there will be 2's that won't and 4's that will.
From this point on the process becomes something of a spirited debate, with cases being considered in an open forum -- area reps speaking up for their favored candidates and questioned, like lawyers before a tribunal, by their colleagues. Because there are fewer applicants during the early-action process, all the cases are heard by the full committee in meetings that go on for two or three weeks in the fall. For regular admission, however, the thousands of cases are divided among 21 subcommittees, each handling a docket. In subcommittees cases may be dispensed with in a quick vote, though any given folder may lead to discussion lasting an hour or more. In the end, each subcommittee, operating with a general target number of admits, comes up with a list of recommended admits, marginal admits and marginal rejections. The idea is to enter the final phase of the process with about 1,900 tentatively admitted applicants, including those from early action -- a very rough sketch of the class. That way, when the full committee begins its intensive discussions, members can begin to imagine particular candidates in the context of a whole class and the class in the context of the entire student body.
However, the full committee goes over every applicant, person by person, school by school. Most cases are dispatched quickly, but all of the tentative admits are rescrutinized, and Fitzsimmons says that maybe 1,000 or more others get serious consideration as well. "There is a lot of discussion on the margins," he said, adding that the number of tentative admits that in the end don't get in is in the hundreds.
In sum, at least in theory, the entire process comes down to each applicant against the rest, a year's worth of high-school students poured into a sifter to see who filters through. It turns out, of course, that there are annual anomalies: the high school that one year had no admits out of 35 applications and the next 9 out of 20; a sudden dip in the number of black male admits; the peculiar fact of seven sets of twins in one class.
Fitzsimmons shrugged: accidents. "I know it sounds corny," he said, "but we really do treat each application individually."
Harvard would not allow a reporter to sit in on committee meetings, citing, at various times, the confidentiality of the process, an unwillingness to set a precedent and a fear that an outsider's presence might affect the decision makers. In any case, the final two days of this year's meetings brought 79 candidates still in the running for 15 places.
"By this time, we really knew the cases incredibly well, and we would have been happy with any of these kids," Fitzsimmons said. "What people had to think about, really think within their hearts, was who of these kids had really earned it, who would make the biggest difference here and then the imponderable one, which of them would make the biggest difference with their lives? Obviously there are no clear-cut answers."
Late in the afternoon on Saturday, March 23, the committee took a final vote, with each of the 40 or so people in the room making 15 choices. The top 15 vote getters were in. As it turned out, it took a minimum of 13 votes to gain admission, and the final three admittees had 13 on the nose.
Two candidates received 10 votes. Two had 11. One had 12.
HARVARD HAS OFFERED admission to the class of 2000 to a total of 1,985 students, including 901 women, 325 Asian-Americans, 187 African-Americans and 169 Hispanic-Americans. About two-thirds came from public high schools. Of the 16 applicants from Van Nuys High, 3 were admitted and 2 placed on the waiting list.
Parham Yashar and Anna Fudacz took their rejections with cheerful good grace, though in conversations on the day they found out, both retreated from the rejections of Harvard they had expressed the day before.
"I feel fine," Anna said. "But I guess if I'd gotten in I'd have considered it."
And Parham, suddenly not so uneasy about traveling far from home, said, well, he hadn't really expected to be admitted, and, anyway, he still hadn't heard from Rice University in Houston, so there was still hope.
To ease the anxiety, Mira Lew had made several wagers. "If I get into Yale, my friend has to shave stripes into his hair," she said. "If not, I have to paint skunk stripes in mine."
Mira went to the mailbox in front of her home in Northridge at about 1 P.M. on April 5. Peeking in, she said, "Uh-oh."
There were two thin letters, the traditional harbingers of doom. From Harvard, a cordial, apologetic note, signed by Fitzsimmons and explaining, in straightforward, not-quite-condescending language, that the competition for places in the class had been unprecedented. But in fact, the news wasn't all bad. Yale's brief missive said she had been placed on the waiting list.
"So I guess there's still hope," she said. And further, Columbia had sent a fat packet, full of congratulations and an explanation of its generous financial-aid offer.
Inside the house, she stared at the letters, one after another. She was fighting back tears, but it was hard to discern what was upsetting her. Perhaps the rejection, perhaps the waiting list, perhaps that after two years of preparation for this moment, the moment itself offered no clear-cut way to feel.
"I'm still in limbo," she said.
On Sunday, April 7, Maya Turre sat in the living room of her home in Reseda, appearing to be very unsettled for someone who had been admitted to nine of the finest colleges in the country. Harvard had said yes. (On the letter, under his signature, Fitzsimmons had written, "Hope you will join us.") So had Yale, Princeton, Columbia, Stanford, the University of Chicago, the University of California at Berkeley, the University of Southern California and, her avowed first choice, M.I.T.
"I'm confused now," she said. "I'm having second thoughts."
Maya sat on the couch, her posture erect, her composed expression occasionally infiltrated by a shy smile. But her naturally implacable demeanor was belied by her hands, which seemed to be nervously washing themselves.
"What if I choose the wrong school?" she said. What was shaking her was her acceptance to Harvard. So what if Harvard doesn't have an undergraduate architecture major? She could devise her own curriculum, right? She could take courses in the graduate program in design. All throughout the week following her admission, Maya was on the phone -- to architects, the Harvard design-school faculty, her contacts at U.S.C. -- asking questions, soliciting opinions, asking advice, fishing for the factor that would push her one way or the other.
Reached by phone late last week, she said, "Well, I'm leaning toward Harvard."

HEADLINE: Acres and Acres of Color

BYLINE: BY CHRISTINE S. COZZENS; CHRISTINE S. COZZENS teaches writing at Agnes Scott College, Decatur, Ga.

 BODY:
POISED where the piedmont meets the coastal plains, the pine-forested mountains of west central Georgia, formed from resilient Hollis quartzite, are scored by dramatic ravines and streaked with pink and white dogwoods in the spring; nearby farmlands, once fleecy with cotton, now are host to the full range of botanical specimens from the southeastern United States and an ever-blooming palette. This swatch of land is sometimes called the Chattahoochee Trace after the river that wanders down from the state's northern mountains to form the border with Alabama. It embraces 200 miles of scenic country roads and some of Georgia's most carefully preserved landscape and history -- all because of a rare native plant called the plumleaf azalea and a hot springs that simmers at 88 degrees year round.
In the 1930's, Cason Callaway, a textile magnate from nearby La Grange, and his wife, Virginia, were exploring land they had purchased near Blue Springs at Pine Mountain, a steeply ridged formation that makes up the last spine of the Appalachian range. When the two discovered a brilliant reddish orange azalea, Virginia identified it as the prunifloria, or plumleaf, variety that grows only within a hundred-mile radius of the spot, and Callaway vowed to build a magnificent garden to reclaim the region's worn-out cotton fields and preserve its flora for the public to enjoy. 
 Today, Callaway Gardens covers more than 14,000 acres, including a 2,500-acre woodland garden; paths for walking, bicycling and driving around a complex of lakes; greenhouses that merge the indoor environment with the natural landscape outside, resort and recreational facilities; historical exhibits, and the country's largest free-flying butterfly conservatory. The nonprofit Ida Cason Callaway Foundation, headed by the Callaways' son, Howard H. Callaway, owns and manages the gardens, an adjacent U.S. natural landmark forest of 8,000 acres, and the for-profit resort and recreational properties. The foundation uses the proceeds from the resort and recreational properties to maintain the gardens and forests. More than a million visitors come to the gardens each year, so I avoid weekend visits at the busiest times -- azalea season and the Fantasy in Lights festival in December -- or take my bicycle to bypass the traffic.
A few years before the Callaways made their discovery, Franklin Delano Roosevelt had come to the region seeking a cure for his polio. Near the town of Warm Springs, on the northern slope of Pine Mountain, he built the vacation home soon known as the Little White House and helped design the state park that bears his name -- 10,000 acres of land between Warm Springs and Callaway Gardens. Roosevelt, architect of the New Deal, and Callaway, a fervent Republican, put politics second to their love for the Pine Mountain area and became friends, united in their efforts to cultivate and conserve its beauty.
Opened to the public in 1952 after years of careful re-establishment of the natural habitat, a policy that continues today, Callaway Gardens can be sampled in a day or savored over a longer period. To appreciate the size and stunning variety of the gardens, I like to begin my visit with a trip by car or tram along roads that wind in and out of the forests and lakes. Visitors can also explore the six footpaths -- Wildflower, Azalea, Holly, Rhododendrom, Laurel Springs and Mountain Creek Lake trails, -- and a 7.5-mile bicycle trail for a closer look at the collections of native plants.
Spring months offer the most varied array of blooms, with banks of azaleas, dogwoods and other flowering trees framing almost every vista; the plumleaf brightens the forest floor in July and August; the woodlands ripen into color in the fall, and formal gardens and greenhouses put on an evolving display of horticultural virtuosity with beds of flowers, vegetables and herbs throughout the year.
THE John A. Sibley Horticultural Center at one end of Mountain Creek Lake is one of the garden's two unusual hothouse displays. When I visited the center this month, its high-ceilinged chambers were filled with pink, white and blue hydrangeas, lilies and orchids of every shade and shape, and beds of daffodils, primroses, pansies, English ivy and bromeliads. A two-story waterfall feeds the system of pools that flows through the greenhouse and out into the yard, and the enormous wood- and glass-paneled doors open onto lawns and gardens, where local orchestras give weekly concerts in fine weather and where the plantings continue the seasonal themes begun inside.
Not far from the Sibley Center another greenhouse, in the Cecil B. Day Butterfly Center, more than a thousand butterflies (20 to 50 species at a time) flutter freely around a tiered arrangement of plants -- lantana, shrimp plant, heather, antherium and a collection of bromeliads, ferns and orchids -- and a waterfall. The center is also home to hummingbirds, mandarin ducks and bleeding heart doves. Visitors to this 8,000-square-foot conservatory may find a zebra longwing, a cloudless sulphur or a tiger swallowtail landing on their sleeves. Exhibits of mounted specimens, 19th-century watercolors by the Chevalier de Freminville and illustrations by the Georgia naturalist John Abbot, a film and cases of live chrysalises hanging from racks like so many jewels illustrate the different stages of butterfly development. The butterfly garden around the center is planted to attract more than 70 local varieties.
Adjacent to the Day Center, Mr. Cason's Vegetable Garden, southern site of the PBS program "Victory Garden," is characterized by usefulness and artistry. Framed by neat hedges, the seven and a half acres of orderly flower and vegetable beds demonstrate seasonal gardening using the latest techniques; there are always gardeners to talk to, ideas being tested and seedlings for sale. A year-round herb garden masses fragrant banks of rosemary, oregano, mint and sage with seasonal flowers.
From Callaway Gardens, it is a 16-mile drive over Pine Mountain to the wild, dusky ravines where, in 1924, Roosevelt sought relief at the hot springs in nearby Bullochville, later renamed Warm Springs. He returned to Warm Springs often, staying at cottages near the pools, and in 1927 he arranged for the renovation of the hot springs facilities, eventually investing two-thirds of his fortune to establish the Roosevelt Warm Springs Foundation for the treatment of polio patients. By 1932, the year he became President, Roosevelt's six-room white frame cabin around the corner from the rehabilitation center had been completed.
Today, the Little White House, with its four slender columns and graceful portico, is preserved exactly as it was on April 12, 1945, when the fourth-term President died there while sitting for his portrait. The simplicity of this Presidential retreat, the only house Roosevelt built and furnished for himself, bespeaks its Depression Era origins.
Furnished almost entirely with the plain but well-designed wooden furniture manufactured in the Val-Kill shops -- unemployment relief factories Eleanor Roosevelt established in New York -- each of the six rooms and outbuildings has a built-in audiotape explaining its history and artifacts, and an attendant is always on hand to tell anecdotes and answer questions, most of which concern Lucy Mercer Rutherfurd, Roosevelt's longtime companion who was with him when he died. Though one of the three small, sparsely furnished rooms is called Mrs. Roosevelt's Bedroom, the First Lady rarely visited Warm Springs.
A kitchen, an entryway and a central living and dining room make up the rest of the cabin; the veranda stretches across the entire back of the house and overlooks the deep ravine with its tall trees, distant horizons and glimpses of flowering underbrush. The unfinished painting still standing on an easel in the living room captures the strain of the war years and the President's failing health. Shortly after Roosevelt was pronounced dead, the woman who had been his cook since 1929 wrote in pencil on the kitchen wall, "Daisy Bonner cook the first meal in this house for the President Roosevelt and the last"; this quiet tribute is preserved under glass.
Across from the house are the two guest cottages, where national and world leaders sometimes stayed. The complex also includes the old sentry house, a neighbor's cottage and the servant's quarters and garage housing the President's specially built, manually controlled Ford convertible.
Up the hill to the left of the Bump Gate, a wide gate Roosevelt designed to open and swing back into place when nudged by a car bumper, is the Georgia Mustian Wilkins cottage, which tells the story of the Warm Springs years and of Roosevelt's struggle with polio. The cottage is filled with documents, photographs and memorabilia, including the President's saddle and bridle, his leg braces and wheelchair. A film comprised of home movies taken at the cabin and the rehabilitation center includes rare footage of Roosevelt in a bathing suit wearing leg braces or in his wheelchair, showing that he comfortably revealed his disability to the friends, family and fellow polio survivors who surrounded him at Warm Springs.
The unassuming approach to the Little White House through a grove of pines is landscaped just enough to provide a pathway and to merge the small lawn and planted terraces with the wooded setting in keeping with Roosevelt's conservationist views.
All during the Warm Springs years he interested himself in reclamation projects including agricultural experimentation and extensive tree planting in the valleys surrounding Pine Mountain. It is said that except during wartime exigency, he never had a tree cut down if he could help it, and the Georgia Department of Natural Resources, which manages the Little White House and the Pools and Springs Area, still respects his wishes.
The road between Warm Springs and Callaway Gardens, State Highway 190, cuts through Franklin Delano Roosevelt State Park, following the crest of the last ridge in the piedmont region and affording spectacular views of the lakes, forests and farmlands on both sides, especially at Dowdell's Knob, the rocky outcropping that was Roosevelt's favorite picnic spot, where the stone grill he and his guests used is still standing.
ROOSEVELT STATE PARK, one of Georgia's largest recreational facilities, offers a web of hiking trails, cabin and tent sites, horseback riding by the hour or day, and fishing in two manmade lakes. Built from local materials by the New Deal's Civilian Conservation Corps, the park's lodge, stone swimming pool and log cabins reflect the rustic style of the early conservation movement.
State Highway 190 leads down into the little town of Warm Springs, where the 19th- and early 20th-century storefronts, raised sidewalks and train depot have been restored to recall the Roosevelt era. And just outside town, the hot springs and pools that Roosevelt made famous, fallen into disrepair in the last 20 years as the institute moved away from hydrotherapy, were opened for public viewing last year.
Whether I begin my trip on the northeastern side of Pine Mountain, at the Little White House and Warm Springs, or at the southwestern end, I am often drawn in the opposite direction later that afternoon, as if the region's two moods -- the serenity of the forest and the riot of color and foliage in the garden -- made the day whole.
 
Guide to the sights of western Georgia
 
Getting There
Take I-85 south from Atlanta to I-185 (Exit 8) south. At Exit 14 on 185 (Pine Mountain), turn left on U.S. Highway 27 and go 12 miles to Callaway Gardens. For the return trip from the Little White House and Warm Springs, take 27 north through 30 miles of pleasant countryside until it joins I-85 north.
 
The Gardens and Resort
A day pass to the gardens, which includes admission to all attractions, costs from $15 to $20 a car (up to 9 people) depending on the season. Bicycles for use in the gardens may be rented for $5 an hour or $20 a day at the Bike Barn near the beach entrance. The gardens' opening hours and special programs also vary according to the time of year. For information on fees, hours, special attractions and reservations, call (800) 225-5292.
The accommodations at the gardens are well-situated for touring the area as well as Roosevelt State Park, Warm Springs and the Little White House, and a wider region from La Grange to Columbus.
Callaway Gardens Inn is a 349-bedroom luxury motel across from the gardens' main entrance. The inn has an outdoor pool, tennis courts, two pleasant restaurants (the Plantation Room and the Georgia Room) and conference facilities. Accommodation for two people costs from $89 a night. There are also 155 two-bedroom cottages (from $165 a night) and 49 larger villas (from $195 a night) -- all fully equipped. Resort facilities include four golf courses, fishing, tennis and racquetball.
For information on other motels or bed and breakfasts nearby, call the Pine Mountain Tourism Association at (706) 663-4000 or (800) 441-3502.
Inside Callaway Gardens the Verandah and Gardens Restaurants serve lunch and dinner with a view of the lake and grounds ($6 to $25). A more casual restaurant and a great place for children, the Country Kitchen, is at the Callaway Country Store on Route 27, where patrons may purchase a variety of country-style foods such as Vidalia onion dressing, scuppernong jelly, speckled heart grits and old-fashioned candy. Call (800) 280-7524 for mail orders.
Near the store, the 23-mile Pine Mountain Trail begins at one of the entrances to Franklin Delano Roosevelt State Park and ends at the WASP-TV tower near Warm Springs. Opened in 1975, a communal project of hikers and conservationists in the area, this trail takes hikers to Cascade Falls, Buzzard Roost and Sunset Rock, nine campsites and samples of all the region's native plants. Maps of the trail may be purchased at most local shops.
 
Rossevelt State Park
The park is on Georgia Route 190 between Pine Mountain and Warm Springs; (706) 663-4858. In the summer months and on pleasant fall weekends, it is necessary to reserve campsites and cabins. Twenty-one cabins, most with screened porches and all with air-conditioning, at $50 to $80 a week for up to eight people, are arranged around the lake, as are some of the car-camping and RV sites, at $12 a night. The central reservations number for all Georgia state parks is (800) 864-7275. In addition to swimming in the Liberty-Bell-shaped pool (Roosevelt's idea), the park offers fishing in two lakes, 30 miles of hiking trails and horseback riding on 18 miles of trail at the F.D.R. Stables on King's Road; (706) 628-4533. A one-hour ride costs $18 and an overnight ride costs from $60.
 
Warm Springs
Admission to the Little White House and grounds is $4, $2 for children 6 to 18; the site is open from 9 A.M. to 5 P.M. daily except Thanksgiving, Christmas and New Year's Day; (706) 655-5870. There is a small snack bar and gift shop at the entrance gate. The Pools and Springs Area on Alternate Route 27 is open the same hours as the Little White House; there is no entrance fee.
The area is full of small motels and bed-and-breakfasts; call the Meriwether County Chamber of Commerce at (706) 655-2558 or the Warm Springs Welcome Center in the depot on Broad Street at (706) 655-3322 or (800) 337-1927. The 13-room Hotel Warm Springs, (706) 655-2114, on the town's main street is the most convenient, historic and pleasant place to stay. Accommodating dignitaries, government agents and the press during the Roosevelt era, the hotel has recently been restored to its 1940's elegance, including Roosevelt memorabilia, the original switchboard and the Presidential Suite with original Val-Kill oak furniture. Bed-and-breakfasts start at $60 a person. C.S.C.

HEADLINE: Upheaval and Calm as Big Dig Transforms Boston

BYLINE: By SARA RIMER 

DATELINE: BOSTON, April 27

 BODY:
Its construction has been likened by people here to that of the Pyramids, the Hoover Dam and the Panama Canal. It has been called "a wonder of the world," by the United States Transportation Secretary.
The Big Dig, as everyone here calls the eight miles of tunnels and highways being built through the heart of Boston, is the largest public works project in America and the most expensive stretch of highway. 
 But what is perhaps most remarkable about the $10 billion project are the 20 years of political engineering and social planning that went into it.
Boston has had a long and bitter experience with the old method of highway construction that ravaged cities and bulldozed houses, businesses and neighborhoods. The Big Dig was designed to repair past damage, and reconnect communities that had been cut off from one another by asphalt, concrete and cars.
The Channel Tunnel between France and England was as ambitious, said the Massachusetts Transportation Secretary, James Kerasiotes, who is overseeing the Big Dig.
But that was built under water. "They weren't building in anyone's neighborhood," Mr. Kerasiotes said.
In Boston, they are building in a lot of people's neighborhoods: From East Boston to Charlestown, from Chinatown to South Boston, from the South End to the North End. Not a single house has been taken, not a single family displaced. Five years after ground was broken, the heaviest construction is going on in congested downtown. Boston is going on around the Big Dig.
Under the plan, in which work is to be completed in 2004, Boston would be transformed. An eight-lane underground highway, much of it covered by parkland, will replace the obsolete, traffic-clogged elevated highway -- the Central Artery -- that cut an ugly swath through downtown when it was built in the 1950's. Boston will regain access to its waterfront, and the North End will no longer be separated from the rest of the city by the artery.
One part of the Big Dig -- the Ted Williams Tunnel under Boston Harbor to Logan Airport -- was opened to commercial traffic in December, and eventually it will be linked to the Massachusetts Turnpike. The project also includes public-transit improvements.
The man who conceived the project, and built the bipartisan coalition that made it a reality, is a bricklayer's son whose philosophy about cities and highways was forged when his grandmother's house was bulldozed for the Massachusetts Turnpike in 1959. An engineer and state Transportation Secretary under former Gov. Michael Dukakis, Fred Salvucci helped stop a highway that would have demolished thousands of houses and businesses 20 years ago.
"Highways don't have to come at the expense of neighborhoods," Mr. Salvucci said the other day, as he drove under the Artery. "When they built the Artery, they thought the city was bad. They thought the suburbs were the future. I hated what the Artery did to the city."
While the project was designed to preserve communities, it is impossible for it not to disrupt life in the city.
The financial district is a maze of reconfigured streets, construction barriers and heavy equipment -- cranes, trucks, backhoes and pile drivers. And peak construction is still two years off.
Traffic is flowing -- as much as it ever flows in historic Boston, with its narrow, twisting streets that started out as cow paths in the 1600's. The usual crowds of tourists have been strolling along the Freedom Trail this spring. Aside from one small restaurant in the North End that closed last month, people are not going out of business -- so far.
With the street torn up in front of Les Zygomates, a French bistro at the edge of downtown, Lorenzo Savona, the co-owner, has been mailing customers parking maps. "They come through snow, sleet, hail -- and dump trucks blocking the street," Mr. Savona said.
"I am awed," said Darlene Bezdegian, a heavy-equipment operator. "There are people everywhere, and equipment everywhere, and life goes on."
An army of union laborers was on the ground in the financial district the other day, excavating dirt, and building walls in preparation for the tunnel beneath the Artery. Many of them come to work with cameras -- "so when it's finished you'll have memories," said Mary Flaherty, a construction worker.
There were men and women crawling with shovels through holes 25 feet underground. They are moving a total of 10,000 miles of underground utility lines -- telephone, television, cable, gas, water, sewer -- to make way for the tunnel. The project will use enough concrete to build a sidewalk from Boston to San Francisco and back three times, enough steel for a one-inch bar that would reach around the Equator.
The project's deputy director, William Flynn, is a retired Army general. During the Persian Gulf war, he directed the movement of 70,000 troops and tanks from Europe to the gulf. "That was easier than this baby here," he said during a break.
One wrong move, and the power could go out in, say, the Federal Reserve Bank. The workers are, literally, digging up the old city -- excavating clay, sand, boulders, wooden piers, all of it used to fill in the harbor when the city was expanded in the 1800's.
To insulate people in the modern city, barricades block work sites. Sound walls muffle noise. Trucks are washed frequently to cut down on dirt. Even fish in the harbor have been protected, with underwater sound waves used to warn migrating herring and alewife away from blasting sites.
The Big Dig is going forward at a time when the Republican-controlled Congress has little taste for such massive urban projects.
What has helped the Big Dig overcome previous political roadblockswas the unlikely coalition that Fred Salvucci built. He brought together business and labor, environmentalists and automobile lovers, Republicans and Democrats, neighborhood leaders and politicians, all committed to the idea that the city's economy depend upon the project's relieving crippling traffic congestion.
In East Boston, though, many people hated the project when it was first proposed 20 years ago because the tunnel to Logan Airport was to have gone through their neighborhood. "We said 'another tunnel? No way,' " recalled one resident, John Vitagliano, whose grandfather's house, and grocery store, had been taken for the Sumner Tunnel across the harbor.
But Mr. Salvucci, who had led a successful fight in the 1960's to prevent further airport expansion, managed to win people's trust during the 1980's, when he was Transportation Secretary, with his mumbling, self-effacing manner.
"Fred went into people's homes and talked to them," Mr. Vitagliano said. "His suits were chronically mismatched. He always wore his belt buckle off to the side in this feeble attempt at imitating Elvis Presley. There wasn't a disingenuous part of his make-up."
Mr. Salvucci, who is 56 and still wears his belt buckle to the side, satisfied many people in East Boston by reconfiguring the tunnel away from their neighborhood.
Charlestown, in turn, was unhappy with a series of interchanges so Mr. Salvucci redrew them.
In the beginning, Mr. Salvucci thought it would be impossible to bring the Artery underground without shutting down Boston. A contractor named Bill Reynolds had approached him at a community meeting in 1970 with the idea. "I said, 'What should we do, put a sign up at the Charles River, saying, Boston closed for alterations, come back in 10 years?' " Mr. Salvucci said.
At the time, Mr. Salvucci was a transportation adviser to Boston's Mayor Kevin White. He would regularly walk from City Hall to the North End for lunch -- under the Artery. "I started looking at it," said Mr. Salvucci, who left government when Gov. William F. Weld took office in 1991 and now teaches transportation studies at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. "I realized, 'Gee, there was actually some space. You could construct around it,' " he said. "I thought you literally had to tear it down first." The tunnel could be built while the Artery stayed open to traffic.
It took political engineering to get Congress to approve the necessary Federal highway funds.
In early 1985, according to a political history of the Big Dig by Harvard's Kennedy School of Government, Mr. Salvucci's Under Secretary of Transportation, Matthew Coogan, went to see a therapist. The therapist asked what was wrong.
Mr. Coogan "replied that he felt excessively nervous, exhausted and tense," according to the report. "The therapist asked why. Coogan went to the window, which overlooked the Central Artery and said, 'You see that road. My boss, the Governor of Massachusetts, and the Speaker of the United States House of Representatives, want to put it underground. The President of the United States and the people who work for him think that that is a terrible idea. My job is to convince them that they are wrong."'
The therapist assured Mr. Coogan that he was absolutely sane.
In 1987, with the political muscle of the late Thomas P. O'Neill Jr., who has then Speaker, and Senator Edward Kennedy, a Massachusetts Democrat, Congress overrode President Reagan's veto, and approved the transportation bill containing funds for what is officially called the Central Artery/Third Harbor Tunnel Project. The project was then expected to cost $4 billion.
In 1991, Federal law authorized 85 percent of the Big Dig's costs, which were then expected to be about $6 billion. That law provided that the state would be responsible for any cost increases beyond that. With Congress expected to vote in 1997 on a transportation bill that would contain new funding, questions have been raised about delays and escalating costs. In a report 18 months ago, the Federal Department of Transportation's Inspector General's office called the project out of control. The state has begun looking at regional transportation authorities for sources of revenue and is considering borrowing against future Federal appropriations.
In Boston, meanwhile, there is plenty of complaining. Every traffic jam is blamed on the Big Dig. There was one nightmarish day last month, when an engineer left a concrete barrier in place during the morning rush hour, and caused a four-hour traffic jam. He was summarily fired.
"It was one of those career defining moments in an engineer's life," said Peter Zuk, the project director.
Mr. Zuk, who is thinking about writing a book on the project, said the vast scope of the Big Dig has challenged some to greatness while infuriating others. "It's a metaphor for the human condition," he said.
With a huge excavation outside the window of her loft in an industrial neighborhood, Rebecca Dwyer, an artist, has been exasperated and inspired by the Big Dig. She has begun making collages out of its schematic maps.
The other day Ronnie Cook, a 50-year-old construction worker, was digging inside a 66-inch pipe, 25 feet underground. He pulled from his pocket a Polaroid snapshot of himself in the hole. It was for his 9-year-old daughter, Crystal, he said. He said he wants Crystal to know her father is working on a project for the history books.

HEADLINE: Ideas & Trends;
The Death of Some Salesmen

BYLINE: By ALLEN R. MYERSON 

DATELINE: DALLAS

 BODY:
PAUL BUNYAN, John Henry or Horatio Alger he's not. But America's workday culture has few figures as storied as the salesman, scouring a territory or going door to door, "riding on a smile and a shoeshine," as Arthur Miller memorably put it. The salesman was once ubiquitous. He trudged from the pages of Theodore Dreiser to a Warner Brothers cartoon where, as Daffy Duck, he wrecked Porky Pig's home while demonstrating a vacuum cleaner and other labor-saving thingamajigs.
No longer. When Encyclopedia Britannica dismissed all its home sales representatives last week, cutting 140 full-time employees and 300 independent contractors, it was but one more sign that the old-style career salesman is dead. 
 But not the smaller independent, face-to-face salesperson. Far from it. Where Willy Loman once was, there is now a new force of part-timers, mostly women, prying at the gates between their economic ghetto and the verdant middle class.
In an era of malls, home-shopping networks and catalogues, there are also more and more people like Cindy Funderburk of Florien, La. She drives a school bus, beginning at 6:20 A.M. She tends the chickens and cows on her farm. And she sells Beauticontrol CQ Herbal Serenity lotions, What A Pear! shampoo and Regeneration anti-aging creams with Alpha Hydroxy Acid Complex 3, making enough to help pay for her daughter's college and for three straight annual vacations to Branson, Mo.
The door-to-door mainstays have slowly mutated, until they are all but unrecognizable. Encyclopedia Britannica abandoned door-to-door sales decades ago in favor of having its salesmen, including many full-timers, follow up leads generated by advertising and referrals. At the Fuller Brush Company, 11 presidents in five years couldn't keep up the legendary sales force that once included Billy Graham and Joe DiMaggio. The company was sold two years ago to a buyer who, relying less on the salespeople, has nearly doubled the number of Fuller Brush stores.
At cosmetics companies, meanwhile, person-to-person sales are booming. The Direct Selling Association says that, from 1990 to 1994, the sales force nationwide has increased by more than third, to 6.3 million.
These figures might smell self-serving, but Neil H. Offen, the association's president, has an alarmingly frank explanation. "We provide an alternative to welfare, an alternative to unemployment compensation," he said. Or to other forms of downward mobility. "You can't stay in the middle class on one income," Mr. Offen said. "Sometimes not on two."
Many of the new salesmen and saleswomen belong to triple- or quadruple-career families. The rise in direct sales might partly explain why unemployment rates remain fairly low, despite wave after wave of corporate layoffs.
The industry's growth has been propelled by incessant recruiting. Many merchandisers give their salespeople a percentage on any other salespeople they recruit, and then on their recruits' recruits.
The pros like Mrs. Funderburk, collecting from 24 recruits, are always on the prowl for more. "Does your wife use Beauticontrol?" she asks.
 
 
 
Avon Calling
 Instead of knocking on unfamiliar doors, a tactic some towns now forbid as a danger to salespeople and customers alike, the new sellers network. "We train people in how to connect at the office and factory," said Christina Gold, Avon's chief for North America. "It's a high-touch business." In two years, she has expanded her sales force by about 45,000, to 445,000. Though Mrs. Gold will occasionally test the market by selling with Avon's traditional ding and dong, more than half the company's sales are made or arranged at work.
Sales at home, for companies like Mary Kay cosmetics that have followed Tupperware's lead, now are more likely to involve living-room parties or classes in skin care than cold calling or knocking.
Even Girl Scouts are learning the tricks of modern direct sales, setting up booths in malls, train stations and office lobbies. This year, troops in Hawaii and the Boston area took to flogging their cookies on the Internet. "Times have changed and so have our selling tactics," said Judy Welage, a spokeswoman for the Girl Scouts.
Some scouts invade Mom's and Dad's offices directly. Who is going to resist buying a few too many boxes of Peanut Butter Patties when confronted in the open by the boss's pigtailed daughter?
Unlike Mrs. Funderburk and Miss Pigtails, the traditional salesman suffers from an image that was muddied long ago. In the 1944 film "Double Indemnity," Fred MacMurray plays a visiting insurance agent noir who gets roped into the murder of a wealthy customer to collect the insurance payoff and the co-conspiring wife.
Decades later, when Louis Harris took a survey for the Direct Selling Association to help the industry shine its image, he reported that spending millions of dollars in one year would add no luster. "He said it would take 20 years," Mr. Offen, the association president, recalls.
As much as consumers have feared predatory salespeople, today's salespeople need protection from their rapacious companies. In an industry where annual turnover runs at 70 percent, companies often stick departing salespeople with overstuffed inventories and hundreds of dollars in training fees
In one sense, though, direct sales may be coming full circle. Mr. Offen reports that men are returning in swarms, with their proportion having more than doubled, to 24 percent, in three years. Most are victims of layoffs or low wages.
Mrs. Gold can readily pick up the scent. "Would you like to be an Avon representative?" she asked with a lilt. "I could have someone see you tomorrow."

HEADLINE: Machine Tool Orders Rose 19% in March

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools rose nearly 19 percent last month, but fell compared with this time last year, an industry trade group reported yesterday.
Orders for machine tools -- which are used to cut and shape metal in products ranging from toasters to tractors -- are considered a sign of whether manufacturers are confident enough about demand to refurbish and expand their factories. 
 March's orders totaled $499.8 million, up from $421.4 million in February, the Association for Manufacturing Technology said. Compared with a year ago, March orders were down 13.5 percent.
The machine tool report said domestic orders totaled $400.6 million in March, up 11 percent from February's level, but down 22 percent from March 1995. Foreign orders totaled $99.2 million, up 65 percent from February and up 54 percent from March 1995.
"The pace of export order growth accelerated in March, driven primarily by two of our major export markets -- Mexico and China," said Albert W. Moore, president of the association, which is based in McLean, Va.
Last month's shipments of machine tools totaled $453.75 million, down 1.5 percent from February, but up 16 percent from March 1995.
First-quarter orders totaled $1.38 billion, up 4 percent from 1995. First-quarter shipments rose to $1.25 billion, 25 percent ahead of the 1995 period.
The backlog of orders -- a measure of the ability of American tool makers to satisfy demand -- was $2.2 billion, up from February's $2.16 billion, but down from the backlog of $2.42 billion a year ago.
