HEADLINE: Freeze in U.S. Aid Hampers New Haitian President's Recovery Effort

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, April 26

 BODY:
The final American combat unit withdrew last week, leaving a reduced United Nations presence here. Now the United States has frozen most of its economic aid to Haiti, which until recently was being hailed as one of the Clinton Administration's shining foreign policy triumphs.
Because of a law sponsored by Senator Bob Dole, the presumptive Republican presidential nominee, about half of the $85 million in assistance that the United States had allocated this year will remain blocked until the Haitian Government shows progress in solving a series of killings of public figures here. 
 Haitian officials complain that the United States is demanding too much of their new police force and failing to cooperate in the investigations.
"It is sad to see the weakness of the criminal investigative capacity of our police, but that is the only structure we have" to meet the American demands, Robert Manuel, Haiti's new Secretary of State for Public Security, said in an interview here. "People should be honest and see the situation that we are in."
Acting under a United Nations resolution, more than 20,000 American troops landed here in September 1994 to restore President Jean-Bertrand Aristide to power three years after he was overthrown in a military coup.
Since then, the Haitian armed forces have been abolished and replaced by a National Police composed of hastily trained civilians, which has had difficulty maintaining order, preventing crime and catching lawbreakers.
The decision to hold back American aid comes at a moment when President Rene Preval, who succeeded Mr. Aristide in February as leader of the poorest nation in the Western Hemisphere, is especially vulnerable. More than $100 million in much-needed foreign assistance has already been on hold for six months because Mr. Aristide failed to fulfill pledges to begin selling inefficient state enterprises to private investors.
Largely as a result of the reduced flow of aid, which accounts for nearly two-thirds of the Government's budget, Mr. Preval has been unable to get the economy, battered by three years of an international embargo, moving again.
Unemployment remains over 50 percent, currency reserves are dropping, many health, education and agriculture projects are stalled, and the Government expects soon to lack even the funds to pay its own employees, who have begun taking to the streets in protest against possible layoffs.
"Come the summer, there could be an explosion here unless the economy starts up," warned a senior adviser to President Preval. "The people are tired, hungry and disillusioned, and we can't continue much longer in this state of prostration."
The Dole Amendment prohibits the United States from dispensing all but humanitarian aid to Haiti unless President Clinton certifies to Congress that the Haitian Government is conducting a "thorough" investigation of a series of killings in the last 18 months that Congress deems political.
Last week, the State Department said it had determined that the Haitian Government had made "insufficient progress" in the cases, and would "consult soon with Congress on how we propose to proceed."
The list of killings Washington wants investigated includes more than 25 cases. Most attention has been on the slaying on March 28, 1995, of Mireille Durocher Bertin, a prominent lawyer and former adviser to the military dictatorship who was ambushed along with a client and shot dead just three days before President Clinton visited here.
In a report issued after a brief trip here earlier this month, a Republican staff delegation strongly criticized the Haitian Government and the American Embassy for their handling of the Bertin case and others. The delegation concluded that the killings were politically motivated, that the Aristide Government had masterminded them and that, in essence, the American Embassy had joined in a cover-up of the slayings.
"While we do not agree with all of the opinions of the staff delegation that went to Haiti," the State Department said in a written response to the report, "we do not believe it would serve a useful purpose to engage in a point-by-point analysis of their statement of the problems they perceived. Human rights remains a high priority of the Embassy, since it is a high priority of U.S. foreign policy."
The Republican report and attempts to block aid have angered many here, who regard the efforts as pure electoral maneuvering.
Jean-Claude Bajeux, director of the Ecumenical Center for Human Rights and one of the country's leading intellectuals, said the Republicans did not care about Haiti or Ms. Bertin but "just want to embarrass Clinton."
But there has also been puzzlement at the Clinton Administration's efforts to gain political mileage from the intervention here, which ended with only one American combat fatality and a transfer of power from one popularly elected President to another for the first time in Haitian history.
The Administration has been quick to claim credit for those achievements, but more reluctant recently to protect its political investment here.
"The more the White House goes on about what a success they have had here, the more the Republicans feel compelled to tear it down," one diplomat said. "Against its will, and very much to its disadvantage, Haiti has been dragged into the American election campaign."
Denying that there was a political motive to the lack of progress in the murder investigations, Mr. Manuel noted that several cases in which supporters of Mr. Aristide had been killed also remained unsolved.
He said the Haitian Government had strengthened the police's Special Investigative Unit, which is investigating the killings, none of which have been formally characterized by human rights groups or the United Nations as political assassinations.
"They don't realize that we are just bad," he said of Haiti's critics in the American Congress. "We will have a good police force in five years, but not in one month."
Other Haitian officials complain that their efforts have been hampered by a lack of cooperation on the part of the United States. They maintain, for example, that American intelligence agencies and the F.B.I. have refused to make available copies of tapes of walkie-talkie conversations that Ms. Bertin had the day she was killed.
"The United States has a lot of information that it has not turned over," one official said. "Our Special Investigative Unit is only being allowed access to material on cases that occurred before Aristide's return."
The officials also say the country continues to face a security threat from renegade elements linked to the former military dictatorship.
Earlier this month, for instance, two Haitians said by officials here to be linked to the Tontons Macoute -- a private army of right-wing thugs -- were arrested in Miami on weapons trafficking charges, and their newly purchased guns and ammunition were seized.
The United Nations mission here is scheduled to end June 30, and it is not yet clear whether Chinese and Russian opposition to an extension can be overcome. The withdrawal of United Nations military and police forces would leave Haiti's security completely in the hands of the 5,000-member National Police, which already seems overwhelmed.
Enrique terHorst, head of the United Nations mission here, said he was "not going to second-guess the American Government" about its decision to suspend aid, but added that he believed it was "necessary to look at the state the Haitian National Police are in right now."
Even with United Nations assistance, he said, the fledgling force is hampered by "a lack of skills at this stage, and criminal investigation is the most sophisticated skill of any police force."
"After 29 years of the Duvalier dictatorship and 10 years of assorted military governments and social upheaval," Mr. terHorst added, "I think it can almost be said that this country is starting from scratch. The police are no exception."

HEADLINE: Libraries Ponder Role In the Digital Age

BYLINE: By WILLIAM GRIMES 

 BODY:
With the turn of the century looming, one of the oldest and most cherished cultural institutions, the library, stands at a crossroads. Librarians all over the world are struggling to cope with new technology that has revolutionized the storing, preserving and disseminating of information and changed the very notion of what a library is.
At the New York Public Library at Fifth Avenue and 42d Street, leaders from 50 of the world's main libraries in 26 countries gathered on Friday and Saturday to discuss the shape that libraries would take in the coming century, the social role that they would play and the promises and challenges presented by new technology. The conference, titled "Global Library Strategies for the 21st Century," was organized by Paul LeClerc, the president of the New York Public Library. 
 It became clear very quickly that for libraries, information is a blessing and a curse. Paul M. Horn, senior vice president for research at the International Business Machines Corporation, pointed out in an address that the cost of information storage had gone down by 40 percent a year in the last 20 years and that he could see no change in that tre'd. At some point in the future, he said, it would theoretically be possible to store all 16 million volumes in the Library of Congress on a disk the size of a penny.
But what gets stored and how? Like several other speakers, Mr. Horn suggested that the traditional role of the library as a place to check out a copy of "Tom Sawyer" would not change. But increasingly, librarians would be the guides and gatekeepers, or perhaps the travel agents, on the information superhighway. "Anyone can put anything on the Internet, and they do," he said. "It is difficult to make your way through the information."
Marilyn Gell Mason, the director of the Cleveland Public Library, agreed. "I sometimes wonder why we don't have librarians organize this stuff," she said. "It is very hard to surf the Internet, and I think the problem will get worse before it gets better."
Frances Schwenger, the chief executive officer of the Metropolitan Toronto Reference Library, said, "We will be judged by how well we create meaning and undertanding in a world of information chaos."
Mr. Horn predicted a much larger role for digital libraries, as traditional libraries create information packages combining the written word, images and sound that can be transmitted to individual work stations. At the Library of Congress in Washington, for example, I.B.M. has been transferring into digital form the achives of the Federal Theater Project, a New Deal arts program, including sets, props and costumes that are deteriorating rapidly.
Brian Lang, the chief executive and deputy chairman of the British Library in London, brought up a seeming paradox in the outlook for libraries. Commenting on the construction of major new libraries in Britain, Germany, France and Denmark, he said: "We are also investing huge sums so that people will not have to visit those buildings. That's a pretty neat trick that librarians have pulled off."
In one way or another, most of the participants in the conference were grappling with the concept of the library without walls and letting go of the traditional concept that every great library should be self-sufficient, with every title available. "The new library will not be judged by its collections but by its potential for connecting users with information," said Borge Sorensen, the director of the Copenhagen Public Libraries.
William G. Bowen, the president of the Andrew W. Mellon Foundation, paid that his foundation was now reluctant to finance projects that had the goal of allowing libraries or scholars generating a greater volume of work more easily. Instead it was looking for projects that generate savings.
In a pilot project supported by the foundation, about 750,000 pages of a dozen journals have been put into electronic form and stored at the University of Michigan at Ann Arbor. Later this year the titles pill be made available to libraries for a subscription fee. Users will be able to call up the titles 24 hours a day. Since, Mr. Bowen estimated, the cost of acquiring and processing a single issue of a scholarly journal is about $20, the savings in time, acquisition costs and storage space should offset the cost of subscribing to the service.
Several participants from Africa, Asia and South America warned that the new information age could end up widening the gap between the first and third worlds. "If we do not become part of this new age, we will be marginalized," said Christine O. Kisiedu, the librarian at the Balme Library of the University of Ghana in Legon. The shiny, high-tech library of the 21st century looks very remote in Angola, for example, which has two librarians and no libraries. Alfonso Romano de Sant'Anna, the president of Brazil's national library, told of visiting a library in Mozambique that had not a single book. The librarian had sold them to pay the light bills.
At tim's, the expansion of the world information map sounded like a game of musical chairs in which some players stood a good chance of being left without a seat. Some participants saw the potential for Anglo-American 'egemony in the universal use of English on the Internet. At the same time, Warren Horton, the director general of the National Library of Australia, said that Australia was among the world leaders in adopting new library technology in part out of a fear of isolation. "Australian culture is as threatened as anyone else's," he said.
In the United States, too, librarians worry about what one speaker called the "info-rich and the info-poor," a notion that runs counter to the original idea of America's libraries as an equalizing force.
"How do we achieve information equity when there is such an economic disparity among our users?" said Susan Goldberg Kent, the city librarian of the Los Angeles Public Library. That question loomed large at the conference, whose participants spent as much time talking about the library as a human gathering place as they did about technological solutions. Virtually every speaker described an expanding social role for libraries as community centers where citizens could receive literacy training, study English as a second language, learn how to function as parents or get health information.
"We are the most welcoming and least intimidating institutions there are," said Mary A. Dempsey, the commissioner of the Chicago Public Library. "The library is a place one can seek this information out without being judged."
Most participants affirmed the idea of the library as a human space in a potentially cold electronic world. The romance of the printed book, all agreed, would continue to cast its spell. And amid the forecasting, more than one speaker sounded the cautious note of experts smart enough to know what they don't know.
Mr. Horn of I.B.M. paused in his high-speed tour of digitization and the Internet long enough to say, "I can't imagine where it's all going." Peter Lyman, the librarian of the University of California at Berkeley, mused about the linguistic fumbling that the information age seemed to inspire. "We always talk about new technology using old vocabulary" he said. " 'Electronic publishing,' 'digital library,' 'information highway': to our grandchildren these terms will probably sound as peculiar as 'horseless carriage.' "

HEADLINE: TELEVISION REVIEW;
Rebels With a Cause And a Video Camera

BYLINE: By WALTER GOODMAN 

 BODY:
"Authority Sucks" is the elegant title of this week's editions of "MTV News Unfiltered," a daily half-hour program made by high schoolers with cameras supplied by MTV and then edited in the famous music-video slash-and-dash style for the benefit of viewers whose attention span could not last through this sentence.
The week begins economically with repeats, but tomorrow night brings a man who claims he teaches students "how to beat the S.A.T.'s"; a critic who calls graffiti "a beautiful crime"; a ferret owner who rhapsodizes on the lovability of the animal, which is outlawed in her home state of California, and enthusiasts of jet skiing and of "free-style walking." (The only rule, a practitioner of the latter, widely unpracticed sport explains, is "doing whatever you want to do." A teacher describes the free-walkers, with reasonable accuracy, as "two goofballs running up and jumping against the wall.") 
 These must be the most prudent rebels with trivial causes that the nation has ever thrown up. The S.A.T. specialist advises students to prepare for the detested test so they can get into good colleges; the glorifier of graffiti cautions against defacing private property; the jet skier prescribes pre-ski instruction and helmets.
Also telling their stories are an Italian exchange student who is stuck in the town of Osceola, Iowa, where the main pastimes seem to be drag racing, cruising and hanging around, and a high school sophomore who is being treated for Hodgkin's disease, the single serious note in what is a giddy half-hour. (The sophomore supplies the sole touch of wit. Speaking of her support group, she cracks, "Some of my best friends are bald.")
One must sympathize with both youths, but why are they lumped in an antiauthority bag? After all, the Italian visitor owes his education, albeit in the "middle of nowhere," to bureaucrats, and the cancer patient owes her apparent recovery to doctors and hospitals, those unrevolutionary institutions.
Maybe the rest of the week's insurgents will show more fiery tendencies, but maybe not. Thursday's program opens with an 18-year-old proclaimed virgin in Oklahoma who is fighting against the loose morals on television. Hey, not on MTV!
 
MTV NEWS UNFILTERED

HEADLINE: Mr. Clinton's Economic Scorecard

 BODY:
The week after the White House settled its budget squabbles with Congress is a good time to assess President Clinton's stewardship of the economy. He came into office promising to bring a consensus style of policy-making to the White House -- no longer would a budget director like Richard Darman, who served under President Bush, bully everyone in sight. He also promised to direct Federal spending away from wasteful programs and toward future-looking investments in training people.
How has he done? The record is mixed. He had many small successes and, in health and welfare reform, a few spectacular failures. On balance, Mr. Clinton did little harm and allowed the economy to prosper. That is not an exciting grade sheet. But it is a responsible one. 
 The economists he enlisted -- such as Joseph Stiglitz, Lawrence Summers, Lawrence Katz, Alan Krueger and Alan Blinder -- were all outstanding academics. But the main figure of the first term has been Treasury Secretary Robert Rubin, an investment banker who started off as the first head of the newly created National Economic Council. The N.E.C. was set up to coordinate policy-making, break down turf and bring big egos into line behind consistent policy objectives. Mr. Rubin's "no sharp elbows" style of management, perfected when he was head of Goldman Sachs on Wall Street, has worked well.
The Administration twisted the arms of Democrats in Congress to pass its 1993 deficit-reduction package and has pursued policies to bring down the deficit ever since, even though Labor Secretary Robert Reich and other Cabinet members were opposed. This was a responsible choice, but one that stifled bold initiatives. Perhaps the most important decision Mr. Rubin made was to keep the Administration off the back of the Federal Reserve Board. That allowed credit markets to breathe easy, leading to lower interest rates and steady growth.
On trade, the Administration struck a consistently aggressive and usually constructive stance -- even though Mr. Rubin himself would have preferred a more boisterous Japan-bashing stance. The White House finished off the Bush agenda by completing the Nafta accord with Mexico and the Uruguay Round treaty with the rest of the world.
A quieter, largely overlooked success of the first term reflected the "New Democrat" side of the Administration. The Administration scrapped scores of sclerotic bureaucratic structures in favor of incentive-based rules. Last week, it issued rules that will require electrical utility companies to open their transmission lines to competition from independent power generators. The Administration backed revisions of the unemployment insurance system so that the money could be used by the unemployed to set up new businesses. An important victory was achieved in the earned-income tax credit for low-paid workers, which was expanded to largely offset the stiff fall in their market wages over the past 20 years.
But there were also large failures. Health care reform was created outside the give and take of the N.E.C. and approved by the President even though nearly every one of Mr. Clinton's economic advisers thought the approach was folly. Welfare reform also failed, but for completely different reasons. The bill put together by David Ellwood, an economist at the Department of Health and Human Services, was a smart mixture of compassion and discipline, fully matching the mantra of Mr. Clinton's campaign to "end welfare as we know it." The failure was timing. Mr. Clinton held off submitting welfare reform until the fate of his health bill was sealed. Hindsight says that decision cost him both reforms.
A big disappointment of the first two years was Mr. Clinton's inability to push the Democrat-controlled Congress to go along with his plan to reshape the Federal budget toward investment. Once the President, at Mr. Rubin's urging, put deficit reduction above all else, he had no new money to spend. Once the Democratic leaders in Congress scuttled his plans to shift money away from their vested interests and toward new training and education initiatives, Mr. Clinton's invest-in-people strategy became little more than rhetoric.
When Mr. Clinton was cautious -- budget, trade, regulation -- he was sensible and modestly successful. When he was bold -- health care and welfare -- he failed. His artful parrying of Republican excesses preserved the safety net of welfare and Medicaid. Do no harm is not exciting. It looks pale next to the promises of his campaign. But the achievement is no less real.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Mali's Democracy

 BODY:
While hardly anybody was watching, the people of Mali were burning some 3,000 firearms in a March ceremony meant to mark the end of a five-year conflict between Tuareg nomad rebels and the Malian Army. These "flames of peace" are a salutary augury for Africa, and a pointed reminder for Washington. Mali has moved without fanfare from 35 years of single-party rule and 23 years of military dictatorship to a multi-party democracy. This feat might be better known if a single high-level American official had troubled in recent years to visit Bamako, Mali's capital.
Much of the credit for these gains belongs to President Alpha Oumar Konare, the victor in Mali's first free elections, in 1992. "The single-party system was a total failure in all countries which practiced it," Mr. Konare recently declared. "The logic of 'shut up and obey' gives immediate results, but offers no tomorrow." He is careful to add that it will take years of calm, openness and rule of law to consolidate free government. 
 But under this 49-year-old historian, Mali, once a French colony, has made an impressive start. The former dictator, Gen. Moussa Traore, who was ousted in 1991, is being tried for the killing of 100 students during riots that year, but his execution is ruled out. "I hope that Moussa Traore and his friends will live as long as possible so that they may see democracy flourish in Mali," says Mr. Konare, who opposes capital punishment. A free press, unknown in Mali until restrictions were lifted in 1992, already flourishes as 20 newspapers supplant a state monopoly. Mali, parting company with most African countries, condemned Nigeria for its execution of Ken Saro-Wiwa and other activists.
All this is happening in one of the world's 20 poorest countries, sandy and landlocked, whose eight million inhabitants are dependent on the River Niger, which flows through the heart of Mali. But the country's economy is percolating, at a 6 percent growth rate, which is partly a result of privatization and other free-market reforms. This success was recognized by the International Monetary Fund, which recently approved a three-year $91 million loan to Mali.
What is needed along with the money is attention and recognition. Spotlighting Mali's turn to democracy, and its efforts to integrate Tuareg refugees, can powerfully bolster an innovative leadership. The people of Mali will obviously be the final arbiters of this promising experiment, but showing that the world cares can improve the odds. That is something Washington might keep in mind the next time a top Government official goes to Africa.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: A Very Small Computer's Big Numbers

BYLINE: By GLENN RIFKIN 

DATELINE: CONCORD, Mass.

 BODY:
It might seem to take hubris to enter a new technology market and compete against the likes of Hewlett-Packard, Apple Computer, Sharp Electronics and Motorola. But with little fanfare, Psion P.L.C., a company based in London that specializes in hand-held computers, has grabbed a lead in the $1 billion worldwide market for these $200-to-$600 electronic organizers in the last few years.
Now Psion is pushing its way into the American market. In little more than a year, Psion has more than doubled the number of American retail stores selling its computers, to 2,800 from 1,200. 
 On Saturday, Psion began a $6 million advertising campaign, and it has dispatched a small army of sales support people to large retailers like Circuit City and Nobody Beats the Wiz.
"The word-of-mouth is terrific," said Richard Shaffer, president of Technologic Partners, a consulting firm in New York. "They make a great product, which is what drives this market. They've gotten good reviews in the trade press, and they don't get many returns."
Unlike its main competitors, Psion makes nothing but hand-held computers and has spent 15 years refining its software and hardware designs, starting with the first electronic organizers.
"We're purely focused on these devices," said David Potter, Psion's founder and chairman. "We don't have to think about microwave ovens or fax machines. We have more resources focused on this area than even our biggest competitors, like Hewlett-Packard or Sharp."
The machines start with $69 electronic address books and run up to palm-sized computers that sell for more than $1,000. The challenge facing Psion and its rivals is to persuade people to abandon their date books for something that costs more and has the potential to wipe out a lifetime of phone numbers if a battery dies.
Forrester Research, a technology research company in Cambridge, Mass., says Psion holds nearly 33 percent of the worldwide organizer market, just ahead of Hewlett-Packard's 200LX and brand new Omnigo, and far ahead of Sharp's Wizard and Zaurus and Apple Computer's Newton. Psion sales rose 48 percent in 1995, to $140 million, and profits increased 78 percent, to $18 million. And on the London Stock Exchange, the company's shares have more than tripled in the last year, closing on Friday at 1,095 pence.
Psion's presence in the United States remains small -- just under 20 percent of its sales are here -- but as the market for such organizers, known as personal digital assistants, or P.D.A.'s, searches for an identity, Psion has the opportunity to grab market share based on its reputation. Psion has 9 percent of the United States market, according to Forrester Research, trailing Hewlett-Packard, Apple and Sharp.
Despite its success, Psion faces a track filled with hurdles, not the least of which is competing in a market that remains ill-defined and fragmented. John Robb, an analyst with Forrester Research, said that early promises of sales rivaling those of PC's had failed to materialize.
"The upscale end of the P.D.A. market hasn't found a true reason for existence yet," he said. Mr. Robb noted that only 500,000 such devices were sold in 1995 worldwide, and though the market is growing by 40 percent annually, it is not likely to become a significant sector until the organizers can be used for effective and cheap access to electronic mail and the Internet.
To that end, Psion has just introduced Psimail, a software package that allows users to connect to popular electronic mail packages through a cable and modem connection. But electronic mail access may not be enough. Motorola, for example, sells the Envoy, which offers a pen-like device to give commands and a wireless radio modem for transmitting and receiving E-mail and files. The Envoy sells for about $1,000 and requires another $50 to $100 a month in phone charges to operate. Sales have been disappointing.
Despite the setbacks, there is no shortage of companies seeking to enter the competition. A new P.D.A. called the Pilot One-Touch Organizer from U.S. Robotics, for example, has already attracted attention. The $299 device, shipped this month, offers connections to desktop computers, allowing files to be transferred easily between the two.
Mitchell Kertzman, the senior vice president of worldwide sales and marketing at Sybase Inc. and a gadget lover, said the Psion was "the best-designed and most integrated" of the organizers on the market. And he owns many of them.
Though 70 percent of Psion's sales are in Europe, the company's marketing director, Peter Norman, said Psion was focused on the United States for its growth. The company has set up an American headquarters here in this suburb west of Boston.
In the United States, 35 percent of Psion's sales are going to corporate and industrial customers like Chrysler, Allied Signal, Dunkin' Donuts and American Airlines, for customized applications like inventory control, route accounting and asset management.
To Europeans, Psion is no upstart. The company was founded in 1980 by Mr. Potter, a former computational physics professor. Mr. Potter had written software, including a successful flight simulator for one of the world's earliest personal computers, a machine made by Sinclair.
Psion actually created the electronic organizer market and gave the category its name when it introduced the $150 Organiser II in 1984.
The latest models are powerful computers with many of the capabilities of a far more expensive laptop or desktop system. The tiny computer has a 16-bit operating system that can run many applications simultaneously, plus two megabytes of random access memory, which can be increased to 10 megabytes, and a liquid-crystal-display screen.
Psion has sold more than two million of its products, including 750,000 of its current high-end $399 Series 3a models, a notable success in a market known mainly for the disappointment of the Apple Newton.
The Psion Series 3a machine employs a small but reasonably efficient keyboard. Normal touch typing is difficult, but two-fingered typing, often using the thumbs, can be mastered.
"I was tired of carrying a Daytimer and a laptop on business trips," said Norma Spreeman, who works for a San Francisco architectural company and bought a Psion six months ago. "I can download pretty much everything I need onto the Psion and work from that."
She did take issue with the machine's design, which she said she found less than user-friendly. If anything, it is too complex, with countless features and a daunting 300-page manual that has turned off more than a few users. "It gets frustrating because I don't have the time to try to learn all these features," Ms. Spreeman said.
As Psion raises its profile in the United States, the competitor that causes most alarm, Mr. Potter said, is Microsoft, which he said had a development group with more than 200 people writing software applications for palmtop computers. "It will be the software that really drives this market," he said, "and Microsoft has the resources to be a key player in this segment."
A spokesman for Microsoft acknowledged that the company was investigating the organizer market but declined to provide details.

HEADLINE: Xerox Is Offering a New Category of Color Printer

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
Hoping to find a color-printing niche with machines that sell for about $200,000 each, the Xerox Corporation has introduced its Docu-color printer, which can transform electronic files into full color pages at a rate of 40 sheets a minute.
That is far faster than the color copiers on the market, which print six or seven pages a minute -- though some of these are far cheaper, selling for less than $50,000. 
 For the most demanding color-printing customers, even faster machines with better quality are available in the $350,000 to $400,000 price range. The AM DCP-1, Agfa Chromapress and IBM 3170, all of which use a print engine made by Xeikon of Belgium, and the Indigo E-Print 1000 each print up to 70 pages a minute.
"A lot of print shops who can't handle the price of an Indigo or a Xeikon machine are going to have to look seriously at these new machines," said Charles A. Pesko Jr. managing director of CAP Ventures in Marshfield, Mass., a printing industry consultant. "They are a new class of production between the color copiers and the Xeikon/Indigo machines, and they will probably displace multiple copiers in many shops."
The Docucolor, introduced last week, is based on a print engine developed by Xerox's Japanese affiliate, Fuji Xerox, and can be equipped with electronic front ends developed by Scitex, Electronics for Imaging and Colorbus for networked operations. Scitex, a specialist in prepress equipment, is already selling a similar machine called the Spontane. Scitex, an Israeli company, rejected an $856 million hostile offer made by an Israeli-American deal maker about a month ago.
Xerox invented the electronic- printing business in 1990, when it introduced the Docutech, a machine capable of accepting electronic files over networks and then printing them out at a rate of 135 pages a minute.
That machine, and the competitors that followed, changed the rules of printing. Since the base data was stored and transmitted in electronic form, only as many paper copies as were needed immediately had to be printed, eliminating long press runs and warehouses to hold excess copies. The operating principle has changed from print and distribute to distribute and print.
The machine has been a huge success for Xerox, with more than 10,000 of them, at $260,000 each, already installed. Whole new businesses, like providing custom anthologies for college courses, have developed.
Since then, many who follow the printing industry have wondered when the company would develop a color Docutech. Last week, the company got about a third of the way there, in terms of speed.
"They are playing a game of price and performance, and will probably fit well in niche markets," said Frank J. Romano, a professor of printing at the Rochester Institute of Technology. "But it will not be another Docutech. This machine existed for two years before Xerox decided to market it."
Professor Romano said that Xerox had been trying unsuccessfully to get Xeikon to supply it with print engines and had also sued the company, accusing it of patent infringment.
Some analysts say the Docucolor could give Xerox a nice boost in profit, both from the sales of the machines and the supplies needed to keep them operating.
"We believe that Xerox could place as many as 1,000 Docucolor machines within the next 12 months and more than 5,000 by the end of the decade," said Jonathan Rosenzweig, an analyst with Salomon Brothers, in a report last week. "Early users are likely to include quick printers, in-plant operations and low-end commercial printers."
Conventional offset printing will always be cheaper for documents printed in large quantities, usually defined as 5,000 copies or more. But because conventional technology requires considerable setup expense, many color documents that are needed only in small quantities are never printed, industry experts say.
One reason the Docucolor is faster than color copiers is that the paper shoots straight through the machine, rather than having to make four turns around the imaging drum to put down the colors cyan, magenta, yellow and black. An imaging belt, rather than a drum, runs under four separate toner heads to apply the elements of an image in one pass.
If a page is to be printed on both sides, it must travel a reverse path through the machine, be inverted, and then head down the belt once again.
With color more easily available, many documents now printed in black and white are expected to add color. Mr. Rosenzweig anticipated that the color machines would be used alongside Docutech-type printers to provide full-color inserts and covers for large black-and-white documents.
Mr. Pesko estimates that the market for short-run color printing will amount to $12 billion a year by the end of the century.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "Bets on a Niche"
Xerox is introducing a color copier and printer aimed at midsized firms. When its predecessor, the black-and-white Docutech Publisher, was introduced in 1990, it created an $8 billion instant printing industry.
 
COST At about $200,000, the price of the Docucolor 40 is half that of some current high-quality color printers.
 
SPEED It prints 40 pages a minute, compared with 7 a minute for color copiers and 70 for more expensive machines.
 
WHY IT'S CHEAPER
The resolution on the Docucolor 40 is 400 by 400 dots per inch; more expensive models have resolutions of 800 by 800 d.p.i. The Docucolor won't perfectly duplicate the tonal range of complex artwork.
 
WHY IT'S FASTER
The printer lays down the four colors necessary for full-color printing in one pass of the paper instead of four.
 
(Source: Xerox)

HEADLINE: Essay;
Same-Sex Marriage Nears

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: MAUI, Hawaii

 BODY:
As the legislative session headed toward a midnight close Friday night, opponents of homosexual marriage insisted on a recorded vote. Supporters of gay rights won. Hawaii's state senators voted 15 to 10 against the more conservative House's bill to ban the licensing of marital unions of persons of the same sex.
As a result, the state's lawyers will find it harder to assert a "compelling state interest" in limiting marriage to one man and one woman. Baehr v. State of Hawaii comes before Judge Kevin Chang in August; the likelihood increases that he will rule that the state Constitution's provision against sex discrimination precludes the state from refusing to issue marriage licenses to gay couples. 
 No longer can defenders of one-man, one-woman marriage claim that the institution is being threatened by hyperactivist judges. One state's duly elected representatives have just paved the way, and under the U.S. Constitution's "full faith and credit" clause, a judicial proceeding held in one state is to be honored in all states.
Nor is the movement limited to the United States. In The Hague last week, a majority of the Dutch parliament called on the Government to submit legislation permitting homosexual marriage. That would affect all members of the European Union; British gays could marry in Amsterdam and claim spousal status in London.
The Hawaii solons, in a tense conference just before the deadline, considered a compromise. Homosexuals in contracted "domestic partnerships" were not to be denied pension or insurance benefits or inheritance or other state tax deductions available to spouses, thereby assuring fairness as taxpayers and equal treatment as employees -- but not including a state of matrimony. The compromise, which might have affected the outcome of the court case, failed. That was unfortunate because that is the way this issue should be resolved.
Libertarians like me have a tough time with same-sex marriage because we despise discrimination, cherish privacy and reject homophobia. When we express reluctance to rush to make a fundamental change in the legal and religious definition of a family, editorialists assail "entrenched anti-gay bigotry" and shrilly accuse all who fail to embrace S.S.M. of being "driven by social intolerance." Such straight-bashing neither contributes to civil discourse nor persuades us we are morally obtuse.
To the heart of the matter: Why break the age-old pattern of defining marriage as the union of one man, one woman? Or, to slant the question the other way: Why should heterosexuals have a lock on wedlock?
Serious arguments presented by supporters of same-sex marriage have force: In times of casual cohabitation, marriage would encourage homosexual couples to stay together and build stable, mutually supportive lives. Moreover, when so many children need loving parents, society's sanction of marriage would make adoption much easier, though not everyone agrees that gay adoptive parents would not influence the sexual orientation of a child.
Religion aside, the state's interest in marriage is to create a unit for mutual care and the bearing and rearing of children. Tax and inheritance incentives have long been offered to bolster these "family values," which are under great strain, and nothing should be done now to further weaken the traditional family.
Underlying this disagreement, and giving the debate its bitterness, is the belief that what is at stake is not just tax and pension fairness (in the works for domestic partners who take contractual vows) but the reach for unequivocal social and theological approbation. Gays understandably want not just equal economic treatment but equal moral status; most Americans are not ready to go beyond toleration to active approval. That division is not in dispute.
Will S.S.M. be a political issue? Hawaii, dominated by Democrats, has shown it already is. In the national campaign, Republicans will oppose gay marriage, perhaps in a platform plank; if Bill Clinton ever holds a press conference and is asked about this, he can be expected to straddle, saying he "personally" opposes same-gender legal liaison, but it is a state issue and he's for states' rights.
The nation and its gay minority would be best served by the compromise Hawaii just scorned.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: Hong Kong Governor, Due in U.S., Is Warily Bullish

BYLINE: By EDWARD A. GARGAN 

DATELINE: HONG KONG, April 28

 BODY:
As Christopher Patten prepares to begin his final year as the territory's last Governor, he will visit the United States this week both to trumpet Hong Kong's successes and future and to outline his concerns about China's takeover of Hong Kong on July 1, 1997.
The Governor will visit the United States in part to campaign for renewal of China's trade privileges, the so-called most-favored-nation status. The visit comes amidst a vitriolic barrage against him from China and in the wake of public demonstrations against China's plans for Hong Kong, which include the abolition not only of its elected legislature, but also of locally elected boards and councils. 
 "I'm still pretty bullish about Hong Kong," the Governor said in an interview in his sparely furnished office. "But it's because I'm confident about the economy and because I think the values that Hong Kong has espoused are the values of the future, both here and the rest of Asia."
But Mr. Patten lashed out at a storm of Chinese pronouncements about Hong Kong, particularly a recent warning from China's de facto ambassador here, Zhou Nan, that Hong Kong must not become "a center for political struggle." Many understood Mr. Zhou's comments as a barely veiled threat against Hong Kong's Democratic Party, which is committed to universal suffrage here.
Mr. Patten, who introduced sweeping democratic reforms of Hong Kong's legislature culminating in the mostly popularly elected body that was sworn in last September, sarcastically dismissed the idea that democracy and political activity were inappropriate for Hong Kong.
"The politics isn't some optional add-on and, as Zhou Nan suggests, something the departing British colonial oppressors are imposing on the patriotic people of Hong Kong," he said. "The politics is both intimately related to Hong Kong's economic success and just part of the fabric of this place as a decent and civilized society."
During the last month, China's officials have denounced Hong Kong's Legislature and popular demands for electoral freedom, statements that have provoked jitters across the territory. When the Chinese Government tried, two weeks ago, to hold public hearings on its plans for Hong Kong, it excluded many hoping to present their views.
Beijing's penchant for secrecy and its discomfort with open government have fueled worries among Hong Kong's professional civil servants, who are still unsure about China's intentions toward them. And there is widespread concern about the preservation of Hong Kong's laws, as well as unease that China's endemic corruption will seep over the border.
Mr. Patten said that only by insuring Hong Kong's autonomy, which China has promised for the first 50 years after the takeover, could those worries be assuaged.
"Hong Kong has been given substantial promises of continuing economic liberty in the future and liberty in all other areas with the exception of foreign affairs and defense -- I don't think there are senior leaders in Beijing who take exception to that," the Governor said. "But on the other hand, I think there are people further down in the bureaucracy who, unless they are dealt with pretty firmly, will try to get their hands on bits of the silverware in Hong Kong."
One guarantee of Hong Kong's continued vibrancy, Mr. Patten said, will be the renewal of China's most-favored-nation status. "It is important to separate trade and politics," he said. "If Hong Kong can continue to act as China's economically free-standing bridge to the world, it's going to be easier to deal with China over the next few years."
Hong Kong's economy is now integrally tied to China's, with more than 60 percent of foreign investment in China emanating from the territory. Indeed, the Government here estimates that failure to renew China's most-favored-nation status would cut growth in the territory's gross domestic product by half and eliminate 90,000 jobs.
Yet, Mr. Patten said, even as he campaigned for most-favored-nation renewal, he would not shrink from discussing the problems facing Hong Kong.
"Does it carry credibility for the Governor of Hong Kong or somebody in my position to stand up and tell the world, 'There ain't no problems'?" he asked. "No. But I think there is enough momentum in this city beyond economic success for it to continue."

HEADLINE: New Pact for Kazak-Russian Oil Pipeline

BYLINE: By MICHAEL R. GORDON 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, April 28

 BODY:
Moving to tap the vast oil resources near the Caspian Sea, Russia, Kazakstan and Oman have agreed on a new accord to build a major oil pipeline.
Seeking to overcome longstanding financial disputes, the accord sets terms for a pipeline to connect Kazakstan's rich Tengiz oilfield to a Russian port on the Black Sea. 
 Eight Western, Russian and Kazak oil companies are to be involved in the project, which is expected to cost at least $1.2 billion.
"For a long time we did not agree on redistributing shares in the consortium," President Boris N. Yeltsin said on Saturday, during a visit to Almaty, the Kazak capital, on his way back from a trip to China. But now, he said, "all questions have been solved."
President Nursultan A. Nazarbayev of Kazakstan called the accord a "historic economic agreement" and predicted optimistically that the project would be completed within two and a half years.
Still, Russia has previously given its blessing to pipeline projects only to raise technical obstacles later.
And much of the financing, which is to be arranged by the oil companies, will not be provided until after the Russian presidential election in June. That election has clouded the economic picture. The Communist candidate, Gennadi A. Zyuganov, still holds a slim lead over Mr. Yeltsin in most opinion polls.
But the Russian stock market recently bounced back, after a long decline, because of opinion polls showing that Mr. Yeltsin has been closing the gap and reports that the rebel leader of Chechnya, Dzhokhar M. Dudayev, has been killed.
Foreign and Russian business executives are wary of the Communists, who have sounded nationalist themes even as they have insisted that foreign investment would be welcome.
On Friday, some of Russia's most important businessmen issued an appeal, warning that Russia was so polarized politically that the nation could be in for a new round of "violence and discord," regardless of who wins the election. The appeal urged that a new compromise platform be forged.
Even without such election-year complications, the Tengiz pipeline has had a long and tangled history.
Under a 1993 accord, the Chevron Corporation has a $10 billion contract to develop the Tengiz field, which has six billion barrels of proven crude reserves. But there were bitter disputes over the construction of the pipeline, including one with the Oman Oil Company, which is owned by the Omani Government.
Along with Russia and Kazakstan, Oman was part of the three-member consortium that sought to build and manage the pipeline.
There was a lot at stake: profits for American companies and a greater political independence from Moscow for Kazakstan.
In an effort to break the stalemate, Kazakstan and Chevron sought to assemble a broader coalition, including American, Russian and Italian oil companies.
During Mr. Yeltsin's visit to Almaty, a protocol was signed by the consortium's board, formalizing a new arrangement and redistributing the shares.
Under the accord, Russia, Kazakstan and Oman will no longer own the entire consortium. Their collective share will be 50 percent.
Specifically, Russia's share will be 24 percent. Kazakhstan will have 19 percent, and Oman's share will be 7 percent, though the Interfax news agency in Russia said Oman might consider selling its share to other participants.
Eight oil companies will control the other half of the consortium. They will also be responsible for financing the project, which is expected to cost more than $1 billion.
Under the new accord, Chevron will own 15 percent. Lukoil, the largest Russian oil company, will own 12.5 percent.
Other owners include the Mobil Corporation (7.5 percent), Rosneft of Russia (7.5 percent), British Gas (2 percent), AGIP of Italy (2 percent), the Dallas-based Oryx Energy Company (1.75 percent) and Munaigaz of Kazakstan (1.75 percent).
The pipeline will be operated by the Transneft oil transportation company of Russia.
"The project has been stalled for many months," said one Western oil official. "If the consortium has the will to finish it, it is a breakthrough."

HEADLINE: For Union Chief, Tough Challenges

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
From the way he used to rail against "the tired old men" of the A.F.L.-C.I.O. and the way he once backed Jesse Jackson for President, Jan Pierce always seemed to relish a good fight.
But now Mr. Pierce, who heads the Communications Workers of America in New York and New England, finds himself in the middle of two fights that he wished had never erupted. 
 If he loses the first battle, in leading the union charge to stop the merger of Nynex and Bell Atlantic, consumer and labor groups fear that it could have big and negative consequences for customers and workers in the Northeast. And if he loses the second battle, one in which a challenger hopes to oust him as head of the region's communications workers' union, political leaders worry that it could alter union and Democratic politics in the region by removing one of the labor movement's most outspoken and liberal leaders.
In political and labor circles, the campaign to defeat Mr. Pierce is being closely watched because he has been such a spark plug in supporting strikers in other cities and in campaigning for Mario M. Cuomo, David N. Dinkins and dozens of other Democrats. And many Democrats are deeply worried that this 58-year-old union leader who poured oceans of manpower and money into their campaigns could be defeated.
"He's an articulate spokesman for social, political and economic justice," Mr. Dinkins, the former Mayor, said. "I think the world of him. If he leaves the scene, it will be a hell of a loss."
The thin, intense Mr. Pierce has been such a force in Democratic politics that last month a who's who of New York Democrats, including Mr. Cuomo, Mr. Dinkins, Mr. Jackson, Bella S. Abzug and a dozen members of Congress, attended a dinner to celebrate his 40 years in the labor movement.
"He is surely one of the most notable political activists among union people," Mr. Cuomo, the former Governor, said. "There are not a lot of them left. There is something invigorating about his kind of commitment."
As leader of 120,000-member District 1 of the communications workers union, Mr. Pierce represents 40,000 Nynex workers, more than 40,000 New Jersey government workers, about 8,000 New York City municipal workers, and thousands of nursing home and cable television employees. The C.W.A.'s reach will soon extend to reporters, editors and other newspaper workers when a merger with the Newspaper Guild takes effect next year.
In battling the proposed Nynex-Bell Atlantic merger, Mr. Pierce is using the strengths that have distinguished him as a labor leader -- a lofty moral tone that seems to sway audiences, a 1930's-type knack for rallying people and an ability to attack enemies on several fronts at once. He has teamed up with consumer groups to assert that the merger of the two regional telephone giants will reduce competition, push up prices and hurt service. He is also spearheading efforts to persuade the public service commissions in New York, New Jersey, Massachusetts and nine other states to block the merger.
"The merger is bad for consumers and for workers," he said. "If you look at the history of breakups and mergers in telecommunications since 1984, you see that service quality has suffered tremendously, and it has not resulted in lower telephone bills. Furthermore, the communications workers have lost 15,000 members from AT&T and the Baby Bells."
The two companies say 3,000 employees, none of them union members, will lose jobs as a result of the merger. But Mr. Pierce contends that the companies are underestimating the numbers to minimize public dismay and union opposition.
"If this merger isn't blocked, then five years after the deal is completed, we could be looking at the loss of 20,000 to 25,000 jobs," he said.
The two companies also assert that such large layoffs are not envisioned and that the merger will improve their efficiency and thus hold down telephone service costs for consumers.
Some of Mr. Pierce's detractors say he is mounting his anti-merger crusade in large part to aid his re-election campaign. Some union members say they oppose Mr. Pierce because he is too far to the left and has created tensions in the union by isolating aides who disagree with him. They also complain that he usually consults with just a close-knit circle of advisers, ignoring most local presidents under him on important decisions like negotiating strategy.
"He talks like he's for the people, but he runs union affairs like a dictatorship," said John Alphonse, president of a C.W.A. local in Poughkeepsie, N.Y.
Mr. Pierce responds that in his 11 years as head of District 1, he has repeatedly crisscrossed the Northeast to meet and consult with dozens of local union officials.
Mr. Pierce suggests that there is one powerful force behind the campaign to unseat him: Morton Bahr, president of the parent communications workers union. According to Mr. Pierce, his longtime mentor, Mr. Bahr, has turned on him and put up a candidate to oppose him in the June election because his positions have often diverged from Mr. Bahr's. Mr. Bahr did not return repeated telephone calls seeking comment.
Mr. Pierce's opponent, Larry Mancino, denies that Mr. Bahr persuaded him to run. He said he had decided to seek the post because there had been grumbling about Mr. Pierce's leadership, adding that he would have a more inclusive administration.
Mr. Pierce was elected to his current post in 1985 when Mr. Bahr handpicked him as his successor. At that time, Mr. Bahr moved to Washington to become president of the 600,000-member international union.
Sounding like a son trying to escape from his father's shadow, Mr. Pierce said: "I don't know if he was able to accept me as a leader in my own right. I don't know if he wanted me to be subordinate my whole life. I couldn't accept that."
The two men were so close that when Mr. Bahr asked Mr. Pierce to move from Buffalo to New York City in 1973 to become his assistant, Mr. Bahr found an apartment for Mr. Pierce in Manorhaven on Long Island, just a few miles from Mr. Bahr's home. For a dozen years, the two commuted together each day on the Long Island Rail Road.
They have moved steadily apart since 1985. Mr. Pierce angered the less confrontational Mr. Bahr by adopting a strategy of pressuring Federal and state regulatory agencies as a way of gaining leverage over Nynex during contract talks. This strategy hit the jackpot in 1994 -- and won praise from union leaders nationwide -- when Nynex promised its 40,000 unionized workers four years of job security in exchange for Mr. Pierce's pledge to stop opposing Nynex's request for a rate increase.
While Mr. Pierce has vowed to use every ounce of his energy to block the Nynex-Bell Atlantic merger, Mr. Bahr has been extremely cautious, saying the union has "serious concerns" about the deal and needs more information.
Mr. Pierce's opponent, Mr. Mancino, used to be his top assistant in New York but has worked in recent years as one of Mr. Bahr's top aides.
"I've been asked by an overwhelming number of locals to run," Mr. Mancino said. "This came about as the result of a grass-roots effort."
Supporters and opponents alike of Mr. Pierce say he alienated Mr. Bahr by expressing views without caring whether they offended the union president. He also upset Mr. Bahr by supporting Mr. Jackson over Michael S. Dukakis in the 1988 Presidential race and by berating the A.F.L.-C.I.O. hierarchy before John J. Sweeney became president.
Some union officials say Mr. Pierce's declaration of independence was part of a plan to someday challenge Mr. Bahr as C.W.A. president -- something Mr. Pierce denies.
Union delegates will choose whether to keep Mr. Pierce at a convention on June 11, with both candidates expressing confidence that they will win. If Mr. Pierce loses, some union members say, the reason will be that he failed to do the political work needed inside his union even as he did plenty of political work outside.

HEADLINE: A.A.R.P. Will License Its Name To Managed Health Care Plans

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
In a move that could prompt millions of older Americans to join managed health plans, the American Association of Retired Persons will begin licensing its name to health maintenance organizations across the country later this year.
The 33-million-member association, best known as a lobbying group for the elderly, is already a big player in the insurance business, endorsing selected auto and homeowners policies, and insurance that pays medical bills not covered by Medicare. In return, it receives substantial revenue from royalties and related income -- $146 million in 1994, which provided 38 percent of its budget. 
 The association said it would deal only with health organizations that met its standards on financial stability, commitment to quality, price and popularity with current members. A consulting firm, Health Benefits America, is screening hundreds of H.M.O.'s for the association. Among other criteria, the association will use standards that are being developed by the National Committee on Quality Assurance, a nonprofit group sponsored by managed-care companies and employers.
The association will seek formal bids late in June or early in July, with the aim of entering into marketing contracts with one or more health organizations in each of 15 or 16 regional markets.
"Our name and logo would be associated with it," said Wayne Haefer, membership director of the association. The group will keep the health maintenance organizations under continuous review, he said, backed by the right to drop any that fail to measure up.
For H.M.O.'s, the prospect of winning the association's endorsement is a mouth-watering opportunity. In a single stroke, they would gain access to a national brand name and to millions of potential customers, and benefit from the association's marketing prowess. The association will promote the health care organizations in mailings; in its national magazine, Modern Maturity, and other publications. And it will provide the H.M.O.'s with lists of its members and advice on "how to talk with people of this age and needs," Mr. Haefer said.
The Federal Government and dozens of big companies with rosters of retirees are already promoting health maintenance organizations as a way of controlling the costs of health care for the elderly; Medicare H.M.O.'s are among the fastest-growing types of managed care.
But the push into managed care is fraught with risk for the nation's biggest membership association. A.A.R.P. will be lending its name to businesses that at times limit or even refuse medical services, instead of just making sure that claims are paid. That could create a backlash in a constituency that has generally looked upon the association as a vigorous advocate for its interests.
Putting the group's seal of approval on particular health organizations could "raise questions about their ability to maintain their credibility as a neutral broker," said Bruce Vladeck, chief administrator of the Federal Medicare program.
"Getting into that business is high-risk stuff," said Marilyn Moon, a researcher in the economics of Medicare at the Urban Institute in Washington. "If some of these guys turn out to be bad actors, it will have an impact -- as it should -- on whoever endorses them."
Among the big companies offering managed care, spokesmen for Cigna, Kaiser Permanente, Humana, Prudential Life Insurance, U.S. Health Care and the national Blue Cross and Blue Shield Association said they were interested. In several states, notably California and Florida, Medicare payment formulas generate high profits for health organizations.
"It's very exciting," said Karen Ignagni, president of the American Association of Health Plans, a trade group in Washington. "Interest is very high around the country."
For its part, A.A.R.P. has little choice but to embrace managed care if it wishes to keep its important role in the health insurance business, industry experts said. It collected $101.7 million in 1994 from its arrangement with Prudential to market so-called Medigap policies and other health coverage to its members, and an additional $17.7 million in interest income.
Adding $25 million collected from the ITT Hartford Group on auto and homeowners policies and $1.7 million from other insurance sources, the group's revenue from insurance that year -- $146.1 million -- was slightly greater than that from membership dues, $145.7 million.
But recently, the association has been losing customers to the Medicare H.M.O.'s, which provide full coverage to elderly Americans, and to other Medigap insurers that offer lower rates to younger, healthier customers. Because some insurance companies charge higher premiums to discourage older, illness-prone Medigap applicants, the association -- which charges the same for all ages and says that it will continue doing so -- has become an insurer of last resort. Its total of 3.2 million policyholders has not changed much in the last few years, though their average age has gone up.
With costs rising, Prudential's premium revenue from Medigap and other health care coverage for the association's members fell $100 million last year, to $3.4 billion, according to A. M. Best, the insurance-industry research firm. Prudential recently raised Medigap premium rates by 30 percent.
"The association is losing its membership right now to H.M.O.'s," said Clifton F. Crawford, a vice president of Medicare marketing for Cigna. "They want to cut a deal before it all dwindles away."
The association's decision to embrace managed-care plans for the elderly is likely to spur the surging growth of Medicare H.M.O.'s just as the baby-boom generation is beginning to turn 50, the minimum age for joining A.A.R.P. And experts on the elderly say consumers are likely to benefit from A.A.R.P.'s involvement in managed care.
The association could "mediate and protect consumers and let them know what is going on," said James Firman, president of the National Council on the Aging, a nonprofit research and advocacy group.
Mr. Vladeck, the Medicare chief, agreed. "There is a desperate, overwhelming need for better consumer information about the choices between fee-for-service medicine and managed care, and among managed-care health plans," he said.
Still, some patient advocates question how well the association -- or anyone else -- can appraise the quality of H.M.O.'s, given the relative sparseness of objective, long-term research. "We have absolutely no idea how well they are caring for people who are really sick," said Diane Archer, executive director of the Medicare Beneficiaries Fund, an advocacy group in New York.
The association plans to endorse managed-care plans for both its younger and older members; the membership is about evenly divided between people 65 or older and those who are 50 to 64. It will do so with the knowledge that employers typically make the choices among health plans for younger, working people, as noted by Tom Elkin, a consultant based in Sacramento, Calif.
Executives at several of the 63 Blue Cross and Blue Shield associations said privately they were concerned about the risk of dividing the enormous A.A.R.P. membership by age, saying that could drive costs even higher for people in their mid-70's and older, who often avoid managed care and stick with their family doctor.
Mr. Haefer said the association looked hard at the trends toward managed health care as Congress and the White House debated proposals to deal with rising Medicare costs. "Our members want a choice," he said. "We are not looking to force people into a particular type of managed-care product."

CORRECTION-DATE: April 30, 1996, Tuesday

 CORRECTION:
A chart yesterday with a front-page article about plans of the American Association of Retired Persons to license its name to health maintenance organizations misstated the organization's 1994 revenue from insurance fees and related interest income. It was $146.1 million, not $164.9 million.

HEADLINE: Antiterrorism Law Gives Immigrants No Second Chances

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re your April 25 news article on President Clinton's signing of the antiterrorism bill: 
 In the name of combating terrorism, the nation's politicians have passed thinly veiled anti-immigrant legislation, pandering to an electorate they perceive as disenchanted with the presence of "aliens" in our midst. While highlighting self-righteous displays of supposed antiterrorist bipartisan solidarity, the reporting of President Clinton's signing of the Antiterrorism Act of 1996 failed to disclose the bill's mean-spirited anti-immigration provisions.
The law eradicates significant relief against deportation, which was previously available to non-United States citizens. For example, before the effective date of one provision of the new law, the Immigration and Nationality Act permitted a long-term lawful permanent resident of the United States to have certain crime-related grounds of deportability waived, if it was demonstrated that there existed compelling reasons for an immigration judge to so order. Such relief was granted only after a demonstration of extraordinary equities and compelling arguments against the applicant's deportation.
Countless Americans born in the United States have reaped the benefits of receiving a second chance, to redeem themselves in the eyes of the public and the law.
Not to permit a long-term lawful resident of the United States to avail himself of that same opportunity, the only difference being national origin, is nothing more than xenophobia.
Another unfortunate provision of the bill prevents a person who entered the United States without inspection to apply for another form of discretionary relief known as suspension of deportation.
Under the new law, a person who has built up a thriving business, employed many American citizens, paid taxes and has been a vital contributor to his community and to the American economy, whose only failing is having entered without a visa, is no longer prima facie eligible for such relief. No matter how valuable an asset to this country such a person proves himself to be, he will not get his day in court under the law.
A particularly punitive aspect of the new law is its summary exclusion provision, which severely limits certain asylum seekers' access to adjudication of their claims of fear of persecution. People who fear persecution do not arrive at our nation's airports with fully developed and documented claims for political asylum, complete with letters from their oppressors.
Our politicians are certainly courageous in enacting legislation that curtails the rights of those least able to defend themselves and least likely to finance an electoral campaign.
 
RICHARD A. GEDULDIG
New York, April 26, 1996
 
The writer is an immigration lawyer.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Absence of Buying by Japan Weakens Dollar Against Yen

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar fell against the yen yesterday, hurt by speculation that the Bank of Japan might soon raise interest rates.
The dollar was also weakened as currency traders took advantage of a national holiday in Japan to sell dollars, believing the Bank of Japan would not intervene to support the dollar against the yen. While Japanese markets were closed yesterday for the holiday, Greenery Day, the Bank of Japan might have left orders with other central banks to buy dollars for yen on its behalf. But such orders did not materialize in the market. 
On Friday, the Japanese central bank bought an estimated $200 million to $500 million for yen, traders said.
"By its absence, the Bank of Japan created a very negative sentiment for the dollar," said Thomas Lapinski, chief dealer at MTB Bank.
The dollar has been weak against the yen since Friday, when a Japanese newspaper reported that the central bank would soon push interest rates up.
Although the report was denied by Bank of Japan officials, the yen rally has not lost any steam. Higher rates would bolster the value of the yen by making deposits denominated in the Japanese currency more attractive.
In late trading in New York yesterday, the dollar settled at 104.67 yen, down from 105.55 yen on Friday. The dollar fell to 1.5265 marks from 1.5284 marks. The British pound fell to $1.5069 from $1.5130.
Despite two days of declines, many currency traders and analysts remained bullish on the dollar.
"We would not regard this as any change in direction for the dollar," said John de Beer, who oversees $2 billion in foreign bonds at Loomis, Sayles & Company in Boston. "We still think the general trend for the dollar is up."
While Japan's bond yields and money-market rates have risen as the economy emerges from a four-year economic slowdown, it may be too early to bet on an increase in rates by the Bank of Japan, traders said.
The country's banking system remains under pressure from an estimated $400 billion of bad loans, and several false starts by the economy in the last three years should make the central bank cautious about raising rates, traders said.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
It's account decision time for a number of companies both big and small.

BYLINE: By Stuart Elliott 

 BODY:
ADVERTISERS old and new, big and small -- including Coca-Cola, Sears, Novell and Wired magazine -- have made decisions involving accounts.
The Coca-Cola Company has assigned creative responsibilities for its Cherry Coke soft-drink brand to Cliff Freeman & Partners in New York, a unit of Cordiant P.L.C. The brand had been without an agency since Chiat/Day in Venice, Calif., resigned in late May after being acquired by the Omnicom Group, which owns shops that handle soft-drink, fast-food and snack-food accounts for Coca-Cola's principal rival, Pepsico Inc. 
 Bob Bertini, a spokesman for Coca-Cola in Atlanta, confirmed a report about Cherry Coke this week in Adweek; billings were not disclosed. Plans for Diet Cherry Coke, which Chiat/Day had also handled, have not been determined, Mr. Bertini said yesterday.
Cherry Coke is the second Coca-Cola assignment for Freeman, which is known for humorous campaigns for clients like Little Caesars pizza and the Staples chain of office-supply stores. Freeman was awarded a Coca-Cola project in October, which Adweek reported involved advertising for two-liter plastic contoured bottles.
The additional work from Coca-Cola may have been what Freeman alluded to last week in announcing that "new assignments and current client business" required a withdrawal from a review being conducted by Driver's Mart Worldwide Inc. in Grand Rapids, Mich., a new company owned by a group of auto dealers that plans to open a national chain of used-car stores this year.
As a result, Driver's Mart has restored to its review Warwick Baker & Fiore in New York, a semifinalist that had been eliminated. Warwick Baker will compete against two finalists based in Minneapolis: Campbell Mithun Esty, owned by top managers and the Interpublic Group of Companies, and Carmichael Lynch.
The Driver's Mart billings were estimated at $75 million in the first three years of operations. Tom Shaver, a senior partner at J. D. Power & Associates in Agoura Hills, Calif., which is managing the review, said a decision was expected in mid-May.
In addition to the Cherry Coke change, Coca-Cola awarded its consolidated national media planning account to D'Arcy Masius Benton & Bowles in New York. The account had been divided among various agencies; though billings were not disclosed, trade publications estimated them at $350 million.
Mr. Bertini said D.M.B.& B., the worldwide creative agency for the Fanta soft-drink brand, was selected after a review that had been narrowed to that agency and another roster shop, the Leo Burnett Company in Chicago. Three others were eliminated earlier: Lowe & Partners/SMS in New York, a unit of Interpublic; McCann-Erickson New York, the Interpublic unit that continues to handle national media buying for Coca-Cola, and Wieden & Kennedy in Portland, Ore.
In another media consolidation, Novell Inc., the software company in Orem, Utah, has consolidated the media portion of its account, with billings estimated at more than $50 million, at the San Francisco office of Young & Rubicam Inc. The media duties had been divided between the Martha Felt Group in Salt Lake City, for the United States, and Y.& R. San Francisco, for overseas.
Y.& R. San Francisco, which also handles Novell's worldwide creative account, was awarded its additional assignment without a review.
Turning to Sears, Roebuck & Company, the Chicago-based retailer has selected the Burrell Communications Group in Chicago to handle advertising aimed at black consumers, which had been handled by Vince Cullers Advertising in Chicago for the last 26 years. Billings were estimated at $4 million to $5 million. To work for Sears, Burrell will relinquish a similar assignment from the Kmart Corporation, which it had handled since 1991.
Burrell was awarded the Sears assignment after a review in which the other participants were not disclosed. Adweek listed them as Cullers, Equinox in Chicago, the Mingo Group in New York and the Uniworld Group in New York.
Teresa Fearon, a spokeswoman for Kmart in Troy, Mich., said the assignment that had been handled by Burrell, with billings that were not disclosed, would be placed in review. A decision is expected by early summer, she added.
As for Wired, the magazine has named Mad Dogs and Englishmen in New York as the first agency to handle assignments like direct mail, direct response television and advertising on the World Wide Web. Billings have not been determined. Mad Dogs was selected by the Wired parent, Wired Ventures in San Francisco, without a formal review.
Finally, the Topps Company in New York, which markets trading cards, gum and candy, has begun a review for its account after Grey Advertising in New York resigned after two and a half years because, the agency said, Topps was redirecting advertising spending into promotions. Though billings were not disclosed, Advertising Age estimated spending at $2 million.
Marty Appel, a spokesman for Topps, said the company had hired Advice and Advisors in New York to manage the review.
