HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices End Lower in Dull Trading

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities fell in dull trading yesterday, despite a report that showed rising interest rates severely hampering housing sales.
While the report pointed to moderating economic growth, market participants remained concerned that data due to be released this weak would portray an economy growing much faster, with inflation accelerating. 
 By late afternoon prices had recouped some of their losses in thin activity, helped along to some extent by lower commodity prices, which eased some inflation fears.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond fell 19/32 of a percentage point, to 89 3/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 6.84 percent, from 6.79 percent on Friday.
Interest rates at yesterday's regular weekly bill auction were slightly higher in what traders described as a mediocre auction, with only average investor interest. The rate for a three-month bill was 5 percent, up from 4.97 percent last week, while the average rate for a six-month security was 5.08 percent, up from 5.02 percent last week.
Bond prices fell even though the housing sales report was weaker than expected. The Commerce Department said sales of new homes fell 7.6 percent in March, to an annual rate of 672,000, much weaker than the revised 1.3 percent sales decline in February. It was the lowest rate for monthly sales since May of last year.
Analysts said the market's limited reaction to yesterday's weak housing report could have been caused by the results of a survey of the American Production and Inventory Control Society, which indicated that overall manufacturing activity picked up sharply in April from March. The housing decrease had also been largely expected since interest rates had headed higher since February.
Josh Stiles, senior bond strategist at I.D.E.A., a consulting firm, said that while the market was becoming more convinced that the economy's housing sector would slow, the market had yet to come to grips with the fact that the manufacturing could pick up.
"The economy is making a subtle shift in direction, but it will not be an explosion," he said. "However, it appears that the best of the inflation news is behind us, and of course the market is going to be concerned -- but it's nothing to panic about."
The market's focus this week will be on the April employment report, which will be released on Friday. Other likely market distractions this week include the Treasury's expected announcement tomorrow about the size of its next quarterly refunding.

HEADLINE: The Pro-Immigration Lobby

BYLINE: By Roy Beck; Roy Beck is author of "The Case Against Immigration."

DATELINE: ARLINGTON, Va.

 BODY:
The forces in favor of reducing legal as well as illegal immigration roared into Washington like a speeding train last year. The momentum appeared to be unstoppable. Polls showed that Americans overwhelmingly favored reductions in the number of legal migrants. Support came from a bipartisan reform commission led by the late Barbara Jordan, from President Clinton and from Republican and Democratic lawmakers, who introduced bills to change the current policy, which brought in 720,000 legal immigrants last year.
But in the last month, both houses of Congress rejected or sidetracked these reductions. In the end, almost nobody was for them -- except the American people. 
 By contrast, Congress has managed to agree on some bars to illegal immigration. The Senate is expected to pass a bill this week that will beef up border enforcement and impose greater penalties on those who violate immigration law.
Puzzled Americans -- fewer than 6 percent of whom support current policies, according to a Roper poll released in February -- may wonder why Congress was able to take modest steps against illegal immigration but backed down from limiting the legal influx. People need look no further than the national organizations representing them.
Groups from across the spectrum -- business, labor, liberal Protestants, the religious right, Jews, Catholics, blacks, Hispanics, Republicans, Democrats -- have been on Capitol Hill supporting the current legal-immigration system. Few issues have created such a gap between the views of the people and the actions of their leaders -- who often go their own way when horse-trading in Washington.
Take business executives and professionals. The Roper poll found that they opposed the present high legal immigration levels by 11 to 1. But their leaders and trade groups like the National Association of Manufacturers have been as relentless in protecting the legal importation of foreign labor as the robber barons were a century ago. Well-connected executives -- representing everything from agribusiness to computer engineering -- flooded Washington to fight to maintain this loose labor market, which has helped depress wages for American workers for two decades.
The corporate lobby has been even more powerful than appears at first glance, as we saw after a key House vote on March 20. With a switch in only 28 votes, the House could have cut the continuing entry of citizens' adult relatives, the largest source of cheap foreign labor. But both Mr. Clinton and the Christian Coalition sent last-minute declarations of opposition to the cuts.
The reason for the President's reversal remains vague. But the Christian Coalition's action was widely attributed to pressure from Grover Norquist, head of Americans for Tax Reform, who brought together a network of conservative groups. His tax group's initially puzzling opposition became more understandable after the media disclosed that since February he has been a registered lobbyist for Microsoft, whose industry employs many immigrants.
And labor? The A.F.L.-C.I.O. legislative director, after meeting with immigrant labor leaders opposed to tighter rules on family reunification, joined the business lobby in urging Congress to defeat those provisions.
It was a strange way to represent laborers, native or foreign-born. Roper found that 58 percent of blue-collar workers want to cut the current level of legal and illegal immigrants to below a total of 100,000 a year. Only 2 percent of workers -- and 4 percent of all Hispanic people nationwide -- support today's level.
But group after group in Washington seems to deny that many of its grass-roots members feel that mass immigration has hurt their wages, environment, schools and the social fabric of their communities.
In fact, some members of Congress may be confused about what Americans want. Or perhaps lawmakers have decided that it is riskier to ignore lobbyists than to ignore voters.
In 1981, a bipartisan Federal commission on immigration warned that the public interest was being subverted by the lobbying of two groups -- conservative business interests and a liberal coalition of religious, immigrant and civil liberties organizations. The commission rejected arguments of those lobbyists about the need for high immigration levels.
Then, as now, Congress has listened to lobbyists more than public opinion. If Americans want a different immigration policy, they may need to challenge their own religious, economic and political organizations.

HEADLINE: Sales of New Homes Fell 7.6% in March

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
Rising interest rates bit substantially into sales of new homes in March, driving them down by 7.6 percent, to an annual rate of 672,000, the slowest since last May, the Departments of Commerce and Housing and Urban Development reported today.
As a result, the already-lofty inventories of unsold houses climbed to the highest point since 1980. 
 But while the pace last month was weaker than most analysts had expected, industry specialists took comfort in upward revisions for January and February and noted that even the March pace was brisker than the monthly average for 1994 and 1995.
"Sales remain relatively healthy," said Joseph Liro, an economist at CIBC Wood Gundy in New York. "We expect new-home sales to remain in a 650,000-to-700,000 range for the next several months."
The March decline for new homes was in sharp contrast to a report on Thursday that sales of previously owned homes jumped 6.9 percent during March, a rise attributed in part to buyers' taking advantage of relatively low mortgage rates.
The divergent results mainly reflected the fact that deals involving existing homes are tallied only when completed, while new houses are counted as sold as soon as contracts are signed. Since financing and other aspects of a deal usually take a couple of months to complete, the figures on new houses are considered a better indicator of current market activity.
Mortgage rates have risen rapidly, from an average 7.08 percent on 30-year conventional mortgages in February to 7.62 percent in March, according to the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation. Increases continued in April, with last week's rate reaching 7.92 percent.
"The higher rates certainly kept home sales from being as high as in January and February, but sales are still pretty strong," said Eileen Neely, senior economist at the Federal National Mortgage Association. She predicted only a modest dampening of the market in coming months as renewed economic growth, while putting upward pressure on rates, also generated more jobs.
Sherry Cooper, chief economist at the Nesbitt Burns securities firm in Toronto, called today's figures "the first indications that the run-up in long-term interest rates is beginning to crimp housing."
In an unusual breach of security, the figures were released about five minutes prematurely when an inexperienced Census Bureau employee misread the clock and put the report on a Government electronic bulletin board before the 10 A.M. designated time, according to Jane Callen, a Government spokeswoman.
One organization that noticed was Stone & McCarthy Research Associates, which at 9:58 posted a notice about the data to its clients on the Internet.
Raymond Stone said in a telephone interview from his firm's offices in Princeton, N.J., that he detected no noticeable market reaction before the official release time and that his firm made no trading profits from the advance information since it neither trades securities nor manages money.
But he also said he regarded the information as "right in the public domain" and suggested that it would not be improper for market operators to profit from such an error.
Home sales slumped most in the Northeast, which has long had the least home-building activity of any region. Sales there dropped 21.2 percent in March to a seasonally adjusted annual pace of 52,000, erasing nearly all of February's advance, the report showed.
Activity in the South dropped 10 percent, to a 296,000 annual pace, while sales in the Midwest were about unchanged at 116,000.
Sales in the West eased 4.1 percent, to a 208,000 pace, but Ms. Cooper said "the big story" was the continued strength in that region. Last month's sales of new homes in the West were 25 percent above those of March 1995.
At the end of the month, builders held 379,000 finished but unsold houses, the highest level in 16 years. At the current sales pace, 6.8 months would be needed to deplete this supply, up from 5.8 months in February and the highest since April 1995.
The revisions for earlier months lifted January's sales pace by 20,000, to 729,000, and February's by 27,000, to 727,000, the report also showed. December sales were trimmed 2,000, to a pace of 683,000.
The median sales price of March contracts was $138,000, unchanged from February.

GRAPHIC: Graphs: "New-Home Sales" tracks new private homes sold, at seasonally adjusted annual rate, from October 1994 through March 1996; "Prices of New Homes" tracks median price for new homes over the same period. (Source: Commerce Department)

HEADLINE: U.S. Strains With China: 1 Exception

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
Despite their strained relations, the United States and China have embarked on a program of environmental cooperation to address problems like rapid urbanization, pollution from energy consumption and the changing agricultural patterns of an increasingly prosperous population.
White House officials said a three-day meeting of senior agency officials from both nations at the White House last week was especially noteworthy because it occurred at a time when Chinese-American relations have been strained by differences over Taiwan and other trade and security issues. 
 They said the talks were the kind of step that Secretary of State Warren Christopher has been calling for in an attempt to put more focus on global environmental concerns as a central element of foreign policy.
"It is significant that this is moving forward with high-level backing when there are so many things going wrong with the relationship," an Administration official said. "The environment is a priority that supersedes those problems."
The meetings involved dozens of fairly senior officials -- assistant secretaries and vice ministers -- from agencies involved in energy, agriculture, environment, urban affairs, disaster relief and other such technical and economic fields. Hui Yong-zheng, vice minister of the State Science and Technology Commission, led the Chinese delegation. Mr. Clinton's science advisor, Jack Gibbons, attended about half the three-day session, and Vice President Al Gore also took part briefly.
White House officials said Mr. Gore and Prime Minister Li Peng consider the talks a preliminary step toward a permanent, high-level relationship aimed at what specialists call "sustainable development." That philosophy assumes that economic growth, especially in countries that are advancing rapidly, like China, must be carefully managed to ensure that such growth does not impose unacceptable costs in environmental degradation and depletion of resources.
The talks were not intended to be negotiating sessions over particular projects or decisions, such as whether the Export-Import Bank of the United States will subsidize loans to American companies for construction of the Three Gorges Dam on the Yangtze River, American officials said. That enormous project has been strongly criticized by environmentalists, and the Administration has recommended against the bank providing financing.
An American participant in the talks said he was impressed with the depth of understanding displayed by the Chinese over the complexities of balancing economic and environmental considerations, the potential for achieving both sets of objectives at once, and the importance of doing so. Chinese delegates emphasized that China's new five-year plan, the Government's central declaration of policies, is a turning point in its explicit and detailed commitments to sustainable development.
China, with its huge population, rapid economic growth and accelerating urbanization, has long been recognized both as a nation with serious environmental problems and as a major market for environmental technologies, an area in which the United States is a world leader.
Among the problems discussed at the meeting, according to American participants, were these:
*China's enormous demand for new electric-generating capacity, which is expected to be met primarily with coal-fired power plants. That causes two main kinds of problems: acid rain in China, and a big source of carbon dioxide, one of the greenhouse gases that threatens to cause global warming.
*China's agricultural needs, which are shifting as the population eats more meat, as China imports more grain, and as arable land is converted to industrial and urban uses.
*The difficulties of housing, transportation and pollution control in growing coastal cities, and whether they can be relieved by planning more growth in smaller cities.
"The Chinese appear virtually certain to emerge as one of the largest economies on earth in the next century," an American official said. "With growth of 10 percent a year, their environmental problems can accelerate rapidly, and that can have a profound impact."
Both nations, in different ways, are major players in deciding whether emissions of greenhouse gases can be controlled in the century ahead. The United States is by far the biggest emitter of carbon dioxide. It could be the leader in technologies to cut those emissions, but so far has failed to live up to its pledge to quickly cut its emissions, which continue to grow. China is the leading example of a fast-growing new contributor to the atmospheric burden of carbon dioxide.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: ADVERTISING;
Business Groups Hope Ads in Pivotal Electoral States Sway Clinton on Product Liability Bill

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
In a coordinated advertising campaign running in states critical to the Presidential race, several groups financed by business are accusing President Clinton of carrying water for greedy trial lawyers.
The groups say they are advertising in hopes of persuading Mr. Clinton to sign legislation limiting damage awards in lawsuits over faulty products. Mr. Clinton has said he intends to veto the legislation. 
 But Democrats see the advertisements in states like Michigan and Ohio as a proxy assault to weaken President Clinton on behalf of Senator Bob Dole, the presumptive Republican nominee, who for the moment cannot afford to advertise. Mr. Dole and Speaker Newt Gingrich plan to hold a joint briefing on Tuesday to call attention to the legislation before they send it to the White House.
The bill, which would cover product liability lawsuits in all Federal and state courts, would limit punitive damages in most cases to $250,000, or two times damages, whichever is greater. Mr. Clinton has said the measure does not provide enough protection for consumers.
Most of the advertisements sponsored by the business groups say that trial lawyers have donated heavily to Mr. Clinton's campaign.
"Even though the President says he puts people first, he plans to veto the product liability bill," says the announcer in one advertisement, paid for by the United States Chamber of Commerce and the National Association of Wholesaler-Distributors. "Who's first now, Mr. President?"
Words flashed on the screen ask: "People first? Or trial lawyers first?"
Frank Coleman, a spokesman for the Chamber of Commerce, said the advertisements were taking advantage of the political season to reach Mr. Clinton.
"His mind-set is in the electoral college, so that's where we're going to drive a stake through his heart," Mr. Coleman said.
The groups behind the advertisements say they represent hundreds of thousands of businesses and individuals. But Democrats see an effort to help Mr. Dole by big business, and in particular tobacco companies, which together with manufacturers and insurers are likely beneficiaries of the legislation limiting damage awards.
"This is Presidential politics, pure and simple, and the driving force behind this is the tobacco companies," said David Eichenbaum, communications director for the Democratic National Committee.
The Chamber of Commerce and the wholesalers association began running their advertisement on April 23. They plan to take it off the air on Tuesday, said Dirk Van Dongen, president of the National Association of Wholesaler-Distributors. He said that, depending on what Mr. Clinton does, it was conceivable that "we will ramp up again in key electoral states on the issue."
He said the advertising campaign was not coordinated with the Dole campaign or the Republican National Committee, but was coordinated with other groups favoring the product liability legislation.
As part of that joint effort, a second advertisement, paid for by Citizens for a Sound Economy, also began turning up last Tuesday in most of the same states. It presents a woman, Peggy Phillips, who says the manufacturer of a medical device she needs may stop making it "because of the threat of frivolous product liability lawsuits."
Addressing the camera, she says, "Mr. President, won't you please sign this bill?"
Both advertisements were produced by the firm Robinson Lerer Sawyer Miller. And all three business associations were among the groups sponsoring a print advertisement, which ran last Thursday in newspapers here and in state capitals, suggesting that Mr. Clinton was beholden to trial lawyers.
Citizens for a Sound Economy has received large donations from tobacco companies, said John Canham-Clyne, research director of Congress Watch, an arm of the organization Public Citizen, which opposes the product liability legislation. He said it was also supported by foundations associated with the Koch family, industrialists based in Wichita, Kan., who have donated heavily to Mr. Dole.
William S. Armistead, vice president for campaign development at Citizens for a Sound Economy, said, "We don't reveal who our members are and our donors are."

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
A Course In History, Not Econ

BYLINE: By WILLIAM C. RHODEN 

 BODY:
THIS season marks the 20th anniversary of Kareem Abdul-Jabbar's decision to pull up stakes in Milwaukee and move west to Los Angeles. Abdul-Jabbar embarked on a 14-year run that included five National Basketball Association championships, one of the great legacies for winning in professional sports.
With players turning professional out of high school and leaving college early -- as Marcus Camby and Kobe Bryant did yesterday -- this isn't an anniversary too many care about. But it's something Shaquille O'Neal should keep in the back of his mind as he contemplates a wild free-agent market this summer. 
 Abdul-Jabbar completed four years at U.C.L.A., earned a degree in history and was regarded as intellectual as well as athletically gifted. At that time, college players couldn't even turn pro early; when they began to do so in the early 1970's, they did it with a "hardship" label that carried a negative connotation.
By the time O'Neal came along in 1992, the early exit was in vogue, and something -- perhaps a certain competitive pride -- was lost along the way. In an odd way, this might contribute to Orlando's not winning the N.B.A. championship this season and not really caring whether it does.
Perhaps, along with all the other things young players sell and market, the competitive instinct, the urge to move beyond just being O.K., has been bought as well.
What besides money was the motivation behind Kevin Garnett's turning professional out of high school last year and Bryant's heading straight from Lower Merion High School to the N.B.A. this year?
An assistant coach in the Big Ten bemoaned the fact that players under so much pressure to reach the pros act as if possessions are life and death. They watch sports highlights on cable to see how players at other programs are doing. Who's getting the minutes? Whose getting the publicity?
The element of passion for the game, the intrinsic joy of playing the sport, has been replaced by the quest to sign and spend.
O'Neal came into the pro game early himself, leaving Louisiana State after his junior year to sign a $40 million contract with the Magic. When it expires on July 1, the Magic will offer the entire state to have him stay. Orlando could make him the richest player in the history of basketball. "It's been a good marriage," Pat Williams, the team's general manager, said yesterday. "We want him to stay, but this is going to be the summer of the great unknown."
The greatest unknown may be what O'Neal wants for himself -- beyond the riches he'll be showered with during the free-agent chase.
O'Neal suggests on one advertisement that he wants a championship ring. He has also compared himself and Anfernee Hardaway -- his point guard teammate -- to Abdul-Jabbar and Magic Johnson, saying, "We're the Kareem and Magic of the late 90's." But what critics wonder is whether O'Neal wants to play like Kareem and Magic or win like them. Can Shaq achieve that goal in Orlando? Or will the Magic be a young team that never becomes great?
"They're not going to win," said Oscar Robertson, who teamed with Abdul-Jabbar on the Bucks in 1971 to win an N.B.A. championship. "They are a lethargic team. For Shaq's future and for the future of really super big-time basketball in Los Angeles, he should go to L.A. If I were the Lakers, I'd go all out to get him."
Maybe it's not that simple. Shaq shatters backboards. Shaq dunks over mountains. Shaq puts fear in opponents' hearts. But does Shaq have the courage to be a Laker?
When Abdul-Jabbar left for the Lakers in 1975, he was following a path that had been traveled by two of the great centers in N.B.A. history: Wilt Chamberlain, who went from the Warriors to the Lakers and won a championship in 1972, and George Mikan, the league's first great center, who joined the Lakers following the 1947 season and helped them win five titles.
Since Abdul-Jabbar's retirement in 1989, there has been a void in Los Angeles and, by extension, on the West Coast. Shaq can fill that void. Or, he can build the addition on his home in Orlando and stay put.
Shaq, like the talented young players who scurry to leave school ever earlier, will be wealthy wherever he goes. But he'll be richer for the experience of going to Los Angeles and making a mark.
Mikan has his legacy. Chamberlain has his, and Abdul-Jabbar his.
At the moment, all Shaq has is trinkets and Disney World. He owes himself more.

HEADLINE: Court Overturns Damage Award In Downing of Korean Airliner

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
A Federal appeals court in Ohio today overturned a judgment that had awarded about $15 million to the relatives of five people who were killed in the 1983 downing of a Korean Airlines jetliner, and experts said the decision might be applied to about two dozen cases still pending 13 years after the incident.
Lower courts had awarded the money to the relatives for pain and suffering of the victims, and loss of society and grief of the survivors. But the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, in Cincinnati, ruled that the law that should apply is the Death on the High Seas Act, which does not allow such non-economic damages. Korean Airlines was the defendant in the suits. 
 "If it had happened 20 miles further inland, over Sakhalin Island, you could collect everything," said Hans Ephriamson-Abt, the chairman of the American Association for Families of KAL-007 Victims. His 23-year-old daughter Alice was on board; her case is pending in New York. The decision is not binding outside the circuit, comprising Kentucky, Michigan, Ohio and Tennessee, but is likely to influence about two dozen cases pending around the country.
The plane was shot down over the Sea of Japan on Sept. 1, 1983, by a Soviet fighter plane after it blundered into Soviet airspace while on the way to Seoul from Anchorage, Alaska. All 269 people aboard were killed.
The case has had a tortured legal history. The Warsaw Convention, which generally governs claims in international air travel, usually limits airlines' liability to $75,000. But in 1989, the Federal District Court for the District of Columbia ruled that the airline had shown "wilful misconduct," voiding that limit.
But in January, the Supreme Court ruled in another case arising from the crash that the Death on the High Seas Act should apply.
"They have taken away just about every way to recover," said Gerald E. Thurswell, a lawyer who represented the estate of Margaret Zerif, an educator and author. "The court exercised its political wisdom in protecting the economic interest of Korean Airlines."

HEADLINE: Provident Agrees to Acquire Paul Revere for $1.2 Billion

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
The Provident Companies, the No. 2 seller of individual disability insurance policies, agreed yesterday to buy the No. 1 insurer in the field in a deal that would give it more than one-third of the market.
The $1.2 billion deal would eliminate about 600 jobs, give a big Swiss insurer a toehold in this niche and may presage a big write-off on money-losing policies sold by the insurer that is being acquired, the Paul Revere Company. 
 Last year Provident's insurance units had $2.6 billion in premium revenues and Paul Revere had $1.5 billion. About 57 percent of the combined $4.1 billion in premiums came from disability policies that were sold to individuals.
Provident's next largest competitor, the UNUM Corporation, has about 10 percent of the market.
Paul Revere has been controlled since 1985 by Textron Inc., the maker of Bell helicopters and Cessna planes. Textron acquired the insurer when it bought Avco Financial Services, which makes high interest rate loans to people whose employment and credit histories make them too risky for bank loans.
Textron is swapping its 83 percent stake in Paul Revere for $750 million cash and $225 million of Provident stock. Textron said it would record a $90 million loss on the sale.
James F. Hardymon, Textron's chairman and chief executive, said the sale "reinforces Textron's strategy to focus on its core manufacturing and finance businesses." He said that up to half the proceeds might be used to pay down the company's more than $10 billion of debt or to buy back stock.
Paul Revere's remaining public shareholders will get $26 in cash for each share, Provident stock worth $26, or $20 in cash and Provident stock worth $6.
Paul Revere's shares had closed at $26 each on Friday, so there was no premium in the deal. The shares slipped 12.5 cents yesterday, to $25.875, on the New York Stock Exchange; Textron's shares fell $1, to $86.125. Meanwhile, Provident's shares jumped $2.50, to $34.
After the consolidation, Provident expects to cut $70 million in annual costs, a 15 percent reduction, said Tom White, a spokesman. He estimated that 600 of the combined insurers' 5,100 jobs would be cut.
To finance the deal, Provident is getting a $300 million infusion of cash from the Zurich Insurance Group of Switzerland, which is buying a 15.2 percent stake in Provident under an agreement allowing it to increase the stake up to 40 percent.
Three analysts said the capital infusion might help pay for write-downs on money-losing individual disability policies sold by Paul Revere in the late 1980's.
Provident, which is based in Chattanooga, Tenn., announced a $423 million write-off on individual disability policies in December 1993. Paul Revere, which is based in Worchester, Mass., has not taken a similar write-off, said John M. Hannon, an analyst at Derby Securities.
"These companies got in trouble because they sold policies that could not be canceled," Mr. Hannon said.
Provident, Paul Revere and other companies have previously said that they had to pay claims on ailments not even known when the policies were written and that many disability claims resulted in larger than expected benefits payments.
The deal drew mixed responses from insurance ratings agencies, which had ranked both companies in the upper tiers, indicating a strong likelihood that they would be able over the long haul to pay claims.
A. M. Best called the deal "positive." But Moody's Investor Services announced it was considering downgrading Provident's ratings because of increased leverage and the general outlook for disability policies. Moody's said it was considering upgrading its rating on Paul Revere's life insurance unit. Standard & Poor's placed two Paul Revere units on its Creditwatch last week, noting that Provident had a slightly lower rating than Paul Revere.

HEADLINE: Gore Urges Cable Industry to Take On the Bells

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, April 29

 BODY:
The cable television industry is picking up some friends in high places, just when it needs them most.
Vice President Al Gore said today in a speech here to the industry's national conference that cable companies had displayed "good judgment, vision and a willingness to compromise" in their lobbying for telecommunications legislation. His remarks constituted a startling turnabout for a politician who was once one of cable's most strident critics and had led the battle to clamp down on cable rates in the 1980's. 
 In the wake of deregulation, however, the cable industry seems far less able to play the predator than a decade ago. Indeed, the competition unleashed by the new Telecommunications Act -- and the two recent merger announcements by four regional Bell companies -- is quickly putting cable executives on the defensive.
Because the Clinton Administration lobbied hard for the telecommunications law, it now requires that the market deliver the kind of freewheeling, free-market competition that deregulation was supposed to usher in. And so, Mr. Gore's speech amounted to a pep rally for the cable industry, which the Administration is counting on to give consumers a true telecommunications alternative.
Mr. Gore -- who as a senator once referred to John C. Malone, the powerful head of the cable giant Tele-Communications Inc. as "Darth Vader" -- today exhorted the industry to take on the telephone companies as aggressively as they could.
"You are the second wire that passes virtually every American home," Mr. Gore said in his speech to the National Cable Television Association. "Use that second wire to make available to America a package of services, including Internet access, that is affordable to every citizen."
For several years, cable executives have predicted that they would soon offer a panoply of communications services, including local telephone service and high-speed data delivery. But so far technological snags and economic constraints have prevented the cable companies from establishing much of a beachhead in either business.
And the phone companies are unlikely to make the cable industry's job any easier.
The pair of proposed multibillion-dollar Bell mergers that have been announced since President Clinton signed the Telecommuniations Act in February -- those of SBC Communications Inc. and Pacific Telesis Group, and the Nynex and Bell Atlantic corporations -- have focused almostly entirely on local and long-distance telephone service. And because the Bell companies seem determined to thrive by dominating their core telephone business, rather than branching into video services, some analysts and cable executives said that they would be even more ferocious about controlling access to their monopoly local markets.
"The toughest nut to crack is going to be the incumbent local telephone business," said James O. Robbins, the chief executive of Cox Enterprises' Cox Communications division, which is the nation's fifth-largest cable operator, with 3.2 million subscribers.
Cox is one of three cable operators that allied with the Sprint Corporation two years ago with a mission of building a national wired and wireless phone service, with cable networks providing the direct links to homes and businesses.
But that alliance has been unraveling. And just today the largest of those cable partners, Tele-Communications, served notice that it would choose its local phone markets selectively. The company said that it had filed for permission to offer local phone service in West Hartford, Conn., Arlington Heights, Ill., and San Francisco -- without Sprint or the other cable operators.
Greg Crosby, the vice president of marketing at the Tele-Communications telephone division, said that his company and Sprint had been unable to agree on which would pay for the costly upgrades of the cable network to enable it to carry switched phone traffic. Mr. Malone, the chief executive of TCI, has said he doubts that it makes economic sense to offer wired telephone service to each of his company's 13.7 million cable subscribers.
Cable operators who do go it alone are encountering other problems. Time Warner Inc., the nation's second-largest cable company, filed for permission in October 1994 to offer local phone service in Ohio. But Time Warner has been hobbled by a protracted squabble in that state with the Ameritech Corporation, the regional Bell company there. The dispute involves the rates that Ameritech charges to complete Time Warner calls to Ameritech customers.
The Ohio Public Service Commission imposed "interconnection" terms that were supposed to settle the matter, but Ameritech sued the commission rather than accept the terms. Thomas Morrow, the president of Time Warner Communications, said the company was encountering similar hurdles in Texas from the Bell company there, SBC Communications.
Given these issues, it is not surprising that cable executives spent most of their time today dwelling on other opportunities, like high-speed data delivery and digital television. The General Instrument Corporation and Motorola Inc. are among the manufacturers announcing ambitious plans here at the convention to ship new cable modems and digital set-top convertor boxes.
General Instrument's digital convertor boxes, designed to improve picture and sound quality while increasing the number of channels, are almost two years late, cable executives said. Meanwhile, complications in the development of cable modems have forced major cable operators like Tele-Communications to delay plans to offer high-speed access to the Internet and commercial on-line services.
Still, some cable executives seized on the anticipated announcements as proof that the industry was finally delivering on its lofty promises.
"I believe that this show may begin to put an end to the all skepticism," said Brian L. Roberts, the president of the Comcast Corporation and the chairman of the National Cable Television Association. "It will serve as evidence that we are finally doing what we've said we were going to do."
Mr. Gore, for his part, referred lightheartedly to his old disputes with the industry, noting that cable executives might perceive him as "Washington's answer to Darth Vader."
Still, the Vice President was probably less controversial than the other big name that the association tried -- unsuccessfully -- to lure to the conference: Jim Carrey, the actor who plays a psychotic cable repairman in the soon-to-be-released movie "Cable Guy."

HEADLINE: U.S. Defeated In Its Appeal Of Trade Case

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
The United States lost its final appeal today in the first major case brought before the newly created World Trade Organization. A three-judge panel ruled that regulations issued under the Clean Air Act discriminated against foreign oil refiners.
The practical effect of the ruling is that the Environmental Protection Agency will almost certainly be forced to rewrite some of the rules concerning the environmental standards for imported gasoline or face trade sanctions from the countries that filed the complaint, Venezuela and Brazil. Imported gasoline accounts for only about 5 percent of gasoline sold in the United States, but about 20 percent of gas sold in the New York area. 
 The changes seem unlikely to have a major effect on the environment. But the political significance of the decision may be far larger. Senator Bob Dole, the presumptive Republican nominee for President, has long argued that the trade accord that created the trade organization in late 1994 as an international arbiter, runs the risk of surrendering sovereignty over American laws to foreign judges.
Today's rulings bolster his argument that the international organization can effectively force the United States to rewrite its rules. Mr. Dole's office said today that it was still reviewing the decision and had no comment on its contents.
The judges -- from the Philippines, Japan and New Zealand -- concluded that standards set by the Environmental Protection Agency constituted "unjustifiable discrimination" because they set different rules for foreign producers than for domestic refiners. They found that the rules were a "disguised restriction on international trade."
In a brief statement today, the acting United States trade representative, Charlene Barshefsky, said that she was "disappointed that the practical result of this case remains unchanged," but "gratified" that the appellate panel ruled for the United States on a number of technical points. Administration officials noted that the panel acknowledged that countries had the right to control air pollution or take other steps to protect the environment -- as long as the rules applied equally to domestic and foreign companies.
The United States faces another potential conflict after the World Trade Organization threatened to scuttle talks over a global telecommunications agreement that faces a deadline of midnight on Tuesday. American negotiators in Geneva said they were not yet satisfied with market-opening offers from Asian nations. The accord among 53 nations would regulate the $500 billion global telecommunications services and equipment industry.
Jeffrey Lang, the deputy United States trade representative and top American negotiator at the talks, said that after American officials made their objections clear, they had not received improved offers from other nations.
Today's decision on gasoline regulations, announced at the World Trade Organization's headquarters in Geneva, was the first ever handed down by the organization's recently appointed Appellate Body, which is essentially the Supreme Court of international trade. It is charged with reviewing decisions issued by panels that hear trade complaints brought by any of the 120 nations that have signed the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade.
The trade organization's judicial authority has long been a main sticking point for economic nationalists who opposed America's entry into the worldwide trade accord. Opposition has created a disparate group of critics, including consumer advocates like Ralph Nader, trade hawks like Ross Perot and Patrick J. Buchanan and conservatives like Senators Jesse Helms of North Carolina and Strom Thurmond of South Carolina. They argue that environmental protections, laws against the purchase of goods made by child labor and even steps taken to cut down on drug imports can be challenged as trade barriers.
The Administration has argued that those concerns are overblown: The world organization does not have the power to strike down laws, only to impose trade penalties.
After weeks of haggling with Administration officials over that issue in 1994, Mr. Dole reached an agreement with Mr. Clinton to support the trade accord -- thus assuring its passage -- as long as a commission was appointed to review all decisions that go against the United States. If the commission concludes that three such decisions are capricious, Congress will automatically be asked to vote on whether to pull the United States out of the trade group.
But more than a year and a half later, the commission has yet to be established. The legislation creating it was held up by Senator Robert C. Byrd, the West Virginia Democrat and an opponent of the trade accord, who said that the appeals commission was not powerful enough.
At the time he struck the deal with Mr. Clinton -- and announced it in a ceremony in the Rose Garden -- Mr. Dole was criticized by the conservative wing of his party, which said he sold out to major corporate donors to his campaign, all supporters of the international accord. Indeed, he will have a hard time blaming Mr. Clinton for agreeing to American participation in the organization. In the end, it was his vote and the votes he brought with him that won the trade accord's passage in the Senate.
The dispute that led to today's decision began three years ago, when Venezuela asked the United States for permission to ship gasoline to the Northeast that probably produced more smog-emitting chemicals than American refiners were permitted to sell.
Under the Clean Air Act, domestic producers are required to meet standards based on the emissions of gasoline they produced in 1990. But many foreign producers did not keep equivalent records of their production then. So a different standard was created: They could ship only gasoline that met an average quality standard in the United States.
The foreign companies argued, successfully, that the rule effectively required them to meet a higher standard than some American concerns were required to meet, putting them at a disadvantage.

HEADLINE: Russia Given a Deal to Repay $40 Billion Debt

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, April 29

 BODY:
Russia got up to seven years' more breathing space today on repaying more than $40 billion of the accumulated debt it owes to foreign governments, possibly giving another lift to President Boris N. Yeltsin's prospects for re-election this summer.
Russian negotiators went into talks with 18 creditor nations here on Thursday asking for a rescheduling plan that would give them up to 25 years to pay off the credits, seven more years than the creditors' group has ever given before. 
 But after what some participants described as "difficult" talks at the French Finance Ministry that stretched through the entire weekend, the Russians were given a comprehensive debt rescheduling package that included most of what they had wanted.
The creditors, known collectively as the Paris Club, described the agreement as "financial support by the creditor countries to Russia's economic reform policies amounting to over $40 billion, which will be repaid over a period of up to 25 years."
But they said the agreement was intended to be final, replacing or incorporating the terms of three stretch-outs since 1993 on $29 billion of the debt in a way that should enable Russia to repay the entire $40 billion total with no further rescheduling or modification.
Christian Noyer, the chairman of the group, said the terms gave the Russians a grace period on repayment of principal until 2002. Until then, Russia would pay only interest, he said, explaining that it would now owe $2 billion this year, compared with $8 billion it would have owed without any rescheduling. The amounts will increase as time passes and as the Russian economy makes a successful transition to the free market.
"The Russian delegation described the economic and financial difficulties faced by its country and its strong determination to reduce the economic and financial imbalances," the creditors' communique said after the talks ended early today. Mr. Noyer said the agreement was conditioned on Russia carrying out the promises it made to the International Monetary Fund on March 26 to make cuts in its budget deficit and lift tariffs on imports, in exchange for a $10.2 billion loan.
"If the agreement with the I.M.F. falls, the agreement with the creditor countries falls as well," he said.
The Russian negotiators, led by Deputy Prime Minister Oleg D. Davydov, flew to London today for talks with commercial bank creditors who have agreed to a 25-year rescheduling of a further $25.5 billion in loans to Russia.
Germany is the largest government creditor to Russia, followed by France, Italy and the United States. All of them, plus Britain and other European countries, Canada, Japan and Australia, took part in the weekend talks.
France has made no secret of its desire to give Mr. Yeltsin every possible boost over the Communist opposition in his campaign for re-election.
President Jacques Chirac of France wants to include the Russian leader in political as well as economic discussions when leaders of the seven most powerful industrial democracies see him in Lyons during their annual summit talks at the end of June.
Mr. Chirac's advisers say the timing of the meeting should give Mr. Yeltsin another boost, just after the first round of the Russian elections and before the expected second round in July.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Government to Sell Oil From Strategic Reserve
President Clinton said the Government would begin to sell about 12 million barrels of oil from the Strategic Petroleum Reserve, in an effort to head off a surge in gasoline prices during an election year. The move was authorized in the Federal budget agreement reached last week and was envisioned as a way to cut the deficit. But the President attributed his decision to concern about the higher prices at the pump. They have risen to a national average of $1.27 for a gallon of regular from $1.16 a few weeks ago. [Page A1.]
 
A Plan to Pool Power Purchases
Electricity customers in California would benefit from a plan to form a pool to buy power competitively throughout the West. [D4.]
 
Microsoft Shifts on News Service
Microsoft is expected to announce that its MSN News service, which has been gathering and repackaging news articles for the Microsoft Network on-line operation, has been shifted to the World Wide Web, where it is available without a charge. [D4.]
 
Home Sales Plunge
Sales of new homes fell 7.6 percent in March as rising interest rates took their toll. As a result, inventories of new homes rose to the highest level since 1980. [D2.]
 
2 Insurers to Join Forces
Provident plans to acquire the Paul Revere Company for $1.2 billion in a merger of the two largest sellers of individual disability insurance. [D2.]
 
Testing a Low-Smoke Smoke
R. J. Reynolds Tobacco has begun to invite smokers in Chattanooga, Tenn., to participate in a test of the company's nearly smokeless cigarette, called Eclipse. [D10.]
 
Boeing's Earnings Hurt by Strike
Boeing's earnings fell 34 percent in the first quarter because of fewer aircraft deliveries as a result of the strike last year. [D8.]
 
MCA Steps Up Fight With Viacom
MCA sued Viacom, contending that it broke their ownership contract for the USA Network by starting a new cable television channel, called Nick at Nite's TV Land. [D7.]
 
The USA Network is trying to capitalize on the fact that its schedule, with a greater variety of shows than most other cable channels, more closely resembles that of a network. [C13.]
 
MCI Bundles Services
MCI Communications announced a one-stop-shopping approach to marketing, saying it would offer clients several communications services, from long distance to paging to Internet access, on a single bill. [D5.]
 
Rockwell Tries to Shift Its Sights
Rockwell International, the maker of the B-1B bomber, has been trying to reduce its reliance on military contracts. So it has put out the word that its military businesses are for sale, but industry experts say there is no deal on the radar screens. Market Place. [D10.]
 
Lawyers Push to Get Around Law
Several trial lawyers have started an effort to use a California ballot initiative to circumvent a law enacted by Congress last year that restricts their ability to bring lawsuits when a company's stock price drops suddenly. [A13.]
 
G.E. Seen in Talks on Buying Space
General Electric is in talks with a Goldman, Sachs investor group to buy the space that G.E. leases in Rockefeller Center, people familiar with the discussions said. [D4.]
 
Dailies Losing Circulation
Most of the country's biggest daily newspapers lost circulation in the six months ended March 31 compared with the similar period a year ago. But the declines were generally smaller than in the past, and a number of papers had gains. [D8.]
 
Russia Reschedules Payments
Russia was given up to seven years' more breathing space on repaying more than $40 billion of the accumulated debt it owes foreign governments, possibly giving another lift to President Boris N. Yeltsin's prospects for re-election this summer. [A10.]

HEADLINE: Kansan Drove Woman to Bronx After Killing Her, the Police Say

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS 

 BODY:
After driving all the way from Kansas with his girlfriend's corpse by his side, a man surrendered at a Bronx police station house Sunday night and confessed to stabbing the woman to death, the police said.
The man, Angel Rivera, 33, formerly of the Bronx and now a resident of Topeka, Kan., told detectives of the 48th Precinct that he stabbed Trudy Poley, also of Topeka, six times during an argument Friday morning somewhere in Kansas, the police said. He told them he had discarded the murder weapon while on the way from Topeka to New York, they said. 
 Mr. Rivera drove east from Kansas apparently to seek advice from his sister and brother-in-law, who live in the Bronx.
"He talked to them, they asked about the girl, and then they advised him he had to come into the police," said Deputy Inspector Patrick Dudgeon, the operations commander for the Bronx detectives.
Inspector Dudgeon said Mr. Rivera, an unemployed restaurant worker, had a record of five or six arrests in Kansas for felonies.
When Mr. Rivera entered the station house, the police said, he told them that they would find his 30-year-old girlfriend lying dead on the passenger seat of his 1993 Mazda.
In suggesting a possible motive for the slaying, the police said Mr. Rivera was apparently agitated over having recently lost his restaurant job.
Mr. Rivera was awaiting arraignment yesterday in Bronx Criminal Court. It was not immediately clear if Kansas authorities would seek extradition, said Charise Campbell, a spokeswoman for the Bronx District Attorney's office.

HEADLINE: McCall Is Balancing Books and Ambition

BYLINE: By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ 

DATELINE: ALBANY, April 29

 BODY:
H. Carl McCall, New York State's chief fiscal monitor and one of its most prominent Democrats, often couches criticism in the numbingly dull language of an auditor: a mix of statistics and bureaucratic minutiae.
But when he swept into an assembly hall here the other morning to address a crowd of public school teachers and students, there was suddenly a glimpse not of the accountant, but of a seasoned political brawler. 
 He quickly tore into the Republican Governor, George E. Pataki, comparing his policies to those of Newt Gingrich. Then, as the crowd nodded with approval, he derided a cut in the state's personal income tax as a break for "the Governor and his rich friends."
"Let me tell you about the Governor's tax cut," Mr. McCall said in a biting tone that drew hoots and laughter. "The Governor's tax cut is very good for everyone in this room who makes over $200,000 a year."
The change in his tone is unusual but easy to explain: Mr. McCall, the State Comptroller, is widely regarded as a likely Democratic candidate for governor, despite his insistence that he is not -- yet. Still, he does appear to be field-testing a possible run against Governor Pataki, while he has ruled out running for mayor of New York despite urging from city Democrats.
Mr. McCall has been traveling around the state regularly -- including upstate areas and New York City's suburbs, where his support has been weakest -- speaking to a variety of groups not always directly associated with his present post. They include college students and political clubs, labor union officials, churches and synagogues.
Mr. McCall also plans to send out 100,000 letters in the coming weeks soliciting campaign donations from individuals, a first effort to expand his list of contributors and thus make him less reliant on powerful corporations and lobbying groups, his aides say.
People in both major parties say that Mr. McCall would make a formidable candidate. As the only elected Democrat on the state level since the Republicans took the two other top offices in 1994, he has tried to use the Comptroller's platform to cast himself as a fiscal moderate who can be trusted with taxpayer money.
He routinely cites his skillful investing of the state's $75 billion pension fund -- a pitch he clearly hopes will sell among current and retired state employees, many of whom are upstate and suburban voters who might not otherwise vote for a black New York City Democrat.
Mr. McCall has also apparently tried to distance himself from such old Democratic allies as Mario M. Cuomo, the former Governor. In fact, he has issued a series of audits and financial reports that criticized the former administration's handling of various state affairs, drawing scorn from his former allies and unusual praise from some Republicans.
 
A Job of Moving To the Middle
"I think he's done an adroit job of moving to the middle," said State Senator Kemp Hannon, a Republican from Long Island. "He's picking the right issues and making sure that he targets very key constituencies. He's a leading candidate for governor. No question about it."
But as Mr. McCall considers challenging Governor Pataki in 1998, political analysts say, he must be careful not to seem too partisan in his role as the state's chief fiscal monitor. "It's going to be a real balancing act between preserving the neutrality of the Comptroller's office and maintaining a voice as a political leader with ambitions for higher office," said Dr. Lee M. Miringoff, the director of the Marist College Institute for Public Opinion in Poughkeepsie. "But Carl McCall is going to have to pull that off if he expects to climb the political ladder."
The Comptroller's office presents another challenge for Mr. McCall. It is a relatively low-profile position that historically has been a poor springboard to higher office. The last comptroller to become governor was Nathan L. Miller in 1921.
Furthermore, a poll released last week by the Quinnipiac College Polling Institute showed that Mr. McCall had a faint image around the state. Of the 715 registered voters surveyed April 18-23, 70 percent said they had not yet formed an opinion about him. The poll had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus 3.6 percentage points.
Mr. McCall has been the State Comptroller since 1993, when he was chosen by the Legislature, at Governor Cuomo's urging, to fill the unexpired term of Edward V. Regan, a Republican, who retired from the post after years of unsuccessful efforts to move on to a higher office. Mr. McCall was elected to the post the following year, becoming the first black candidate to win a statewide office in New York.
Mr. McCall (who dislikes being called by his first name, Herman) was born in the Roxbury section of Boston 60 years ago, the oldest of six children. His father left the family after losing his job as a railroad dining-car waiter, and the children were raised by their mother with the help of welfare.
 
No. 2 Official In U.N. Delegation
Mr. McCall was encouraged by a family friend from church to study at Dartmouth College. After serving in the Army briefly, he went to Andover Newton Theological School and was ordained a minister in the United Church of Christ. But instead of becoming a pastor, he moved to New York City, where he helped register voters in Harlem in the 1960's.
In 1974, he won a seat in the State Senate representing Harlem, and four years later President Jimmy Carter appointed him the second-ranking official in the United States delegation to the United Nations. He held a variety of jobs until 1985, when he was made a vice president of Citibank, where he stayed until he became State Comptroller.
As Comptroller, he is sole trustee of the nation's second-largest state pension fund, auditor of state agencies and local governments, and the chief arbiter of state budgeting and borrowing practices. Mr. McCall has drawn criticism from allies and foes alike during his tenure, as he has tried to demonstrate his independence from both parties.
He has infuriated Mr. Cuomo and the former Governor's supporters for the audits and reports accusing the Cuomo administration of mismanaging a number of state agencies. ("He's very unhappy," Mr. McCall said. "But I think he'll get over it.")
The Comptroller's most recent criticism was in a January audit that assailed the lending practices of two state agencies that tried to foster job growth under the former Governor. The report was released just as the Pataki administration was claiming that it had uncovered gross mismanagement of the two agencies during the Cuomo era.
Mr. Cuomo's allies immediately accused the Comptroller of being a political opportunist, noting that he had been a member of a board that had evaluated and approved loans that the audit criticized.
"He's taking unfair shots to disavow his past ties and the decisions he once agreed with," said David H. Peirez, a lawyer who was appointed by Mr. Cuomo to sit on the board of the Job Development Authority, one of the agencies in question.
"It's totally political," Mr. Peirez continued. "I have no doubt in my mind that he's trying to recreate himself to run for higher office."
Some Republicans have been just as critical.
The Pataki administration recently attacked Mr. McCall's decision to block its plan to move nearly 5,000 state-financed jobs from Democratic strongholds of New York City and Albany to Republican districts.
The administration said the move would have consolidated the state's data processing in vacant buildings to be leased from I.B.M. Mr. McCall questioned the fiscal soundness of the lease deal, opening him up to accusations from the Governor and his aides that he politicized his duties to curry favor with Democrats. But he did approve a scaled-down version of the plan.
 
'Equally Critical Of Pataki'
As Mr. McCall sees it, the attacks from both political sides show he is doing his work impartially.
"This job requires independence," he said. "If Mario Cuomo did something that I felt was inappropriate, then I was critical of him. And I've been equally critical of Pataki."
Mr. McCall keeps the kind of schedule that would exhaust men half his age, typically putting in 12-hour workdays packed with speaking engagements, news conferences, staff meetings and television interviews. He has an easy, approachable manner in public gatherings; in private, he can be surprisingly blunt, and at other times distant and hard to read.
Mr. McCall becomes impassioned when talking to groups about social issues like poverty and race, sometimes drawing from his own experiences. "When I arrived at Dartmouth College," he recently joked in a speech to a group of college students, "I was a member of a freshman class with the largest number of African-Americans ever: There were eight of us."
Not surprisingly, then, he includes among his list of accomplishments his efforts to urge companies in which the state invests to adopt social policies like affirmative action.
But Mr. McCall also keeps reminding people that his first duty has been to strengthen the state's economic foundation -- a stance that political analysts say may help give him the credibility to take on Republicans on one of their biggest issues: their claim that that they are more fiscally responsible than Democrats.
"The Comptroller's office is a good place for Carl McCall to be," said Hank Morris, a political consultant who works for Representative Charles E. Schumer, another potential gubernatorial candidate. "It gives him a chance to establish himself as a fiscal conservative."
For his part, Mr. McCall says it is too early to decide whether he will run for governor. "I'm focusing on doing this job and I'm trying to do this job well," he said. "I don't want to be distracted."

HEADLINE: A Campus Appetite for a la Carte;
Student Tastes Lead Yale to Consider Menus of Other Colleges

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ 

DATELINE: NEW HAVEN, April 24

 BODY:
Fifty years ago, Yale students, dressed in jackets and ties, were served dinner on fine china by waiters and waitresses in majestic Gothic dining halls. A typical menu in 1937 featured broiled loin lamb chops and tenderloin steak.
Now the university wants to open a food court with cuisine by Taco Bell, Pizza Hut and Subway. 
 While Yale and its dining hall workers have been grappling over the revamping of the school's food services -- a monthlong strike just ended with no agreement -- both sides acknowledge that students are dissatisfied with the current cafeteria program.
At colleges nationwide, officials have been scrambling to outdo each other with focaccia grills, 850-seat food courts, make-your-own Belgian waffles, candlelight dinners and around-the-clock service to satisfy the eating habits of today's college students.
"Students come to campus used to eating what they want when they want it and where they want it, and we have to adjust to that," said Emily Lloyd, executive vice president for administration at Columbia University, which is overhauling its dining halls. "The sort of 'Leave It to Beaver' household where everyone, at the same time, eats meat loaf, string beans and mashed potatos followed by apple crisp doesn't exist anymore."
At a time when many undergraduates consider breakfast a dirty word and college dietitians classify midnight snacks as a fourth meal, the traditional Yale food plan -- 21 meals a week at roughly 9 A.M, noon and 6 P.M. -- has become an anachronism.
"For me, the strike didn't make a difference," said one sophomore, Casey Jarrin, over a Greek salad at Yorkside Pizza and Restaurant at 11 P.M. "In two years here, I've had breakfast in the dining hall maybe three times. I end up going out to eat practically every night."
On that particular day, Ms. Jarrin had been up until 3:30 A.M. studying, woke up shortly before a 1 P.M. class, grabbed a bagel with butter on the side at 4 P.M., and then bought a few slices of pizza and frozen yogurt at 6:30 P.M. By 11, like other students at Yale and around the country, she was ready for a fairly substantial late-night meal -- something that Yale does not provide.
A survey by Marriott Management Services, which operates the cafeterias and restaurants at dozens of colleges, showed that half of 20,000 students polled ate regularly between 9 P.M. and midnight. The poll, which did not include Yale, also revealed that one-third said they preferred snacking and eating four to six small meals throughout the day, rather than two or three full meals.
"There's no lunch for me anymore," said another Yale undergraduate, Weatherly Ralph, who has classes from 11:30 A.M. to 2:15 P.M. and "grazes" throughout the day on fruit, bagels and frozen yogurt. When the dining halls were open and she had to rely on the meal plan, she was left in the frustrating predicament of having to "run into the dining hall, make a sandwich and shove it into my mouth" to get her money's worth, she said.
She said the strike had made it possible to eat when she wanted because Yale had provided students with a weekly strike stipend to cover meals off campus. While she said she sometimes liked meeting classmates at the dining hall, she wanted it on her own terms.
"I want Yale to be a service to me, and to take advantage of it when I want it," she said.
Yale has been slower than most colleges in making such a shift, in part, because it involves tampering with a dining system that is steeped in tradition. Although the dining halls moved to cafeteria-style service during World War II, the original intent of the residential college dining system remains a central part of the Yale experience.
Each of these intimate gated and enclosed dormitories, where about 300 students live and dine together, is intended to foster diverse social and intellectual communities to enhance a student's education.
In the last few years, however, a growing number of students have moved off campus, and one of their main gripes has been the dining hall system. The complaints are less about the food than the schedule and students' desires to be able to eat at odd hours with the option to "grab and go."
Yale has acknowledged the need to change. In the last two years, it has extended dining hall hours in some colleges. The university also offered students the option of a 14-meal plan instead of the once-mandatory 21-meal plan. While both programs cost $3,060 a year, the new plan gives students $200 credit at local restaurants.
Yale hopes to take further steps, though school officials and its food workers union are at odds over what pay scale should be applied to the new services and whether they should be union jobs.
Ultimately, the university believes that an updated food service will produce better quality for the same price.
"The whole idea of getting some flexibility here is to provide students with what they want," said Yale's president, Richard C. Levin.
While vowing to maintain the residential dining halls as centers of campus life, he cited a need for the school to provide late-night dining and more options, most notably a food court.
"We're hardly thinking of anything revolutionary," Dr. Levin said. "We're trying to do at Yale what most other schools have done years ago."
There certainly are plenty of examples to follow.
At Rider College in Lawrenceville, N.J., a campus serviced by Marriott, the dining hall recently started opening at 9:30 P.M. for a late meal. A new campus-run convenience store, which lets students charge purchases to their meal plans, does a steady business in hamburgers, pan pizzas and frozen dinners that can be popped into microwave ovens in students' rooms.
The State University College at Oswego, N.Y., recently opened a 350-seat food court that serves about 2,500 meals a day, with a Taco Bell, Sub City, an ice cream shop and hamburger grill. In the dining halls, it offers omelets and soups. It puts out cereal, fresh fruit and other snacks after 8:30 P.M.
"The days of the mandatory board plan are gone, gone, gone," said Craig Traub, Oswego's food services director. "We have to compete for our customer's dining dollar."
Columbia is also trying to catch up with the trend. A couple of years ago, it brought a Pizza Hut and Taco Bell on to campus.
This year, with the cooperation of its food workers' union, it has begun redesigning its meal plan, beginning with a focaccia grill and New York-style delicatessen in one dining hall as well as offering prepackaged salads, sandwiches and other prepared foods.
Over the summer, two dining halls at Columbia are scheduled for renovations, and in September, one will operate around the clock.
These changes are motivated, to some extent, by economics. While universities once considered their students a captive clientele, that is no longer true. At the same time, schools have recognized that improved food services can actually turn a profit and attract faculty and staff.
At some universities, students are provided with debit cards that they can use to charge meals at a variety of different venues, including food courts, convenience stores and dining halls.
Not only are schools offering the fast-food option, dining halls also are adding grills, elaborate salad bars and woks where student can watch the cooking of their stir-fry vegetables. There are increasing numbers of ethnic and vegetarian items.
"A decade ago, students didn't have many choices so they were forced to dine with us," said Dean A. Wright, president of the National Association of College and University Food Services, a trade group based in East Lansing, Mich. "Now, we find they have transportation and can leave campus. They come with a brand name recognition that didn't exist a generation ago. They come with a preconceived idea of what is acceptable and what is good. If we don't meet that, they'll walk."
Mr. Wright, the food services director at Utah State University, oversees an operation that serves 6,000 meals a day, makes fresh pasta and fresh bread every morning and can turn out a tenderloin steak with portobello mushrooms for visiting dignitaries.
An 850-seat food court that opened in March 1995 is expected to have $1.5 million in sales this year.
Things have become more sophisticated, Mr. Wright added, with college chefs usually graduates of culinary schools. The old days of "mystery meat" and casseroles are gone.
"Breaded veal and shepherd's pie -- they were good products, once the classics of college cuisine -- are virtually extinct in campus dining centers," Mr. Wright said. "I haven't seen a breaded veal meal in five years."

HEADLINE: Much Bluster Over 90 Cents

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
As a political matter, the issue of raising the minimum wage is relatively straightforward, like mathematics. As an economics question, it is much more subjective, like, say, religion.
Take the politics first. 
 The issue has proved to be a bonanza for President Clinton and Democrats in Congress. For the first time since Republicans won control of Congress, a significant number of backbenchers have defected from the position of their leaders.
The only reason the Democrats have not yet succeeded in passing a bill that would raise the minimum wage, now $4.25 an hour, by 90 cents or even $1 is that the Republican leaders have gone to great lengths to prevent a vote.
The Democrats seem to have held the issue in reserve until the election year.
In the 1992 election campaign, Mr. Clinton advocated raising the minimum wage, but the issue was never a central part of his platform. In his first two years in office, when Democrats controlled Congress, he did not mention the issue once in a speech or other public forum. (His staff says the President was too busy dealing with health care, as if Bill Clinton can handle only one issue at a time.)
Then, this year, Democrats began trying to attach an increase in the minimum wage to various pieces of Republican legislation. Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the majority leader and Republican Presidential nominee-in-waiting, was blindsided. He treated the issue as a totem of organized labor, sort of like repeal of right-to-work laws, that most Americans cared little about. In fact, polls show that Americans, by ratios of 4 or 5 to 1, favor raising the minimum wage.
As Senator Dole and his colleagues stumble, Democrats portray the question as one of right and wrong. "It is a moral issue," Representative John Lewis of Georgia said last week. "Raising the minimum wage is the right thing to do."
With stakes that high, Republicans will probably fold their cards rather than continue to fritter away political chips. The prevailing view in the Capitol and among lobbyists downtown is that Republican leaders cannot afford to block a vote much longer and that sooner rather than later Congress will vote to increase the hourly minimum.
Now consider the economics of the matter. On the minimum wage, the advocates on both sides hold views so unshakeable that they resemble articles of faith.
The arguments have hardly changed at all since the Federal minimum wage was first introduced, at 25 cents an hour, half a century ago.
Many politicians and scholars, mostly Republicans and conservative economists, maintain that raising the minimum wage would distort the marketplace and cause a significant loss of jobs. Some of those jobs, they say, would be taken from poor people, the very people the increase was meant to help.
Much of the benefit of an increase, the opponents say, would go to teen-agers and footloose young adults who do not really need the money. Some businesses, especially smaller ones, would suffer and perhaps even go broke, the opponents continue, and inflation would result when businesses raised their prices to offset the higher wages.
Other politicians and scholars, mostly Democrats and liberal economists, argue that people who go to work every day are entitled to a living wage.
The advocates of raising the wage say that the value of the minimum wage has been eroded by inflation, that market forces have failed workers at the bottom of the income scale and that the job losses resulting from a moderate increase would at most be trivial. A fair minimum wage, they say, prevents sweatshops and encourages poor people to leave the welfare rolls and go to work.
This is one of those issues (lowering the capital gains tax is another) where economists know what they believe and often use research and statistics not so much to answer questions as to document what they are sure they already know. They view the same data through completely different lenses.
One side, for instance, argues that 37 percent of those earning the minimum wage are teen-agers. The other side says 63 percent are adults. One side asserts that 62 percent live in homes with someone else who works. The other side, fist banging on the table, declares that 38 percent must live off the minimum wage alone.
Statistics on the number of jobs created and lost are favorites on both sides. Those opposed to raising the minimum wage point out that in the first two years after the wage was last raised, in 1989, the number of jobs in the United States declined by 495,000. Those who advocate an increase observe that 10 million jobs have been created in the economy since 1989.
Those figures are cited even though no responsible economist would say that the increase in the minimum wage was the main reason the number of jobs declined in the recession of 1990-91, just as none would say that keeping the minimum wage steady for the last seven years had much to do with the growth in jobs.
Last year, two Princeton economists, David Card, who won the 1995 John Bates Clark medal for the most outstanding economist under age 40, and Alan B. Krueger, now chief economist at the Labor Department, published a book based on research showing that an increase in the minimum wage in New Jersey in 1992 did not cause jobs to be lost among fast-food workers. As soon as their findings hit the academic journals, economists opposed to raising the minimum wage submitted articles that sought to discredit the Card-Krueger research.
Views on the topic are held so firmly that research is hardly likely to change any economist's mind.
Asked in an interview whether any argument could be made that would change his opposition to raising the minimum wage, Martin A. Regalia, chief economist at the United States Chamber of Commerce, replied: "No, as an economist, not really. The literature on this goes back 50 or 60 years. It is extremely hard to find any studies that don't find this disemployment effect."
The same question was put to a strong advocate of a minimum-wage increase, Jared Bernstein, a labor economist at the Economic Policy Institute, a liberal research center partly financed by unions. "I don't think there's a theoretical argument that would change my mind," he responded. "If someone could show me you were hurting the people you were trying to help, that would change my mind, but I don't think anyone can do that."
The truth of the matter is that raising the minimum wage from $4.25 an hour to $5.15 would probably marginally improve the living conditions of a relatively small number of workers. It would also reduce the profits somewhat of some businesses, mostly in the food industry, and cause a fairly small number of jobs to be lost.
It would, in short, be neither a boon nor a disaster. But you would never know that from the vigorous way the politicians and economists state their cases.

CORRECTION-DATE: May 1, 1996, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
A news analysis article yesterday about the minimum wage cited an outdated affiliation for Alan B. Krueger, an economist who was co-author of a book on increases in the minimum wage. Mr. Krueger returned last August to the faculty at Princeton University; he is no longer chief economist at the Labor Department.
